Rational Judgments
beginner
4 min
The Three Categories of Rational Judgment
Sanusi enumerates the three rational judgments and defines each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational judgments (obligatory, impossible, permissible) and how they form the foundation for theological reasoning.

Core Idea
Rational judgment has only three categories: necessary, impossible, and possible.

Sanusi begins by establishing the fundamental categories of rational judgment. Before we can discuss attributes of Allah or the world, we need a shared vocabulary for what the intellect can conceive. This passage defines the three and only three rational judgments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through. Then read the literal translation. Notice the concise style and the opening praise.
2
Identify the three categories
Find where Sanusi lists the three divisions: الوجوب, الاستحالة, and الجواز. Then look at each definition.
Each definition uses the phrase 'ما لا يتصور في العقل' or 'ما يصح في العقل'.
3
Understand 'inconceivable'
The definitions rely on what the intellect can or cannot conceive. Ask yourself: what does it mean for something to be inconceivable?
In kalam, this is about rational necessity, not empirical impossibility.
4
Reflect on the completeness
Why only three? Consider that any proposition about existence is either necessarily true, necessarily false, or neither. These three cover all possibilities.
This is the foundation for all subsequent arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحمد لله، والصلاة والسلام على رسول الله. أقسام الحكم العقلي: اعلم: أن الحكم العقلي ينحصر في ثلاثة أقسام: الوجوب، والاستحالة، والجواز. فالواجب: ما لا يتصور في العقل عدمه. والمستحيل: ما لا يتصور في العقل وجوده. والجائز: ما يصح في العقل وجوده وعدمه.
Praise be to Allah, and blessings and peace be upon the Messenger of Allah. The divisions of rational judgment: Know that rational judgment is confined to three divisions: obligation, impossibility, and permissibility. The obligatory: that which is inconceivable by the intellect its non-existence. The impossible: that which is inconceivable by the intellect its existence. The permissible: that which is valid by the intellect its existence and its non-existence.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition. It begins with the basmala and salutation, then introduces the topic of rational judgments. Sanusi states that rational judgment is limited to three categories: al-wujub (obligation/existence must be affirmed), al-istihalah (impossibility/existence cannot be affirmed), and al-jawaz (permissibility/possibility). He then defines each: The wajib (obligatory) is what the intellect cannot conceive of as not existing; its non-existence is rationally impossible. The mustahil (impossible) is what the intellect cannot conceive of as existing; its existence is rationally impossible. The ja'iz (possible) is what the intellect can conceive as either existing or not existing; both are rationally sound. These three categories form the foundation for all theological reasoning about God and creation.

Key Terms
الحكم العقلي
الوجوب
الاستحالة
الجواز
الواجب
المستحيل
الجائز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'ja'iz' means something is actually undecided or that it could be anything.
Correction: Rather, 'ja'iz' means both existence and non-existence are rationally conceivable; it does not imply arbitrary or unknowable.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, how many divisions of rational judgment are there?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The three rational judgments—obligatory, impossible, and permissible—are the fundamental categories for all logical reasoning about God and creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if God's existence is rationally necessary (wajib) and your own existence is merely possible (ja'iz)? How would your daily choices change if you internalized that you have no necessary existence and that you could be annihilated at any moment?
?
What are some beliefs you hold about God or His creation that you have never examined through the lens of rational judgment? Are any of them actually impossible (mustahil) or merely permissible (ja'iz) yet you treat them as necessary?
?
When you say 'I believe in God', is your conviction based on rational certainty that His non-existence is inconceivable, or is it a comfortable habit? What would it cost you to subject your faith to the test of these three categories—and are you willing to do so today?

Rational Judgments
beginner
4 min
The Three Rational Judgments and Their Definitions
Sanusi defines the three rational judgments and states the legal duty to know them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational judgments (necessary, impossible, possible) and why every legally responsible person must know what applies to Allah and the prophets.

Core Idea
Kalām begins with three rational categories: necessary, impossible, and possible.

Before studying any theological attribute or proof, a student of kalām must master the basic vocabulary of rational judgment. Sanusi opens with the threefold division of what the mind can and cannot conceive, and then immediately declares that every Muslim is obligated to learn these categories as they apply to Allah and His messengers. This passage lays the conceptual foundation for all subsequent discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: first a classification, then three definitions, then a legal ruling.
Pay attention to the shift from logical definitions to Islamic obligations.
2
Identify the three categories and their definitions
Find where Sanusi defines the necessary, impossible, and possible. Note the logical criterion for each: the necessary is that whose non-existence is inconceivable; the impossible is that whose existence is inconceivable; the possible is that whose existence and non-existence are both conceivable.
These are logical possibilities, not physical or empirical possibilities.
3
Understand the legal obligation
The passage states that it is legally incumbent (wājib shar‘an) upon every legally responsible person (mukallaf) to know which of these three categories apply to Allah and to the messengers. This makes the study of kalām a religious duty.
The obligation is to know the attributes themselves, not just the definitions.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself: Why are these three categories the first thing a student of kalām must learn? The commentary will help you see that they are the foundation for all subsequent arguments about divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعْلَمْ : أَنَّ الْحُكْمَ الْعَقْلِيَّ يَنْحَصِرُ فِي ثَلَاثَةِ أَقْسَامٍ : الْوُجُوبُ ، وَالِاسْتِحَالَةُ ، وَالْجَوَازُ . فَالْوَاجِبُ : مَا لا يُتَصَوَّرُ فِي الْعَقْلِ عَدَمُهُ . وَالْمُسْتَحِيلُ : مَا لا يُتَصَوَّرُ فِي الْعَقْلِ وُجُودُهُ . وَالْجَائِزُ : مَا يَصِحُ فِي الْعَقْلِ وُجُودُهُ وَعَدَمُهُ . وَيَجِبُ عَلَى كُلِّ مُكَلَّفٍ شَرْعاً : أَنْ يَعْرِفَ مَا يَجِبُ فِي حَقِّ مَوْلانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ ، وَمَا يَسْتَحِيلُ ، وَمَا يَجُوزُ ، وَكَذَلِكَ يَجِبُ عَلَيْهِ أَنْ يَعْرِفَ مِثْلَ ذَلِكَ فِي حَقِّ الرُّسُلِ عَلَيْهِمُ الصَّلاةُ وَالسَّلامُ .
Know that the rational judgment is confined to three divisions: necessity, impossibility, and possibility. The necessary is that whose non-existence is inconceivable in the mind. The impossible is that whose existence is inconceivable in the mind. The possible is that whose existence and non-existence are both sound in the mind. And it is legally incumbent upon every legally responsible person to know what is necessary in respect of our Master, Majestic and Mighty, what is impossible, and what is possible; and likewise it is incumbent upon him to know the like of that in respect of the Messengers, may peace and blessings be upon them.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a definition and a legal ruling. It first defines the threefold division of rational judgment: the necessary (wājib), the impossible (mustaḥīl), and the possible (jā’iz). For each, it gives a precise logical criterion: a necessary judgment is one whose opposite is inconceivable; an impossible judgment is one whose existence is inconceivable; a possible judgment is one for which both existence and non-existence are conceivable. After defining these, the passage states the legal (shar‘ī) obligation of every legally responsible person (mukallaf) to know which of these three categories apply to God and which apply to the prophets. The focus is on knowing what is necessary, impossible, and possible concerning God and His messengers. This establishes the foundation for the entire structure of kalām: one must first master these basic judgments before proceeding to specific attributes or proofs.

Key Terms
الحكم العقلي
الواجب
المستحيل
الجائز
المكلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the rational judgments are about physical possibility or probability, not logical conceivability.
Correction: The passage defines the possible as that whose existence and non-existence are both conceivable in the mind—a logical category, not an empirical one.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the definition of the 'necessary' (wājib) in rational judgment?
A) That whose existence is necessary in the external world.
B) That whose non-existence is inconceivable in the mind.
C) That whose existence is conceivable but its non-existence is not.
D) That which is always actual.

Key Takeaway
The three rational judgments—necessary, impossible, possible—provide the logical framework for understanding what can and cannot be said about Allah and His messengers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you personally fulfilled the legal duty mentioned in this passage? Do you know what is necessary, impossible, and possible regarding Allah, or have you neglected this obligation? What will you do today to ensure you are not among those who are ignorant of this foundational knowledge?
?
When you think about Allah, do you ever imagine something that would be impossible for Him, like thinking He needs help or has a partner? How does the category of 'impossible' challenge your current beliefs and force you to purify your understanding of Allah?
?
The category of 'possible' reminds you that many outcomes in your life are not necessary—they depend on Allah's will. Are you living with that reality, surrendering to the possibilities He chooses, or do you cling to your own expectations as if they were necessary? What is one area where you need to submit to the possible will of Allah today?

Proof of the Necessity of Divine Oneness
Sanusi gives a rational proof that God must be one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for God's oneness based on the impossibility of multiple gods.

Core Idea
If God were not one, nothing could exist.

Sanusi presents a concise rational argument for the necessity of God's oneness. The proof is based on the logical consequence of multiple gods: they would be incapable of creating the world. This short passage contains a powerful logical demonstration that the existence of the world itself requires a single Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the meaning.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if...then...'
2
Identify the logical structure
Identify the condition (God not one) and the consequence (nothing exists). Note the reason given: 'because of the necessity of His incapacity'.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why multiple gods would lead to incapacity. How does this prove that God must be one?
This connects to the broader theme of tawheed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُوبِ الْوَحْدَانِيَّةِ لَهُ جَلَّ وَعَزَّ : فَلَأَنَّهُ لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ وَاحِداً لَزِمَ أَلا يُوجَدَ شَيْءٌ مِنَ الْعَالَمِ ; لِلْزُومِ عَجْزِهِ
حينيد
As for the proof of the necessity of oneness for Him, Exalted and Mighty: it is that if He were not one, it would necessitate that nothing from the world exists, because of the necessity of His incapacity [?]
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage provides a rational proof for God's oneness. It states that if God were not one, then nothing in the world would exist, because of the necessity of incapacity. The argument is concise: multiple gods would lead to conflict and impotence, preventing any creation. The word 'عَجْزِهِ' is unclear but likely refers to the inability that would arise from multiple gods.

Key Terms
برهان
وحدانية
عجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Multiple gods could cooperate to create the world.
Correction: The proof shows that multiple gods would lead to mutual incapacity and thus prevent any creation.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what would happen if God were not one?
A) Nothing would exist from the world
B) The world would be perfect
C) God would be all-powerful
D) Nothing would change

Key Takeaway
The proof for God's oneness shows that multiple gods would be incapable of creating anything, so the existence of the world itself proves His oneness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your very existence depends on God being one. If you ever feel that something other than God can give you what you need, you are denying that oneness in action. Where in your heart have you set up a false partner that you rely on? What will you do today to tear it down?
?
The proof says multiple gods would be incapable, so creation exists only because God is alone without rivals. Look at the world around you—your family, your provision, your next breath. Do you see them as proof of oneness? Or do you still worry about who else might control your destiny? When you lie down tonight, thank God that He alone is the One who sustains you.
?
Sanusi argues that even one moment of existence proves God's oneness. Yet you live every day, year after year, and you may rarely pause to reflect on that. What would change in your daily prayer, your trust, your fear, if you truly lived with the awareness that without God's oneness, you would not be here? Write down one specific way you will remind yourself of this truth every morning.

Rational Judgments

The Shahada Contains Three Rational Judgments
Sanusi shows the shahada contains three rational judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the first shahada, 'La ilaha illa Allah', inherently contains the three rational judgments: the necessary, the impossible, and the permissible concerning Allah.

Core Idea
The shahada is a concise summary of all divine attributes.

Sanusi now reveals a profound insight: the simple declaration of faith, 'There is no god but Allah', is not just a negation of false gods but a container for the entire framework of theological knowledge. Every accountable person must know three categories of judgments about Allah, and this single statement encompasses them all. This lesson unpacks that connection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get its rhythm. Then read the English translation. Notice how Sanusi presents his conclusion with 'Thus it has become clear...'.
The Arabic uses a very concise structure.
2
Identify the three divisions
The passage lists three Arabic terms: ما يجب (what is obligatory), ما يستحيل (what is impossible), and ما يجوز (what is permissible). Highlight them in the text.
These are the three rational judgments in kalam.
3
Connect the shahada to the divisions
Ask yourself: how does 'La ilaha illa Allah' imply these three? The commentary will explain that affirming Allah as the only true god necessitates that certain attributes are necessary for Him, others impossible, and some merely possible.
This is the heart of Sanusi's argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَقَدْ بَانَ لَكَ بِهَذَا : تَضَمُّنُ قَوْلِ ( لا إِلَهَ إِلا اللَّهُ ) لِلأَقْسَامِ الثَّلاثَةِ الَّتِي يَجِبُ عَلَى الْمُكَلِّفِ مَعْرِفَتُهَا فِي حَقٌّ مَوْلانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ ؛ وَهِيَ : مَا يَجِبُ فِي حَقِّهِ تَعَالَى ، وَمَا يَسْتَحِيلُ ، وَمَا يَجُوزُ .
Thus it has become clear to you by this: that the statement 'There is no god but Allah' comprises the three divisions which the morally responsible person must know concerning the right of our Lord, may He be glorified and exalted: and they are: what is obligatory concerning Him, may He be exalted, what is impossible, and what is permissible.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification. Sanusi explains that the first part of the shahada, 'La ilaha illa Allah', encapsulates the three categories of judgments that every accountable person (mukallaf) must know regarding Allah. These three categories are the wajib (obligatory/necessary), the mustahil (impossible), and the ja'iz (permissible). This tripartite division is fundamental to kalam: all divine attributes are either necessary, impossible, or possible for Allah. Sanusi previously established this framework, and now he shows that the simple statement of tawhid contains them all.

Key Terms
الأقسام الثلاثة
ما يجب
ما يستحيل
ما يجوز
المكلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe the shahada only denies false gods and has no detailed theological content.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the shahada actually implies the entire framework of necessary, impossible, and permissible attributes, making it a comprehensive theological statement.

Checkpoint
How many categories of judgments regarding Allah does Sanusi say are contained in the first shahada?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The first shahada contains the three rational judgments: what is necessary, impossible, and possible for Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'La ilaha illa Allah,' do you consciously affirm that Allah possesses necessary attributes, is free from impossibilities, and has permissible actions? If not, how will you start incorporating this understanding into your daily recitation?
?
The passage says every morally responsible person must know these three divisions. Can you name at least one attribute from each category right now? If you cannot, what does that reveal about your commitment to knowing your Lord?
?
You have just learned that the shahada itself demands precise theological knowledge. Will you allow this lesson to remain academic, or will you let it transform the way you utter the most important statement of faith? What specific step will you take today to deepen your knowledge of what is obligatory, impossible, and permissible for Allah?

Rational Judgments
beginner
5 min
The Three Rational Judgments: Obligation, Impossibility, Permissibility
Sanusi defines the three rational judgments and explains the division of judgment into types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of rational judgment and how they form the foundation for evaluating theological claims.

Core Idea
The three rational judgments are the basic grammar of all kalam reasoning.

This passage lays the conceptual groundwork for all theological argument in the Ash'ari tradition. Before we can assess any claim about God or the world, we need a shared vocabulary for what is rationally necessary, impossible, or possible. Sanusi begins by defining these three categories and then explains how judgment itself is classified by its source.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very clear and direct style.
2
Identify the three rational judgments
The passage presents three categories: obligation (wujub), impossibility (istihala), and permission (jawaz). Find how each is defined in terms of conceivability in the mind.
These correspond to the necessary (wajib), impossible (mustahil), and possible (ja'iz).
3
Understand the definition of judgment
Sanusi defines judgment as affirmation or negation of a matter. Then he states that the source of judgment can be Law, custom, or intellect. See how this leads to three types of judgment.
4
Reflect on the importance
Consider why this classification is the starting point for kalam. Every theological proposition falls into one of these rational categories. How does this help in argumentation?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعْلَمْ : أَنَّ الْحُكْمَ الْعَقْلِيَّ يَنْحَصِرُ فِي ثَلَاثَةِ أَقْسَامٍ : الْوُجُوبُ ، وَالاسْتِحَالَةُ ، وَالْجَوَازُ . فَالْوَاجِبُ : مَا لَا يُتَصَوَّرُ فِي الْعَقْلِ عَدَمُهُ . وَالْمُسْتَحِيلُ : مَا لَا يُتَصَوَّرُ فِي الْعَقْلِ وُجُودُهُ . وَالْجَائِزُ : مَا يَصِحُ فِي الْعَقْلِ وُجُودُهُ وَعَدَمُهُ . الْحُكْمُ : هُوَ إِثْبَاتُ أَمْرٍ أَوْ نَفْيُهُ . وَالْحَاكِمُ بِذَلِكَ : إِمَّا الشَّرْعُ ، أَوِ الْعَادَةُ ، أَوِ الْعَقْلُ . فَلِهَذَا انْقَسَمَ الْحُكْمُ إِلَى ثَلَاثَةِ أَقْسَامٍ : شَرْعِيٌّ ، وَعَادِيٌّ ، وَعَقْلِيٌّ .
Know: that the rational judgment is confined to three divisions: obligation, impossibility, and permission. So the necessary is that whose non-existence is not conceivable in the mind. The impossible is that whose existence is not conceivable in the mind. The possible is that whose existence and non-existence are sound in the mind. Judgment: is the affirmation or negation of a matter. And the judge in that is either the Law, or custom, or the intellect. Therefore judgment is divided into three divisions: legal, customary, and rational.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage introduces the fundamental classification of rational judgments in kalam. It begins by stating that rational judgment (al-ḥukm al-ʿaqlī) is confined to three categories: obligation (wujūb), impossibility (istiḥāla), and permission (jawāz). Each category is then defined: the necessary (al-wājib) is that whose non-existence cannot be conceived by the intellect; the impossible (al-mustaḥīl) is that whose existence cannot be conceived; and the possible (al-jāʾiz) is that whose existence and non-existence are both intellectually sound. Next, it defines judgment (al-ḥukm) in general as the affirmation or negation of something, and states that the judge (al-ḥākim) determining that judgment is either the Law (al-sharʿ), custom (al-ʿāda), or the intellect (al-ʿaql). Consequently, judgment is divided into three types: legal (sharʿī), customary (ʿādī), and rational (ʿaqlī). This passage establishes the basic logical framework that underlies all subsequent kalam reasoning about God's existence, attributes, and actions.

Key Terms
الحكم العقلي
الوجوب
الاستحالة
الجواز
الواجب
المستحيل
الجائز
الحكم
الشرعي
العادي
العقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that rational judgment only includes necessary and impossible, excluding the possible.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly includes the possible (ja'iz) as the third essential category, stating that its existence and non-existence are both sound in the mind.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what are the three divisions of rational judgment?
A) Wajib, Mustahil, Ja'iz
B) Wajib, Mandub, Haram
C) Wajib, Mustahil, Mubah
D) Wajib, Mustahil, Haram

Key Takeaway
Rational judgments are the foundation of all theological reasoning, and they are three: necessary, impossible, and possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If only God is truly necessary, then every created thing in your life—your health, your family, your job—is merely possible and could cease to exist at any moment. Are you truly attached to them as gifts from the Necessary Being, or do you act as if they must be there? What would change today if you lived in constant awareness that none of these are necessary?
?
The source teaches that judgment can be based on custom ('ādī) as well as intellect. Consider a belief you hold without question—perhaps about success, relationships, or worship. Is that belief actually rational, or have you inherited it from custom? Are you willing to test it against the standard of rational judgment as defined by Imam Sanusi?
?
Imam Sanusi defines the possible as that which can both exist and not exist. Your very existence is possible, not necessary. Do you live with the humility of one who could have been non-existent, or do you walk with arrogance as if you are indispensable? What act of gratitude or reliance on God would flow from truly accepting your contingency?

The Three Rational Judgments
Sanusi enumerates the three rational judgments and defines each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational judgments (obligatory, impossible, possible) and their definitions based on rational conceivability.

Core Idea
Rational judgment sorts propositions into necessary, impossible, and possible.

Sanusi begins by stating that rational judgment is confined to three categories. These categories are the fundamental building blocks of all theological reasoning. Before we can argue about what God must be or cannot be, we need a clear vocabulary for what the mind can and cannot conceive.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the three terms: الواجب, المستحيل, الجائز.
Remember that these are technical terms with specific rational definitions.
2
Understand each definition
For each of the three, note how Sanusi defines it in terms of the mind's ability to conceive existence or non-existence. The obligatory is that whose non-existence is inconceivable; the impossible is that whose existence is inconceivable; the possible is that whose existence and non-existence are both conceivable.
3
Connect to later usage
Ask yourself: how would these categories apply to statements about Allah? For example, existence is obligatory for Allah, non-existence is impossible, and creating the world is possible. This is the basis of kalam arguments.
This prepares you for the next lessons.

اقسام الحكم أَعْلَمْ : أَنَّ الْحُكْمَ الْعَقْلِيَّ يَنْحَصِرُ فِي ثَلَاثَةِ أَقْسَامٍ الْوُجُوبُ ، وَالاسْتِحَالَهُ ، وَالْجَوَازُ . فَالْوَاجِبُ : مَا لَا يُتَصَوَّرُ فِي الْعَقْلِ عَدَمُهُ وَالْمُسْتَحِيلُ : مَا لا يُتَصَوَّرُ فِي الْعَقْلِ وُجُودُهُ . وَالْجَائِزُ : مَا يَصِحُ فِي الْعَقْلِ وُجُودُهُ وَعَدَمُهُ الْحكم : هو إثبات أمرٍ أو نفيه والحاكم بذلك : إما الشرع ، أو العادة ، أو العقل فلهذا انقسم الحكمُ إلى ثلاثة أقسام : شرعي ، وعادي ، وعقلي
Divisions of judgment. Know: that rational judgment is restricted to three divisions: obligation, impossibility, and possibility. The obligatory: that whose non-existence is inconceivable in the mind. The impossible: that whose existence is inconceivable in the mind. The possible: that whose existence and non-existence are valid in the mind. Judgment: is affirming or negating something. And the judge in that is either the Law, or custom, or reason. Therefore judgment is divided into three divisions: legal, customary, and rational.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies judgment. It begins by stating that rational judgment is confined to three categories: obligatory (wujub), impossible (istihala), and possible (jawaz). Each is defined: the obligatory is that whose non-existence cannot be conceived by the mind; the impossible is that whose existence cannot be conceived; the possible is that whose existence and non-existence are both conceivable. Then it gives a general definition of judgment: it is the affirmation or negation of something. The agent of judgment (al-hakim) is either the divine Law (shar'), custom ('adah), or reason ('aql). Hence judgment is divided into three corresponding types: legal, customary, and rational. The focus then returns to rational judgment, whose three subdivisions were already given. This classification is foundational for kalam because theologians employ rational arguments to establish or deny propositions.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'impossible' refers to physical impossibility (e.g., a rock floating) rather than logical impossibility (e.g., a square circle).
Correction: Sanusi makes clear that impossibility is defined by rational conceivability, not physical or customary possibility.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what defines the possible (al-ja'iz)?
A) That whose non-existence is inconceivable.
B) That whose existence is inconceivable.
C) That whose existence and non-existence are both valid in the mind.
D) That which is permitted by the Law.

Key Takeaway
Rational judgment is limited to three categories: obligatory (rationally necessary), impossible (rationally impossible), and possible (rationally contingent).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know rationally that God's existence is necessary—inconceivable that He not exist. Yet do you ever live a single day as if His existence were optional? Pause now and ask yourself: when did you last act as if God might not be watching? What would it mean to truly let the rational judgment of obligation govern your heart?
?
Your future, your health, your next breath—all possible in the mind's eye, neither necessary nor impossible. How much of your anxiety comes from treating the possible as if it were necessary? Will you release your grip on the contingent and trust the Necessary tonight?
?
The mind cannot conceive of a partner with God. Yet do you ever, even subtly, assign independent power to creation—wealth, status, people—as if they could exist or act without Him? That too is a form of conceiving the impossible. What would it look like to uproot that from your heart today?

The Division of Judgment by Source
Sanusi defines judgment and divides it by the source of judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how judgment is divided into legal, customary, and rational based on the source of judgment (al-hakim).

Core Idea
Every judgment comes from either law, custom, or reason.

After defining the three rational judgments, Sanusi steps back to give a general definition of judgment and classify it according to the agent that issues it. This broader classification shows that rational judgment is only one type, alongside legal and customary judgments. Understanding this helps prevent confusion between different domains of judgment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on judgment and its division
Read from 'الحكم : هو إثبات أمرٍ أو نفيه' to the end. Notice the definition and then the three sources: الشرع, العادة, العقل.
This passage immediately follows the previous one.
2
Understand the definition of judgment
Sanusi defines judgment (al-hukm) as the affirmation or negation of something. This is a logical definition applicable to all domains.
3
Identify the three judges and their corresponding judgments
The judge (al-hakim) is either the Law (shar'), custom ('adah), or reason ('aql). Hence judgment is divided into legal (shar'i), customary ('adi), and rational ('aqli).
Rational judgment is the one whose subdivisions were given earlier.

The Customary Judgment: Correlation Not Causation
Sanusi defines the customary judgment and refutes its misinterpretation as rational causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the customary judgment only indicates repeated concurrence, not causation, and that true agency belongs to Allah alone.

Core Idea
Customary judgments are about correlation, not causation; Allah is the sole agent.

In this passage, Sanusi clarifies a crucial principle of Islamic theology: when we say 'fire burns,' we are not making a rational claim about fire's power, but merely observing a regular pattern. This lesson will help you distinguish between the customary judgment and rational causation, and see why attributing effects to creatures is a theological error.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation together, noting the definition and example. Pay attention to the precise wording Sanusi uses.
The term 'customary judgment' is central.
2
Understand the definition
Sanusi defines the customary judgment as affirmation of a connection between two things based on repeated sense perception. This means custom indicates mere concurrence, not a necessary causal link.
Contrast with a rational judgment which claims necessity.
3
Examine the example of fire
The judgment 'fire burns' is customary: we observe fire and burning together repeatedly. Sanusi stresses this does not mean fire is the true agent of burning.
The true agent is determined by reason and revelation.
4
Identify the refutation
Sanusi refutes those who treat customary judgments as rational, attributing effects to the natures or powers of things. This leads to blameworthy innovation.
This part guards against a common theological error.
5
Connect to tawhid
Reason and revelation agree that Allah alone creates all effects. The customary judgment is compatible with divine omnipotence if understood correctly.
This maintains God's exclusive agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام على الحكم العادي
وأما الحكم العادي : فحقيقتُهُ : إثباتُ الربط بين أمرٍ وأمرٍ وجوداً أو عدماً بواسطة تكرر القِرانِ بينهما على الحس (۱)
مثال ذلكَ : الحكم على النار بأنَّها محرقة ، فهذا حكم عادي ؛ إذْ معناه : أنَّ الإحراق يقترن بمس النار في كثير من الأجسام ؛ لمشاهدة تكرر ذلك على الحس ، وليس معنى أنَّ النار هي التي أَثَرَتْ في إحراق ما مسَّتْهُ مثلاً أو في تسخينه ؛ إذْ هذا المعنى لا دلالة للعادة عليه أصلاً ، وإِنَّما غاية ما دلَّتْ عليه العادة الاقترانُ فقط بينَ الأمرين ، أمَّا تعيين فاعل ذلكَ فليسَ للعادة فيه مدخل ، ولا منها يُتلقَّى علم ذلكَ ، وقِسٌ على هذا سائر الأحكام العاديَّةِ ؛ ككون الطعام مشبعاً ، والماء مروياً ، والشمس مضيئة ، والسكين قاطعةً ، ونحو ذلك ممَّا لا ينحصر ، وإِنَّما يُتلقَّى العلم بفاعل هذه الآثارِ المقارنة لهذه الأشياء مِنْ دليلي العقل والنقل ، وقد أطبق العقلُ والشرع على انفراد المولى جل وعزَّ باختراع جميعِ الكائنات عموماً (۱) ، وأنَّهُ لا أثر لكلِّ ما سواه تعالى في شيء من الآثار جملة وتفصيلاً
وقد غلط قوم في تلك الأحكام العادية ؛ فجعلوها عقليَّةً وأسندوا وجودَ كلِّ أثر منها لما جرَتِ العادةُ أَنَّهُ يوجد معَهُ ؛ إِمَّا بطبعه ، أو بقوَّةٍ أُودعَتْ فيهِ (۲) ، فأصبحوا في هوس ذميم ، وبدعة
Discourse on the customary judgment (al-hukm al-'adi)
As for the customary judgment: its reality is the affirmation of a connection between one matter and another, in existence or non-existence, by means of the repeated concurrence between them in sense perception (1). An example of that is the judgment that fire is burning: this is a customary judgment, because its meaning is that burning is concomitant with contact with fire in many bodies, due to observing this repeated occurrence in sense perception. It does not mean that fire is the one that produced an effect in burning what it touched, for instance, or in heating it; for this meaning has no indication from custom at all. Rather, the utmost that custom indicates is only the concurrence between the two matters. As for specifying the agent of that, custom has no role in it, nor is the knowledge of that derived from it. And analogize to this all other customary judgments, such as food being satiating, water being thirst-quenching, the sun being illuminating, the knife being cutting, and similar things that are innumerable. Rather, knowledge of the agent of these effects concomitant with these things is only derived from the two proofs of reason and revelation. And reason and revelation have agreed upon the exclusivity of the Lord, glorified and exalted, in inventing all beings in general (1), and that there is no effect for anything other than Him, exalted, in any of the effects, in totality and in detail. And some people have erred in these customary judgments: they made them intellectual (rational) and attributed the existence of each effect to that which customarily co-exists with it, either by its nature or by a power deposited in it (2); so they fell into blameworthy delusion and innovation.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage defines the customary judgment (al-hukm al-'adi) and distinguishes it from rational causation. Sanusi begins by stating its reality: it is the affirmation of a connection (al-rabt) between two things based on repeated sense perception. The example is fire and burning: we judge that fire burns because we repeatedly observe burning when touching fire. However, this judgment does not imply that fire is the actual agent of burning; custom only indicates concurrence (al-iqtiran), not causation. Specifying the true agent is outside custom's scope and must come from reason and revelation. Reason and revelation both confirm that Allah alone creates all beings and effects, and nothing else has any independent effect. Sanusi then refutes those who treat customary judgments as rational (intellectual) judgments, i.e., they ascribe effects to the natures or powers of things. Such people are in error and innovation. The passage upholds the Ash'ari position on occasionalism: observed regularities are just God's custom, not necessary causation.

Key Terms
الحكم العادي
الحس
العادة
الاقتران
الفاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the customary judgment 'fire burns' means fire is the cause of burning, implying a necessary causal link.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that custom only indicates repeated concurrence, not a necessary causal relation. The actual cause is always Allah alone.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what does the customary judgment 'fire burns' actually indicate?
A) Fire is the true cause of burning.
B) There is a repeated concurrence between fire and burning in sense perception.
C) Fire has a nature that necessitates burning.
D) Fire is the agent of burning by divine delegation.

Key Takeaway
The customary judgment only indicates a pattern of concurrence, not a causal relationship; all true effects come from Allah alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a natural event like rain causing growth, do your heart and tongue immediately attribute the effect to the rain or to Allah? What does this reveal about your tawhid in actions?
?
Consider a time you relied on a 'natural cause' (e.g., medicine healing, fire warming) without consciously remembering that Allah is the sole agent. How can you train yourself to break this habit and see the customary veil?
?
The passage warns against attributing effects to natures or powers deposited in things. Are there areas in your life where you functionally believe in 'natural laws' independent of Allah's direct creation? What would change if you internalized that every effect is directly from Him?

Rational Judgments

Definition of Rational Judgment
Sanusi defines rational judgment and states its criteria.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what a rational judgment is and how it differs from customary and revealed judgments.

Core Idea
Rational judgments are known by reason alone, without repetition or convention.

Sanusi begins by defining the foundational category of rational judgment (al-ḥukm al-ʿaqlī). This is the type of judgment that the intellect makes independently, and it is the basis for all subsequent kalam arguments. The definition is compact but precise, establishing two key contrasts: rational judgments do not depend on repeated experience (takarru), nor on any external stipulation (waḍʿ wāḍiʿ). Understanding this distinction is essential before moving to the other types of judgment.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the key terms: ḥukm ʿaqlī, takarru, waḍʿ wāḍiʿ.
2
Understand 'by itself'
The phrase 'bi-mujarradihi' means the intellect needs no external help. Ask: what kinds of things can the intellect know without experience or revelation?
3
Contrast with other judgments
Rational judgment is contrasted with two other types: customary (dependent on repetition) and revealed (dependent on divine stipulation). Identify what each depends on.
This distinction is key to kalam methodology.
4
Test your understanding
Use the checkpoint question to confirm you can identify what is NOT a characteristic of rational judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ الكلام على الحكم العقلي ] وأما الحكم العقلى : فهو عبارةٌ عمَّا يُدرك العقل بمجرَّدِهِ ثبوته أو نفيَهُ مِنْ غيرِ توقُّف على تكرُّرٍ ولا وضعِ واضع
[Discussion on the rational judgment] As for the rational judgment: it is an expression for that which the intellect, by itself, perceives its affirmation or negation, without depending on repetition or the stipulation of a stipulator.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage defines rational judgment (al-ḥukm al-ʿaqlī). The author states that it is a judgment made by the intellect alone (bi-mujarradihi), i.e., without external aid. It does not depend on repeated observation (takarru) — which is the basis of customary judgments — nor on the stipulation of a stipulator (waḍʿ wāḍiʿ) — i.e., divine command or convention, which underlies revealed judgments. Thus, rational judgments are necessary or impossible propositions that reason affirms directly, such as 'the whole is greater than its part' or 'a thing cannot both exist and not exist at the same time.'

Key Terms
حكم عقلي
تكرر
وضع واضع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rational judgments depend on repeated observation like scientific laws.
Correction: The passage explicitly says rational judgments do NOT depend on repetition (takarru), thus they are known by reason alone, not by observation.

Checkpoint
According to the definition, which of the following is NOT a characteristic of a rational judgment?
A) It is perceived by the intellect by itself.
B) It depends on repeated observation.
C) It does not depend on the stipulation of a stipulator.
D) It may be a judgment of affirmation or negation.

Key Takeaway
Rational judgments are propositions that the intellect affirms or denies without relying on repetition or convention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The passage says rational judgments do not depend on convention. In your daily worship, which acts are done purely out of convention? How might you transform them into acts of rational conviction?

Rational Judgments

Rational Judgment: Definition and Three Divisions
Sanusi defines rational judgment and enumerates its three divisions, then defines the necessary with subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of rational judgment, its three necessary divisions, and the distinction between intuitive and theoretical necessity.

Core Idea
Rational judgment is what the mind perceives independently, divisible into necessary, impossible, and possible.

Sanusi begins by clarifying the nature of rational judgment—what the mind grasps on its own. He then explains that every conceivable thing falls into one of three categories: necessary, impossible, or possible. Finally, he elaborates on the necessary, distinguishing between self-evident and reflective necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the definition and three divisions
Find the definition of rational judgment. Then note the three divisions: necessary, impossible, possible. Understand that every conceivable thing falls into one of these.
The three divisions are explicitly stated as wajib, mustahil, ja'iz.
3
Understand the necessary and its subtypes
The necessary is defined as that whose non-existence is inconceivable. Then note the two subtypes: daruri (intuitive, immediate) and nazari (theoretical, needing reflection). Use the examples given: body occupying space for daruri, God's eternity for nazari.
This distinction is important for recognizing self-evident vs. reasoned truths.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why these three categories are foundational for kalam. They provide the logical framework for all theological arguments.
This connects to the broader aim of establishing sound reasoning about God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحكم العقلى: فهو عبارة عما يدرك العقل بمجرده ثبوته أو نفيه من غير توقف على تكرر ولا وضع واضع. وهذا الثالث هو الذي تعرضنا له في أصل العقيدة، فقولنا: الحكم العقلي (احتراز من الشرعي والعادي، وقد عرفت معناهما) قوله: (ينحصر في ثلاثة أقسام) يعني: أن كل ما يتصور في العقل - أي: يدركه - لا يخلو عن هذه الثلاثة الأقسام؛ أي: لا بد له أن يتصف بواحد منها؛ إما بالوجوب، أو الاستحالة، أو الجواز. [حد الواجب] قوله: (فالواجب: ما لا يتصور في العقل عدمه) يعني: أن الواجب العقلي: هو الأمر الذي لا يدرك في العقل عدمه؛ إما ابتداء بلا احتياج إلى سبق نظر، ويسمى الضروري؛ كالتحيز للجرم مثلاً؛ فإن العقل ابتداء لا يدرك انفكاك الجرم عن التحيز؛ أي: أخذ قدر ذاته من الفراغ، وإما بعد سبق النظر؛ ويسمى نظرياً؛ كالقدم لمولانا جل وعز؛ فإن العقل إنما يدرك وجوبه له تعالى إذا فكر وعرف
As for the rational judgment: it is an expression of what the mind perceives by itself its existence or its negation without depending on repetition or the stipulation of a stipulator. And this third is what we have encountered in the foundation of the creed. So our saying: the rational judgment (as a restriction against the religious and the customary, and you have known their meanings) his saying: (it is confined to three divisions) means: that everything that is conceivable in the mind — i.e., that it perceives — does not escape these three divisions; i.e., it must be characterized by one of them: either necessity, impossibility, or possibility. [Section on the necessary] His saying: (the necessary: that whose non-existence is not conceivable in the mind) means: that the rational necessary is the matter whose non-existence is not perceived in the mind; either initially without need for prior reflection, and it is called the necessary intuitive/daruri — like occupying space for a body, for example; for the mind initially does not perceive the separation of a body from occupying space, i.e., taking the measure of its essence from the void; or after prior reflection, and it is called the necessary theoretical/nazari — like eternity for our Lord, Mighty and Majestic; for the mind only perceives its necessity for Him, exalted, if it reflects and knows
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage defines rational judgment (al-hukm al-'aqli) and explains its three divisions, then defines the necessary (al-wajib) in detail. The author begins by stating that rational judgment is what the mind perceives by itself—without reliance on repetition or convention—regarding the existence or negation of something. This distinguishes it from religious (shar'i) and customary ('adi) judgments. He then asserts that this rational judgment is confined to three categories: necessary (wajib), impossible (mustahil), and possible (ja'iz). Every conceivable thing must fall into one of these. Next, he defines the necessary as that whose non-existence is not conceivable in the mind. This inconceivability can be either immediate (daruri), needing no prior reasoning—like the impossibility of a body not occupying space—or reflective (nazari), requiring prior thought—like the eternity of God, which the mind understands only after considering the attributes of the Creator. The author thus distinguishes between self-evident and theoretical necessities within the rational category.

Key Terms
الحكم العقلي
الواجب
المستحيل
الجائز
الضروري
النظري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rational judgments are derived from religious texts or custom.
Correction: Rational judgment is based solely on intellectual perception, independent of revelation or societal convention, as Sanusi emphasizes by excluding shar'i and 'adi judgments.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the three divisions of rational judgment?
A) Religious, customary, rational
B) Necessary, impossible, possible
C) Self-evident, theoretical, customary
D) Definite, probable, doubtful

Key Takeaway
Rational judgment is the mind's independent perception, and everything conceivable is either necessary, impossible, or possible; necessity can be self-evident or theoretical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have read that rational judgment is independent of repetition and stipulation. Think of a core belief you hold about God—is it grounded in genuine rational reflection, or is it merely inherited from culture or upbringing? Pause and identify one belief you could re-examine rationally today.
?
The passage distinguishes between self-evident and reflective rational judgments. Have you ever undertaken the mental effort to reflect deeply on God's attributes, such as His eternity, or do you accept them as a matter of course? If not, what specific step will you take this week to engage in such reflection?
?
Rational judgments, the lesson says, are what the mind perceives by itself. Ask yourself: in moments of doubt or confusion about your faith, do you rely on rational demonstration or on emotional comfort? Are you willing to follow reason where it leads, even if it challenges what you assume to be true? What intellectual virtue do you need to cultivate starting today?

The Three Rational Judgments and the Definition of the Necessary
Sanusi enumerates the three rational judgments and defines the necessary with its subdivisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational judgments that all mental concepts fall into, and how the necessary is defined and divided into necessary by necessity and theoretical.

Core Idea
Every rational concept is either necessary, impossible, or possible.

Sanusi begins by establishing the foundational classification of rational judgments. He explains that everything conceivable by the mind must be either necessary, impossible, or possible. Then he defines the necessary and shows that it is further divided into two types: one known immediately and one known through reflection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the three rational judgments
Notice how Sanusi states that every mental concept falls into one of three categories: necessary, impossible, or possible. This is the basic grammar of kalam.
2
Understand the definition of the necessary
Sanusi defines the necessary as that whose non-existence is not conceivable in the mind. This is a definition based on mental conception, not external existence.
3
Learn the two types of the necessary
The necessary is divided into the necessary by necessity (ḍarūrī) known without reflection, and the theoretical (naẓarī) known after reasoning. Examples are given for each.
4
Reflect on the examples
Spatial occupation for a body is an example of the immediate necessary; pre-eternity of God is an example requiring reasoning. See how each fits the definition.

And this third [judgment] is what we have presented in the chapter on creed. So our saying: 'the rational judgment' (is a safeguard against the revealed and the customary, and you have already known their meanings) — his saying: 'is confined to three divisions' means: that everything that is conceivable in the mind — i.e., that the mind perceives — does not escape these three divisions; i.e., it must necessarily be characterized by one of them: either necessity, impossibility, or possibility.
[Definition of the Necessary]
His saying: 'The necessary is that whose non-existence is not conceivable in the mind' means that the rational necessary is the matter whose non-existence is not perceived in the mind; either originally without need for prior reflection, and it is called the necessary [by necessity]; like the occupation of space for a body, for example; for the mind initially does not perceive a body's separation from spatial occupation — i.e., taking up a portion of empty space — or after prior reflection, and it is called theoretical; like pre-eternity for our Lord, may He be glorified and exalted; for the mind only perceives its necessity for Him, exalted, when it reflects and knows what follows from establishing temporality for Him, may He be glorified and exalted, of circular reasoning or infinite regress, which are clearly impossible.
So you have thus learned the division of the necessary into necessary [by necessity] and theoretical.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage defines the rational judgment and its three divisions, then defines the necessary (wājib) with its subdivisions. The passage begins by stating that the third rational judgment — the one under discussion in the creed — is confined to three categories: necessary, impossible, and possible. This is a classification: every mental concept must fall into one of these. The author then defines the necessary as 'that whose non-existence is not conceivable in the mind.' This is a definition by mental conception, not by external existence. The necessary is then divided into two types: one that is known immediately without any reasoning, called 'necessary by necessity' (ḍarūrī), and one that requires reflection, called 'theoretical' (naẓarī). Examples are given: for the necessary by necessity, the spatial occupation of a body — the mind cannot conceive a body without occupying space. For the theoretical, the pre-eternity of God — one must reason that if God were temporal, it would lead to an infinite regress or circularity, which is impossible, so His pre-eternity is necessary. The passage also includes lengthy glosses clarifying that the definition applies to both absolute and conditional necessity, and that the 'non-existence' includes both positive and negative realities (like God's attributes). The conclusion reiterates that the necessary is thus divided into two types.

Misconception: The necessary is about what actually exists in external reality.
Correction: The necessary is defined by mental conception: the mind cannot conceive its non-existence, regardless of external existence.

Key Takeaway
The necessary is defined by mental inconceivability of non-existence, not by external existence.

Definition of the Impossible and Its Subdivisions
Sanusi defines the impossible and divides it into two types with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's definition of the impossible and its division into necessary (ḍarūrī) and theoretical (naẓarī), with examples.

Core Idea
The impossible is what the mind cannot conceive as existing, either immediately or through reasoning.

Sanusi now turns to the impossible (mustaḥīl), mirroring the structure he used for the necessary. He provides a clear definition and then divides it into two types: that which is immediately inconceivable and that which requires reflection to recognize its impossibility. Through concrete examples, he shows how both types function in theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read Sanusi's definition of the impossible carefully. Notice that it mirrors the definition of the necessary but in the negative: 'that whose existence is not conceivable in the mind.'
2
Identify the two divisions
The passage divides the impossible into necessary (ḍarūrī) and theoretical (naẓarī). Identify the examples given for each.
3
Analyze the example of God as a body
Work through the reasoning Sanusi provides to show why God being a body is impossible. It leads to a conjunction of contradictories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ حد المستحيل ]
قوله : ( والمستحيلُ : ما لا يُتصوَّرُ في العقل وجوده ) يعني أيضاً : ابتداءً ، أو بعد سبق النظرِ
وو
فمثالُ الأَوَّلِ : عرقُ الجرمِ عن الحركة والسكون ؛ أي : تجرُّده عنهما معاً ؛ بحيثُ لا يوجدُ فيهِ واحدٌ منهما ؛ فإنَّ العقل ابتداءً لا يتصوَّرُ ثبوت هذا المعنى للجرم
ومثال الثاني : كونُ الذاتِ العليَّةِ جِرْماً تعالى الله عن ذلكَ علوّاً كبيراً ؛ فإنَّ استحالة هذا المعنى عليهِ جلَّ وعزَّ إِنَّما يدركُهُ العقلُ بعد أنْ يسبق له النظرُ فيما يترتَّبُ على هذا مِنَ المستحيل ؛ وهو الجمعُ بين النقيضين ؛ وذلكَ أنَّه قد وجبَ لمولانا جل وعزَّ القدم والبقاء ؛ لئلا يلزم الدورُ أوِ التسلسلُ حينئذ لو كانَ تعالى حادثاً سبحانه ، فلو كانَ تعالى جرماً لوجبَ له الحدوث ، تعالى عن ذلك علوّاً كبيراً ؛ لما تقرَّرَ مِنْ وجوب الحدوثِ لكلِّ جِرم ، فيلزم إذا لو كانَ تعالى جِرْماً أَنْ يكونَ واجب القدمِ ؛ لألوهيَّتِهِ ، وواجب الحدوثِ ؛ لجرميتِهِ تعالى ذلك ، وذلك جمع بين النقيضين لا محالة .
فقد عرفت أيضاً بهذا : انقسام المستحيل إلى ضروري ونظري
[Definition of the Impossible]
His saying: 'The impossible is that whose existence is not conceivable in the mind' means also: either originally or after prior reflection.
An example of the first: a body being stripped of motion and rest; i.e., being divested of both together, such that neither is present in it; for the mind does not originally conceive the establishment of this meaning for a body.
And an example of the second: the Supreme Essence being a body — exalted is God above that greatly! For the impossibility of this meaning for Him, may He be glorified and exalted, is only perceived by the mind after prior reflection on what follows from this of the impossible, namely the conjunction of two contradictories. And that is because pre-eternity and everlastingness have been established as necessary for our Lord, may He be glorified and exalted, so that circular reasoning or infinite regress would not occur if He, exalted, were temporal — exalted is He above that! For it has been established that temporality is necessary for every body. So if He, exalted, were a body, He would be both necessarily pre-eternal due to His divinity and necessarily temporal due to His being a body — exalted is He above that! And that is a conjunction of two contradictories without doubt.
So you have also thus learned the division of the impossible into necessary and theoretical.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage defines the impossible (mustaḥīl) and divides it into two types, following the same pattern as the definition of the necessary. The definition is given: 'The impossible is that whose existence is not conceivable in the mind.' As with the necessary, the impossibility can be either immediate (ḍarūrī) or requiring reflection (naẓarī). Two examples illustrate this. The first example of immediate impossibility is a body being free of both motion and rest. The mind cannot conceive of a body that is neither moving nor still; it is an immediate mental impossibility. The second example of theoretical impossibility is the attribute of being a body being attributed to God. To recognize this impossibility, one must reason: God is necessarily pre-eternal and everlasting (because if He were temporal, an infinite regress would follow). Also, every body is temporal (ḥādith) by necessity. Therefore, if God were a body, He would be both pre-eternal (due to His divinity) and temporal (due to being a body), which is a conjunction of two contradictories (jamʿ bayn al-naqīḍayn). This absurd result shows that God being a body is impossible, but it requires reasoning. The passage concludes by noting that the impossible is thus divided into necessary and theoretical.

Key Terms
المستحيل
الضروري
النظري
الجمع بين النقيضين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The impossible is simply what cannot exist in the external world.
Correction: Sanusi defines the impossible as that whose existence is not conceivable in the mind, whether immediately or after reflection. This includes logical contradictions, not just empirical impossibilities.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of the impossible by necessity (mustaḥīl ḍarūrī) given by Sanusi?
A) God being a body.
B) A body being free of motion and rest.
C) The existence of a creator.
D) A body being both black and white simultaneously.

Key Takeaway
The impossible is what the mind cannot conceive as existing, and it is divided into necessary and theoretical, with the second type requiring logical reasoning to expose a contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that some truths about God require careful reasoning to uncover. When you reflect on your own beliefs about Allah, are there areas where you have accepted something without thinking through the logical implications? What would happen if you subjected that belief to the same kind of reasoning Sanusi uses here?
?
The proof that God cannot be a body rests on avoiding contradictions. Have you ever held two beliefs about God that, when examined, actually contradict each other? How would you reconcile them in light of the principle that Allah is free from all imperfection?
?
Sanusi’s definition of the impossible is that the mind cannot conceive its existence. Is your own understanding of what is impossible for God based on immediate intuition or on sound reasoning? When was the last time you took the time to work through a logical proof to strengthen your conviction in a core Islamic belief?

The Impossible: Definition and Division
Sanusi defines the impossible and divides it into immediate and theoretical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the impossible in rational theology and its division into immediate and theoretical, along with key examples.

Core Idea
The impossible is what cannot be conceived, either immediately or after reflection.

In the previous section, Sanusi established the division of the necessary into immediate and theoretical. Now he turns to the other side of the three rational judgments: the impossible. He defines it in a parallel fashion and shows that it too can be known immediately or through theoretical reasoning. Two concrete examples — a body without motion and rest, and God being a body — clarify the distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to understand the flow.
Notice the parallel structure with the earlier division of the necessary.
2
Identify the two examples of the impossible
Find the examples for immediate and theoretical impossible. Note why the first is immediately inconceivable and the second requires reflection.
The first example is a body devoid of both motion and rest.
3
Reflect on the theoretical impossible
Ask yourself: Why is God being a body impossible only after reflection? What contradiction does it entail?
The contradiction is between necessary pre-eternity and necessary contingency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يترتب على ثبوتِ الحدوثِ لَهُ جلَّ وعزَّ مِنَ الدور أو التسلسل الواضحي الاستحالة . فقد عرفت بهاذا انقسام الواجب إلى ضروري ونظري . قوله : ( والمستحيلُ : ما لا يُتصوَّرُ في العقل وجوده ) يعني أيضاً : ابتداءً ، أو بعد سبق النظرِ وو فمثالُ الأَوَّلِ : عرقُ الجرمِ عن الحركة والسكون ؛ أي : تجرُّده عنهما معاً ؛ بحيثُ لا يوجدُ فيهِ واحدٌ منهما ؛ فإنَّ العقل ابتداءً لا يتصوَّرُ ثبوت هذا المعنى للجرم . ومثال الثاني : كونُ الذاتِ العليَّةِ جِرْماً تعالى الله عن ذلكَ علوّاً كبيراً ؛ فإنَّ استحالة هذا المعنى عليهِ جلَّ وعزَّ إِنَّما يدركُهُ العقلُ بعد أنْ يسبق له النظرُ فيما يترتَّبُ على هذا مِنَ المستحيل ؛ وهو الجمعُ بين النقيضين ؛ وذلكَ أنَّه قد وجبَ لمولانا جل وعزَّ القدم والبقاء ؛ لئلا يلزم الدورُ أوِ التسلسلُ حينئذ لو كانَ تعالى حادثاً سبحانه ، فلو كانَ تعالى جرماً لوجبَ له الحدوث ، تعالى عن ذلك علوّاً كبيراً ؛ لما تقرَّرَ مِنْ وجوب الحدوثِ لكلِّ جِرم ، فيلزم إذا لو كانَ تعالى جِرْماً أَنْ يكونَ واجب القدمِ ؛ لألوهيَّتِهِ ، وواجب الحدوثِ ؛ لجرميتِهِ تعالى ذلك ، وذلك جمع بين النقيضين لا محالة . فقد عرفت أيضاً بهذا : انقسام المستحيل إلى ضروري ونظري
What follows from establishing contingency for Him, may He be exalted and glorified, of circularity or regress whose impossibility is obvious. So you have known by this the division of the necessary into immediate and theoretical. He said: 'And the impossible: that whose existence is not conceivable in the intellect' — meaning also: initially, or after preceding reflection. An example of the first: the divestment of a body of motion and rest; i.e., its being stripped of both, such that neither of them is found in it, for the intellect initially does not conceive the establishment of this meaning for a body. An example of the second: the Supreme Essence being a body — exalted is God above that with great exaltation. For the impossibility of this meaning for Him, may He be exalted and glorified, is only grasped by the intellect after preceding reflection on what follows from this of the impossible, which is the combination of two contradictories. That is because pre-eternity and everlastingness have become necessary for our Master, may He be exalted and glorified, lest circularity or regress occur if He were contingent — exalted is He. So if He were a body, contingency would be necessary for Him — exalted is He above that with great exaltation — because it has been settled that contingency is necessary for every body. Thus it would follow, if He were a body, that He would be necessarily pre-eternal due to His divinity and necessarily contingent due to His becoming a body — exalted is He — and that is a combination of two contradictories without doubt. So you have also known by this: the division of the impossible into immediate and theoretical.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage serves a dual purpose: first, it reminds the reader that the necessary (al-wājib) has been divided into immediate (ḍarūrī) and theoretical (naẓarī) based on the preceding proof of God's existence. Then it proceeds to define the impossible (al-mustaḥīl) and similarly divides it. The definition of the impossible is 'that whose existence is not conceivable in the intellect,' which like the necessary can be known either immediately (without reflection) or after theoretical consideration. Two examples illustrate this: the immediate impossible is a body being devoid of both motion and rest—the intellect immediately judges this inconceivable. The theoretical impossible is God being a body—the intellect only realizes its impossibility after reflecting that it would entail combining two contradictories: because God must be pre-eternal (to avoid circularity) but every body is contingent, so God-as-body would be both pre-eternal and contingent, a clear contradiction. The passage concludes that the impossible likewise divides into immediate and theoretical, mirroring the division of the necessary.

قدم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The theoretical impossible is actually possible but difficult to conceive.
Correction: It is impossible in reality, but the intellect requires reflection to recognize its impossibility due to an underlying contradiction.

Key Takeaway

Definition and Division of the Contingent
Sanusi defines the contingent and divides it into immediate and theoretical with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's definition of the contingent and its division into immediate and theoretical, illustrated with examples from motion and divine reward/punishment.

Core Idea
The contingent is what the mind can equally conceive as existing or not existing.

In this passage, Sanusi completes his classification of rational judgments by defining the contingent. He not only defines it but immediately shows that, like the necessary and impossible, the contingent comes in two forms: what the mind grasps instantly and what requires reasoning. The examples—motion in a body and God's punishment of the obedient—reveal how deep theological principles underpin what at first seems like a simple logical category.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and division
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting how Sanusi defines 'al-jā'iz' and then immediately splits it into two kinds: immediate and theoretical.
Notice the parallel structure with the previous categories of necessary and impossible.
2
Understand the immediate example
Consider the example of motion in a body. Why does the mind accept both existence and non-existence of a particular motion without any reasoning? Because it is self-evident that a body can move or not move.
3
Work through the theoretical example
Trace the reasoning for the punishment of the obedient. Why does this require reflection on the proof of God's unity? Because we naturally assume actions have inherent moral worth, but the proof shows all actions are created by God alone, removing inherent necessity.
This example illustrates how deep theology can overturn initial intuitions.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Understand the implication: faith and disbelief are rationally equal. This does not mean God treats them as equal in law, but that no action inherently deserves reward or punishment—God is absolutely sovereign.
This safeguards against any notion of independent causality or inherent moral necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( والجائزُ : ما يصحُ في العقل وجوده وعدمه ) يعني أيضاً : إمَّا ضرورةً ، وإِمَّا بعدَ سَبْقِ النظرِ . فمثالُ الأَوَّلِ : اتصافُ الجرمِ بخصوص الحركة ؛ فإنَّ العقل يدركُ ابتداءً صحة وجودِها للجرمِ وصحة عدمها له . ومثال الثاني : تعذيب المطيع الذي لم يعص الله قطُّ طرفة عينٍ فإنَّ العقلَ إنَّما يحكم بجواز هذا التعذيب في حقِّهِ عقلاً بعدَ أنْ ينظر في برهان الوحدانيّةِ ويعرف أنَّ الأفعال كلَّها مخلوقة لمولانا جل وعزَّ ، لا أثر لكلِّ ما سواه تعالى في أثر ما ألبتةَ ، فيلزمُ مِنْ ذلك استواء الإيمان والكفر والطاعة والمعصية عقلاً ، وأنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ مِنْ هذهِ يصح أنْ يُجعَلَ أمارةً على ما جُعِلَ الآخرُ أمارةً عليهِ
He said: 'And the contingent: that whose existence and non-existence are valid in the intellect' — meaning also: either necessarily, or after preceding reflection. An example of the first: the attribution of a body to a particular motion, for the intellect initially perceives the validity of its existence for the body and the validity of its non-existence for it. An example of the second: punishing the obedient person who has never disobeyed God for the blink of an eye, for the intellect only judges the permissibility of this punishment in his case, rationally, after it has reflected on the proof of unity (burhān al-waḥdāniyya) and knows that all actions are created by our Master, may He be exalted and glorified, with absolutely no effect for anything other than Him in any effect whatsoever. Hence it follows from that that faith and disbelief, obedience and disobedience are equal rationally, and that each one of these may be made a sign for what the other is made a sign for.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage defines the contingent (al-jā'iz) as what the intellect accepts as possibly existent or non-existent. Like the necessary and impossible, the contingent is divided into immediate (ḍarūrī) and theoretical (naẓarī). The immediate example is a body's specific motion: the intellect instantly recognizes that the body could either have that motion or not have it. The theoretical example is God punishing an obedient person who never sinned. The intellect does not immediately accept this as possible; only after reflecting on the proof of God's unity (burhān al-waḥdāniyya) does it realize that all actions are created solely by God, with no secondary causes having any independent effect. Consequently, from a purely rational standpoint, faith and disbelief, obedience and disobedience are all equal in possibility — none inherently deserves reward or punishment. God can arbitrarily make any of them a sign for what He wills. This underscores God's absolute sovereignty and refutes any notion of inherent moral necessity or independent causality.

Key Terms
جائز
ضرورة
نظر
برهان الوحدانيّة
خلق الأفعال
استواء الإيمان والكفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because faith and disbelief are rationally equal, God treats them equally in His law.
Correction: The passage only states rational equality regarding inherent merit; God's revealed law establishes distinctions. The equality underscores that no action has intrinsic causal power—all is subject to God's will.

Checkpoint
Why does the intellect only judge the punishment of the obedient as permissible after reflection?
A) Because reflection shows that punishment is always just
B) Because the proof of divine unity shows that all actions are created by God alone, so no action is inherently deserving of punishment
C) Because the obedient person may have hidden sins
D) Because the intellect needs time to understand the concept of punishment

Key Takeaway
The contingent is whatever the intellect can conceive as either existing or not existing, and it may be known immediately or through reasoning, with deep implications for God's absolute creative power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that God could rationally punish you even if you never sinned, how would that change the way you approach your obedience today? Would you still obey out of love and hope, or would you fall into despair? What does your answer reveal about your current understanding of God's mercy?
?
The passage teaches that all actions are created by God alone, with no effect for anything else. Does your daily life reflect this belief, or do you still feel that your efforts independently produce results? Where do you see the most resistance in your heart to this truth?
?
If faith and disbelief are rationally equal, then your faith does not inherently deserve reward. What is your ultimate ground for hope? Is it solely God's free choice to make faith a sign for Paradise? How does that make you feel about your salvation?

Definition and Division of al-Ja'iz (The Contingent)
Sanusi defines al-ja'iz, divides it into necessary/theoretical, and defends the theoretical subtype.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-ja'iz, its division into necessary and theoretical, the argument that some rational judgments require reflection, and why the intellect alone judges possibility.

Core Idea
Contingency is not always immediately obvious; some require rational proof.

Sanusi now turns to the third rational judgment: the contingent. While some things are obviously possible or impossible at first glance, others require deeper reasoning. In this passage, he defines al-ja'iz, splits it into two types, and provides a key theological example—punishment of the obedient—to show why reflection on God's nature is needed. Understanding this division is essential for grasping how kalam distinguishes between what is merely thinkable and what is rationally sound.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and division
Start by reading the Arabic and translation of the definition. Notice that Sanusi immediately states that contingency can be either necessary (daruri) or theoretical (nazari).
The two types mirror the same distinction found in the earlier judgments of wajib and mustahil.
2
Examine the necessary example
Look at the example of a body's motion. Why does the intellect immediately see both existence and non-existence as valid? Because no contradiction arises from either state.
Necessary contingency is self-evident; no prior reasoning is needed.
3
Unpack the theoretical example
Now study the punishment of the obedient person. Sanusi argues that this is not immediately obvious—it depends on knowing that all actions are created by God, that injustice (zulm) is impossible for Him, and that reward/punishment are purely by His choice. Work through the proof step by step.
This shows how theological doctrines (tawhid, divine justice) shape rational judgments.
4
Understand the six subdivisions and the qualification
Finally, see how Sanusi concludes that the three judgments (wajib, mustahil, ja'iz) each have two types, making six total. Also note why he adds 'in the intellect' to the definition: to separate rational possibility from revealed actuality.
The Law (shar') informs us what God actually chose, but reason alone cannot determine that.
