قوله : ( والجائزُ : ما يصحُ في العقل وجوده وعدمه ) يعني أيضاً : إمَّا ضرورةً ، وإمَّا بعدَ سبقِ النظرِ فمثالُ الأَوَّلِ : اتصافُ الجرمِ بخصوص الحركة ؛ فإنَّ العقل يدركُ ابتداءً صحة وجودِها للجرمِ وصحة عدمها له . ومثال الثاني : تعذيب المطيع الذي لم يعص الله قطُّ طرفة عينٍ فإنَّ العقلَ إنَّما يحكم بجواز هذا التعذيب في حقِّهِ عقلاً بعدَ أنْ ينظر في برهان الوحدانيّةِ ويعرف أنَّ الأفعال كلَّها مخلوقة لمولانا جل وعزَّ ، لا أثر لكلِّ ما سواه تعالى في أثر ما ألبتةَ ، فيلزمُ مِنْ ذلك استواء الإيمان والكفر والطاعة والمعصية عقلاً ، وأنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ مِنْ هذهِ يصح أنْ يُجعَلَ أمارةً على ما جُعِلَ الآخرُ أمارةً عليهِ . والظلم على مولانا جل وعزَّ مستحيل كيفما فعل أو حكم ؛ إذ الظلم هو التصرُّفُ على خلافِ الأمرِ ، ومولانا جل وعزَّ هو الأمرُ الناهي المبيحُ المحرِّمُ ، فلا أمْرَ ولا نهْيَ يتوجَّهُ إِليهِ مِنْ سواهُ ؛ إِذْ كلُّ ما سواه ملك له جلَّ وعلا ، لا يُبْدِى شيئاً ولا يعيده ، ولا أثر له في شيء البتةَ ، ولا شريكَ لهُ تعالى في مُلْكِهِ ، ولا يُسأل عما يفعلُ . فصح إذاً أنْ يُدرِكَ العقلُ لكلِّ مِنَ المؤمن والكافر والمطيع والعاصي صحة وجودِ الثواب والعقاب أو عدمهما ، واختصاص كلَّ واحد بما اختص بهِ مِنْ ذلكَ إنَّما هو بمحض اختيار مولانا جل وعزَّ ، لا بسبب عقليّ اقتضى ذلك ، لكنَّ إدراك العقل لجواز هذا المعنى موقوف على تحقيق النظر الذي قدَّمنا . فبانَ لكَ بهذا : أنَّ الجائز ينقسمُ أيضاً إلى قسمين ؛ ضروري ونظري ، كما انقسم القسمانِ اللذانِ قبلَهُ . واتضح بهذا : أنَّ الأقسام الثلاثةَ قد تفرَّعَتْ إلى ستة أقسام ؛ مِنْ ضرب ثلاثة في اثنينِ ؛ إذْ كلُّ قسم منها فيهِ قسمانِ . وإنما قيَّدنا الصحة بالعقل في حق الجائز فقلنا فيه : ( ما يصح في العقلِ ) ليدخلَ فيهِ نحوُ جواز العذابِ في حق المطيع ؛ فإِنَّ العقل هو الحاكم بصحة وجودِ العذابِ وعدمِهِ في حقه ؛ بمعنى : أَنَّهُ لو وقع كلٌّ منهما لم يلزم مِنْ وقوعِهِ نقص في حق مولانا تعالى ولا محال ألبتةَ ، أمَّا الشرعُ : فقد بيَّنَ أنَّ اللهَ تعالى قد اختار بمحْضِ
His saying: (And al-ja'iz (the contingent): that whose existence and non-existence are valid in the intellect) means also: either necessarily, or after preceding reflection. An example of the first: the qualification of a body with a particular motion; for the intellect perceives initially the validity of its existence for the body and the validity of its non-existence for it. An example of the second: the punishment of the obedient one who never disobeyed God for the blink of an eye; for the intellect only judges the permissibility of this punishment in his case rationally after he reflects on the proof of oneness and knows that all actions are created by our Lord, Glorious and Exalted, that there is no effect for anything other than Him, Exalted, in effect at all, so from that follows the equality of faith and disbelief, obedience and disobedience rationally, and that each one of these can be made an indicator of what the other is made an indicator of. And injustice (al-zulm) is impossible for our Lord, Glorious and Exalted, however He acts or judges; for injustice is acting contrary to the command, and our Lord, Glorious and Exalted, is the commander, the prohibitor, the permissifier, the prohibitor, so no command or prohibition is directed to Him from other than Him; since everything other than Him is His possession, Glorious and Exalted, He does not initiate anything nor repeat it, and there is no effect for anything at all, and there is no partner for Him, Exalted, in His dominion, and He is not asked about what He does. So it is valid then that the intellect perceives for each of the believer and the disbeliever, the obedient and the disobedient, the validity of the existence of reward and punishment or their non-existence, and the specification of each one with what he is specified with from that is only by the mere choice of our Lord, Glorious and Exalted, not by a rational cause necessitating that, but the intellect's perception of the permissibility of this meaning depends on the verification of the reflection that we presented. So it became clear to you by this: that al-ja'iz also divides into two divisions: necessary and theoretical, just as the two preceding divisions divided. And it became clear by this: that the three divisions have branched into six divisions; by multiplying three by two; since each division among them contains two divisions. And we only qualified validity with the intellect in the case of al-ja'iz, and we said in it: (that which is valid in the intellect) to include in it something like the permissibility of punishment in the case of the obedient one; for the intellect is the judge of the validity of the existence of punishment and its non-existence in his case, meaning: that if each of them occurred, no deficiency in the right of our Lord, Exalted, nor any impossibility would follow from its occurrence at all. As for the Law: it has clarified that God, Exalted, has chosen by mere...
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is a definition and division of al-ja'iz (the contingent), the third of the three rational judgments. Sanusi begins by quoting his earlier definition: 'al-ja'iz is that whose existence and non-existence are valid in the intellect.' He then explains that this validity can be either necessary (daruri) — i.e., the intellect judges it immediately without reflection — or theoretical (nazari) — i.e., after reflection. He provides an example of each. The necessary example is a body's specific motion: the intellect sees at once that it may exist or not exist for the body. The theoretical example is the punishment of a person who has never disobeyed God. The intellect does not immediately judge that this is possible; it first requires reflection on the proof of God's oneness (tawhid) and the fact that all actions are created by God alone, with no real causal efficacy from creatures. From this, the intellect deduces that faith and disbelief, obedience and disobedience are rationally equal—each can be made a sign for reward or punishment by God's will alone. Sanusi then argues that injustice (zulm) is impossible for God because injustice is defined as acting contrary to a command, and God is the commander and prohibitor, with no command above Him. Everything other than God is His possession, utterly dependent, with no independent effect. Therefore, God cannot be unjust, and whatever He does is not subject to questioning. Consequently, the intellect can conceive that the believer and disbeliever, obedient and disobedient could equally receive reward or punishment; their actual differing outcomes are due solely to God's choice, not to any rational necessity. However, this theoretical judgment depends on prior reflection. Sanusi then draws two conclusions: (1) al-ja'iz itself divides into necessary and theoretical, just like the other two judgments (wajib and mustahil). (2) The three judgments together yield six subdivisions (each having a necessary and theoretical type). Finally, he explains why he qualified 'validity' with 'in the intellect' in his definition: to include cases like the punishment of the obedient, which the intellect deems possible (no logical contradiction) even though revelation specifies that it will not happen. The intellect judges that if such punishment occurred, it would neither entail a deficiency in God nor an impossibility. Revelation then tells us God's actual choice. The passage ends mid-sentence in the manuscript, but the argument's core is complete: the definition, division, proof, and qualification are all presented.

ضروري
نظري
الظلم
برهان الوحدانية
العقل
الشرع

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'theoretical contingency' means it is arbitrary or based on personal opinion.
Correction: Theoretical contingency is strictly defined: it is a rational judgment that becomes evident only after proper argumentation, not mere speculation. Sanusi's example shows it depends on sound theological reasoning (tawhid and divine justice).

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why is punishing the obedient person an example of theoretical (nazari) contingency?
A) Because the intellect immediately sees it as possible.
B) Because it depends on a prior argument about God's creating actions and the impossibility of injustice.
C) Because it is impossible in both intellect and revelation.
D) Because it is a case of necessary contingency.

Key Takeaway
The contingent (al-ja'iz) is divided into necessary and theoretical, and the theoretical type requires rational argument—such as the proof that God alone creates actions and cannot be unjust—to establish its possibility in the intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If God were to punish you despite your obedience, would you still consider Him just? This lesson says no injustice can be attributed to God. Does your heart accept that, or does it resist? What does that resistance reveal about your understanding of God's absolute sovereignty?
?
The intellect requires prior reflection to accept the possibility of the obedient being punished. Have you ever paused to think through the implications of God's oneness and power for your own accountability? Or do you assume that because you are 'good' you deserve reward? This lesson challenges that assumption—how will you let it reshape your worship starting today?

Subdivision of the Possible: From Three to Six Rational Divisions
Sanusi subdivides ja'iz and derives six rational divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the three basic rational judgments (wajib, mustahil, ja'iz) each subdivide into necessary (daruri) and theoretical (nazari) categories, producing six divisions in total.

Core Idea
Every rational judgment branches into necessary and theoretical forms, yielding six total divisions.

Sanusi has established that the possible (al-ja'iz) is a distinct rational judgment alongside the necessary and impossible. Now he shows that the possible also has two subtypes: what the intellect perceives necessarily and what it perceives only through inquiry. This subdivision mirrors the earlier ones, and together they produce a complete set of six rational categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the three original divisions
The passage refers to 'the two preceding divisions' (al-qismāni alladhāni qablahu). Recall that these are the wajib and mustahil. Now the third, ja'iz, is also being subdivided.
The phrase 'as the two preceding divisions were divided' indicates symmetry.
3
Understand the subdivision of ja'iz
Sanusi says ja'iz is divided into daruri and nazari. Daruri is perceived necessarily; nazari requires inquiry. This mirrors the subdivision of wajib and mustahil.
4
Calculate the six divisions
Sanusi says multiply three (original divisions) by two (each has two subtypes) to get six. Confirm that each of the three now has a daruri and nazari form.
This is a simple but important logical expansion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما سواه ملك له جلَّ وعلا (۱)، لا يُبْدِى شيئاً ولا يعيده (۲)، ولا أثر له في شيء البتةَ، ولا شريكَ لهُ تعالى في مُلْكِهِ، ولا يُسأل عما يفعلُ فصح إذاً أنْ يُدرِكَ العقلُ لكلِّ مِنَ المؤمن والكافر والمطيع والعاصي صحة وجودِ الثواب والعقاب أو عدمهما (۳)، واختصاص كلَّ واحد بما اختص بهِ مِنْ ذلكَ إنَّما هو بمحض اختيار مولانا جل وعزَّ، لا بسبب عقليّ اقتضى ذلك، لكنَّ إدراك العقل لجواز هذا المعنى موقوف على تحقيق النظر الذي قدَّمنا فبانَ لكَ بهذا : أنَّ الجائز ينقسمُ أيضاً إلى قسمين؛ ضروري ونظري، كما انقسم القسمانِ اللذانِ قبلَهُ. واتضح بهذا : أنَّ الأقسام الثلاثةَ قد تفرَّعَتْ إلى ستة أقسام؛ مِنْ ضرب ثلاثة في اثنينِ؛ إذْ كلُّ قسم منها فيهِ قسمانِ. وإنما قيَّدنا الصحة بالعقل في حق الجائز(٤)؛ فقلنا فيه : ( ما يصح في العقلِ ) ليدخلَ فيهِ نحوُ جواز العذابِ في حق المطيع؛ فإنَّ العقل هو الحاكم بصحة وجودِ العذابِ وعدمِهِ في حقه؛ بمعنى : أَنَّهُ لو وقع كلٌّ منهما لم يلزم مِنْ وقوعِهِ نقص في حق مولانا تعالى ولا محال ألبتةَ، أمَّا الشرعُ : فقد بيَّنَ أنَّ اللهَ تعالى قد اختار بمحْضِ فضله للمؤمن المطيع أحد الأمرين الجائزين في حقه؛ وهو الثواب والنعيم المقيم، كما اختار تعالى بعدلِهِ للكافرِ الجائز الآخرَ؛ وهو النار والعذاب الأليم.
Other than that belongs to His dominion, may He be glorified and exalted, He neither originates anything nor returns it, and there is no effect for it in anything at all, and there is no partner for Him, exalted, in His dominion, and He is not asked about what He does. So it is correct, then, that the intellect perceives for everyone—believer, disbeliever, obedient, and disobedient—the possibility of existence of reward and punishment or their non-existence, and the specification of each one with that which he is specified with from that is only by the pure choice of our Master, may He be glorified and exalted, not by a rational cause that necessitated that. However, the intellect's perception of the permissibility of this meaning depends on the verification of the inquiry which we have presented. So it became clear to you by this: that the possible (al-ja'iz) is also divided into two parts: necessary (daruri) and theoretical (nazari), just as the two preceding divisions were divided. And it became clear by this: that the three divisions have branched into six divisions, by multiplying three by two, since each division has two divisions within it. And we only restricted the validity to the intellect with respect to the possible (al-ja'iz); we said concerning it: 'what is valid in the intellect' to include in it something like the permissibility of punishment in the case of the obedient; for the intellect is the judge of the validity of the existence of punishment and its non-existence in his case, meaning: if each of the two occurred, no deficiency would follow from their occurrence in the right of our Lord, exalted, nor any impossibility at all. As for the divine law (al-shar'), it has clarified that Allah, exalted, has chosen by pure grace for the believing obedient one of the two permissible matters in his case, which is reward and permanent bliss, just as He, exalted, chose by His justice for the disbeliever the other permissible, which is the Fire and painful punishment.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument that completes the discussion of the possible (al-ja'iz) and its subdivision. First, Sanusi reaffirms the absolute sovereignty of Allah: nothing besides Him has any real effect, no partner, and He is not questioned. From this, he concludes that the intellect can perceive the possibility (sihha) of reward and punishment for both believer and disbeliever, obedient and disobedient. However, the specific assignment of reward or punishment is purely by Allah's choice, not by any rational necessity. But this intellectual perception of possibility depends on the prior verification of rational inquiry (al-nazar). This leads to the key point: the possible (al-ja'iz) itself is divided into two types: necessary (daruri) and theoretical (nazari), just like the two previous categories (wajib and mustahil) were divided. Then he derives that the three original divisions (wajib, mustahil, ja'iz) now each have two subcategories, producing six divisions in total (3 × 2). Next, he explains why the definition of ja'iz was restricted to 'what is valid in the intellect' (ma yasihh fi al-'aql). This restriction is to include cases like the possibility of punishment for an obedient person: the intellect judges it as possible, meaning if it occurred no impossibility would follow. However, the divine law (shar') informs us that Allah in His grace gives reward to the believer and in His justice gives punishment to the disbeliever. Thus the intellect only judges possibility, not actuality. This passage guards against the misconception that rational possibility implies actual occurrence or any compulsion on Allah.

Key Terms
جائز (ja'iz)
ضروري (daruri)
نظري (nazari)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many total rational divisions result from the subdivision?
A) Three
B) Six
C) Nine
D) Twelve

Key Takeaway
The three rational judgments each subdivide into necessary and theoretical forms, giving six divisions in total.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your fate in the afterlife is determined solely by Allah's choice, not by your actions alone, do you still strive to obey Him? What does that say about your motivation?
?
Sanusi says Allah 'is not asked about what He does.' When you face a trial or injustice, do you submit to this truth, or do you find yourself demanding reasons? What would it take for you to truly trust His choice?
?
The intellect can only perceive possibility, not certainty, in matters of reward and punishment. How does this uncertainty affect your daily life? Are you living as if your salvation is possible but not guaranteed? How should that change your urgency?

Intellect vs. Divine Law: The Possibility of Punishment for the Obedient
Sanusi explains the restriction to intellectual validity and the role of divine law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi restricts the definition of the possible to 'what is valid in the intellect' and how this relates to the divine law's actual decree.

Core Idea
The intellect judges possibility; divine law reveals actuality.

After establishing the sixfold division, Sanusi turns to a subtle but crucial point: the definition of the possible includes only intellectual validity, not actual occurrence. This distinction is vital for cases like punishment for the obedient—rationally possible but never actual. The intellect alone cannot determine what Allah will do; it only affirms what does not entail impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the restriction
Sanusi says 'we only restricted the validity to the intellect with respect to the possible'. Find this phrase and understand what it means.
The restriction is that the definition of ja'iz includes 'sihha fi al-'aql' (valid in intellect), not necessarily actual.
3
Analyze the example
Sanusi gives the example of punishment for the obedient. The intellect says it is possible (no impossibility), but the divine law (shar') tells us that Allah chooses reward for the believer and punishment for the disbeliever.
This shows that intellect and divine law have different roles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما سواه ملك له جلَّ وعلا (۱)، لا يُبْدِى شيئاً ولا يعيده (۲)، ولا أثر له في شيء البتةَ، ولا شريكَ لهُ تعالى في مُلْكِهِ، ولا يُسأل عما يفعلُ فصح إذاً أنْ يُدرِكَ العقلُ لكلِّ مِنَ المؤمن والكافر والمطيع والعاصي صحة وجودِ الثواب والعقاب أو عدمهما (۳)، واختصاص كلَّ واحد بما اختص بهِ مِنْ ذلكَ إنَّما هو بمحض اختيار مولانا جل وعزَّ، لا بسبب عقليّ اقتضى ذلك، لكنَّ إدراك العقل لجواز هذا المعنى موقوف على تحقيق النظر الذي قدَّمنا فبانَ لكَ بهذا : أنَّ الجائز ينقسمُ أيضاً إلى قسمين؛ ضروري ونظري، كما انقسم القسمانِ اللذانِ قبلَهُ. واتضح بهذا : أنَّ الأقسام الثلاثةَ قد تفرَّعَتْ إلى ستة أقسام؛ مِنْ ضرب ثلاثة في اثنينِ؛ إذْ كلُّ قسم منها فيهِ قسمانِ. وإنما قيَّدنا الصحة بالعقل في حق الجائز(٤)؛ فقلنا فيه : ( ما يصح في العقلِ ) ليدخلَ فيهِ نحوُ جواز العذابِ في حق المطيع؛ فإنَّ العقل هو الحاكم بصحة وجودِ العذابِ وعدمِهِ في حقه؛ بمعنى : أَنَّهُ لو وقع كلٌّ منهما لم يلزم مِنْ وقوعِهِ نقص في حق مولانا تعالى ولا محال ألبتةَ، أمَّا الشرعُ : فقد بيَّنَ أنَّ اللهَ تعالى قد اختار بمحْضِ فضله للمؤمن المطيع أحد الأمرين الجائزين في حقه؛ وهو الثواب والنعيم المقيم، كما اختار تعالى بعدلِهِ للكافرِ الجائز الآخرَ؛ وهو النار والعذاب الأليم.
Other than that belongs to His dominion, may He be glorified and exalted, He neither originates anything nor returns it, and there is no effect for it in anything at all, and there is no partner for Him, exalted, in His dominion, and He is not asked about what He does. So it is correct, then, that the intellect perceives for everyone—believer, disbeliever, obedient, and disobedient—the possibility of existence of reward and punishment or their non-existence, and the specification of each one with that which he is specified with from that is only by the pure choice of our Master, may He be glorified and exalted, not by a rational cause that necessitated that. However, the intellect's perception of the permissibility of this meaning depends on the verification of the inquiry which we have presented. So it became clear to you by this: that the possible (al-ja'iz) is also divided into two parts: necessary (daruri) and theoretical (nazari), just as the two preceding divisions were divided. And it became clear by this: that the three divisions have branched into six divisions, by multiplying three by two, since each division has two divisions within it. And we only restricted the validity to the intellect with respect to the possible (al-ja'iz); we said concerning it: 'what is valid in the intellect' to include in it something like the permissibility of punishment in the case of the obedient; for the intellect is the judge of the validity of the existence of punishment and its non-existence in his case, meaning: if each of the two occurred, no deficiency would follow from their occurrence in the right of our Lord, exalted, nor any impossibility at all. As for the divine law (al-shar'), it has clarified that Allah, exalted, has chosen by pure grace for the believing obedient one of the two permissible matters in his case, which is reward and permanent bliss, just as He, exalted, chose by His justice for the disbeliever the other permissible, which is the Fire and painful punishment.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument that completes the discussion of the possible (al-ja'iz) and its subdivision. First, Sanusi reaffirms the absolute sovereignty of Allah: nothing besides Him has any real effect, no partner, and He is not questioned. From this, he concludes that the intellect can perceive the possibility (sihha) of reward and punishment for both believer and disbeliever, obedient and disobedient. However, the specific assignment of reward or punishment is purely by Allah's choice, not by any rational necessity. But this intellectual perception of possibility depends on the prior verification of rational inquiry (al-nazar). This leads to the key point: the possible (al-ja'iz) itself is divided into two types: necessary (daruri) and theoretical (nazari), just like the two previous categories (wajib and mustahil) were divided. Then he derives that the three original divisions (wajib, mustahil, ja'iz) now each have two subcategories, producing six divisions in total (3 × 2). Next, he explains why the definition of ja'iz was restricted to 'what is valid in the intellect' (ma yasihh fi al-'aql). This restriction is to include cases like the possibility of punishment for an obedient person: the intellect judges it as possible, meaning if it occurred no impossibility would follow. However, the divine law (shar') informs us that Allah in His grace gives reward to the believer and in His justice gives punishment to the disbeliever. Thus the intellect only judges possibility, not actuality. This passage guards against the misconception that rational possibility implies actual occurrence or any compulsion on Allah.

Key Terms
صحة (sihha)
عقل ('aql)
شرع (shar')
ثواب (thawab)
عقاب ('iqab)
اختيار (ikhtiyar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if something is rationally possible, it must occur or that the intellect can determine Allah's actual choice.
Correction: Sanusi shows that rational possibility only means no impossibility follows; actual occurrence is determined by divine law based on Allah's choice.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi restrict the definition of the possible to 'what is valid in the intellect'?
A) To include cases like punishment for the obedient, which is rationally possible
B) To exclude cases that are impossible
C) To align with divine law
D) To simplify the definition

Key Takeaway
The possible is defined by intellectual validity, not actual occurrence; the divine law reveals which possibility Allah chooses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your good deeds, do you feel a sense of entitlement to paradise, or do you truly recognize that any reward is purely by Allah's grace and not something you can demand? How does this lesson correct any hidden assumption of deservingness?
?
If you were to face a trial or hardship that you feel is undeserved, would you still trust that Allah's choice is just and wise, even though the intellect alone cannot demand otherwise? What does your reaction reveal about your reliance on your own judgment versus submitting to divine decree?
?
This lesson shows that the intellect can conceive of punishment even for the obedient. How does this truth affect your fear of Allah and your hope in His mercy? Are you more driven by fear or hope, and how does that balance reflect your understanding of Allah's grace and justice?

Motion and Rest as an Example of the Three Rational Judgments
Sanusi illustrates the three rational judgments using motion and rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concrete example of motion and rest clarifies the abstract concepts of rationally necessary, impossible, and possible.

Core Idea
A body must be moving or at rest, but neither alone is necessary.

Sanusi now grounds the three rational judgments in a tangible example: the motion or rest of a physical body. This concrete illustration makes the abstract categories intuitive. Pay careful attention to the distinction between the necessary (one of them without specification) and the possible (one of them with specification).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice how Sanusi introduces the example with 'And know' (واعلم), signalling an important clarification.
The Arabic is concise; each judgment is defined in one clause.
2
Identify the three judgments in the example
Find the three rational judgments as applied to motion and rest: necessary (wajib), impossible (mustahil), possible (ja'iz). Write down each definition in your own words.
The necessary is that one of the two must exist, but not which one.
3
Reflect on the key nuance
Understand why the necessary is 'one of them, not specifically determined' – the necessity is for a body to be either moving or at rest, not for a specific state. The impossible is both being absent, and the possible is the actual presence of one specific state.
This nuance is often misunderstood.
4
Test with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to confirm your grasp of the necessary judgment in this example.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أنَّ الحركة والسكونَ للجِرْمِ يصح أن يُمثل بهما لأقسامِ الحكم العقليّ الثلاثةِ؛ فالواجبُ العقلي : ثبوتُ أحدهما لا بعينه للجزم، والمستحيلُ : نفيهما معاً عن الجرم، والجائز : ثبوتُ أحدهما بالخصوص للجرمِ.
And know: that motion and rest for a body can be used as an example for the three divisions of rational judgment. The rationally necessary (al-wajib al-'aqli): the existence of one of them, not specifically [determined], for a body. The impossible (al-mustahil): the negation of both together from the body. The possible (al-ja'iz): the existence of one of them specifically for the body.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage presents a concrete example to illustrate the three rational judgments. Sanusi uses motion and rest of a body (jirm). He states three cases: First, the rationally necessary (al-wajib al-'aqli) is that a body must have either motion or rest, without specifying which one. It is impossible for a body to be devoid of both. Second, the impossible (al-mustahil) is that both motion and rest are negated from the body simultaneously. Third, the possible (al-ja'iz) is that a body has specifically one of them (e.g., it is moving). This example clarifies that the necessary does not require a specific attribute, only that one of the two must exist. The impossible is the absence of both, and the possible is the actual presence of one particular attribute.

Key Terms
حركة (haraka)
سكون (sukun)
جرم (jirm)
واجب عقلي (wajib 'aqli)
مستحيل (mustahil)
جائز (ja'iz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the rationally necessary (wajib) in the example means either motion or rest is individually necessary for the body (i.e., the body must be moving, or must be at rest).
Correction: The necessary is that one of the two must exist, but not specifically which one. The necessity is for the disjunction, not for a particular state.

Checkpoint
In the example of motion and rest, what does the rationally necessary (wajib 'aqli) require?
A) That the body must be in motion
B) That the body must be at rest
C) That the body must have either motion or rest, without specifying which
D) That both motion and rest are impossible

Key Takeaway
The example of motion and rest shows that the rationally necessary is the indeterminate existence of one of two opposites, not a specific one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that the rationally necessary is not always specific: a body must have either motion or rest, but neither is necessary in itself. In your own life, are you treating some outcomes as necessary when they are actually only possible? How might this lesson help you release unnecessary anxiety about things that could go either way?
?
The example shows that simultaneous denial of opposites is impossible. Where in your faith or worldview are you tempted to deny what cannot be denied—like ignoring that you must choose between good and bad actions? How does this lesson call you to clarity?
?
If you had to explain to someone why a body cannot be neither moving nor at rest, you would use the same rational necessity Sanusi teaches. What does that tell you about the power of the mind to know necessary truths? Are you using that gift to know your Creator, or neglecting it?

Motion and Rest as Examples for the Three Rational Judgments
Sanusi exemplifies the three rational judgments using motion and rest and stresses their foundational role in intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the three rational judgments (necessary, impossible, possible) apply to a concrete example and why mastering these categories is essential for rational thought about God.

Core Idea
The three rational judgments are not just theory—they are the very structure of intellect.

Sanusi uses the familiar attributes of motion and rest to make the three rational judgments tangible. By seeing how each judgment applies to a physical body, the abstract categories become clear. He then stresses that internalizing these categories is what makes a person truly rational. This passage grounds the entire theological project in a clear, intuitive framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a sense of Sanusi's argument. Notice the clear structure: he states the example, defines each judgment, then explains why this knowledge is crucial.
2
Identify the three judgments in the example
Focus on Sanusi's application of necessary, impossible, and possible to motion and rest. Write down each definition in your own words.
3
Reflect on the quote from Imam al-Haramayn
Consider the claim that knowledge of these three divisions is the very intellect. What does this imply about the role of rational categories in faith?
4
Ask: why does Sanusi emphasize repetition?
He says repeating these ideas 'familiarizes the heart' and removes effort. Think about how memorization and reflection work together in learning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم: أنَّ الحركة والسكونَ للجِرْمِ يصح أن يُمثل بهما لأقسامِ الحكم العقليّ الثلاثةِ؛ فالواجبُ العقلي : ثبوتُ أحدهما لا بعينه للجزم، والمستحيلُ : نفيهما معاً عن الجرم، والجائز : ثبوتُ أحدهما بالخصوص للجرمِ . واعلم : أنَّ معرفة هذهِ الأقسام الثلاثة وتكريرها تأنيس القلبِ بأمثلتها؛ حتى لا يحتاج الفكرُ في استحضار معانيها إلى كلفة أصلاً . . مما هو ضروري على كلّ عاقل يريدُ أنْ يفوز بمعرفةِ اللهِ تعالى ومعرفة رُسُلِهِ عليهم الصلاةُ والسلامُ، بل قد قال إمام الحرمين وجماعةٌ : إنَّ معرفة هذه الأقسام الثلاثة هي نفس العقل، فمَنْ لم يعرفها فليس بعاقل أصلاً
And know: that motion and rest for a body are valid to be exemplified by them for the three divisions of the rational judgment. So the rationally necessary: the establishment of one of them not specifically determined for the body, and the impossible: the negation of both together from the body, and the possible: the establishment of one of them specifically for the body. And know: that knowledge of these three divisions and their repetition familiarizes the heart with their examples, so that the mind does not need any effort at all in recalling their meanings... which is necessary for every rational person who desires to attain knowledge of Allah Most High and knowledge of His messengers, peace and blessings be upon them. Rather, Imam al-Haramayn and a group said: Indeed, knowledge of these three divisions is the very intellect, so whoever does not know them is not rational at all.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage provides a concrete example for understanding the three rational judgments (al-ahkam al-'aqliyya) that are fundamental in Islamic theology. The author uses motion and rest—two mutually exclusive attributes of a physical body—to illustrate each judgment. The rationally necessary (al-wajib al-'aqli) is that one of the two must be present in the body, though not specifically determined; the impossible (al-mustahil) is that both are absent simultaneously; the possible (al-ja'iz) is that one of them is present specifically. He then stresses the importance of repeatedly contemplating these three divisions until their meanings become effortlessly present in the mind, as this is essential for anyone seeking knowledge of God and His messengers. The passage culminates in a quote from Imam al-Haramayn that knowledge of these three divisions is the very essence of intellect ('aql), so a person lacking this knowledge is not truly rational. This underscores that these rational categories are not merely academic but define the foundation of sound reasoning itself.

Key Terms
الحكم العقلي
الواجب العقلي
المستحيل
الجائز
الحركة
السكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think these categories are arbitrary or merely logical exercises.
Correction: Sanusi shows that these categories are essential for rational thought about God, and Imam al-Haramayn equates them with intellect itself.

Checkpoint

A) Both motion and rest must be present
B) One of the two must be present, but not specifically determined
C) One of the two must be present specifically
D) Both must be absent

Key Takeaway
The three rational judgments are the very building blocks of rational thought, and mastering them is essential for knowing God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi says that whoever does not know these three divisions is not rational at all. Have you ever considered your own grasp of necessary, impossible, and possible? If you are honest, do you truly understand when something is rationally necessary, impossible, or possible when it comes to your beliefs about Allah? How would you defend your understanding from the Qur'an and Sunnah? Commit to memorizing these three categories so that your intellect is not deficient.
?
The repetition of these examples 'familiarizes the heart' so that the mind recalls them without effort. What repeated reflection on Allah's attributes and the rational judgments do you practice daily? If you have no such habit, could your intellect be spiritually idle? Resolve to repeat and reflect on these three judgments each day this week.
?

Do you consider your current intellectual effort sufficient for knowing Allah and His messengers? What concrete step will you take today to strengthen this foundational knowledge?

The Obligation of Knowledge vs. Blind Imitation
Sanusi defines knowledge and distinguishes it from blind imitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why true faith requires knowledge (ma'rifa) based on evidence, not blind imitation (taqlid).

Core Idea
Faith without evidence is mere imitation; real faith rests on knowledge through proof.

Sanusi opens his discussion of what every legally responsible person must know by setting a foundational distinction: the difference between true knowledge (ma'rifa) and blind imitation (taqlid). Without this distinction, one might think that simply believing without evidence is sufficient for faith. This passage establishes that genuine faith requires decisive conviction based on evidence, not just following others.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic text once, then the English translation. Note that Sanusi uses the word 'know' (يعرف) deliberately instead of 'be certain' (يجزم).
The choice of words is a key teaching point.
2
Identify the obligation
Sanusi states that every mukallaf (adult, sane person) must know what is necessary, impossible, and possible regarding Allah and the messengers. This is the core obligation.
3
Understand the definition of ma'rifa
Ma'rifa is defined as decisive conviction (jazzm) that conforms to reality (mutābiq) based on evidence (dalīl). This is more than mere certainty; it requires a proof.
This contrasts with taqlid, which is conviction without evidence.
4
Distinguish from taqlid
Taqlid is also decisive conviction conforming to reality, but without evidence. Sanusi explicitly says it is not sufficient for the beliefs of faith.
This shows that evidence is necessary for valid faith.
5
Reflect on the implication
Why is evidence required? Because faith must be based on insight (baṣīra). Without evidence, one's belief is simply following others and may not be genuine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام على المكلف وما يجب عليه معرفة ويجب على كل مكلف شرعاً: أن يعرف ما يجب في حق مولانا جل وعز، وما يستحيل، وما يجوز، وكذلك يجب عليه أن يعرف مثل ذلك في حق الرسل عليهم الصلاة والسلام. يعني: أنه يجب شرعاً على كل مكلف ـ وهو البالغ العاقل ـ أن يعرف ما ذكر؛ لأنه بمعرفة ذلك يكون مؤمناً محققاً في إيمانه، على بصيرة في دينه. وإنما قال: ( يعرف ) ولم يقل: ( يجزم ) إشارة منه إلى أن المطلوب في عقائد الإيمان المعرفة؛ وهي الجزم المطابق عن دليل. ولا يكفي فيها التقليد؛ وهو الجزم المطابق في عقائد الإيمان بلا دليل.
The speech concerning the legally responsible person and what he must know. It is obligatory upon every legally responsible person by sacred law: that he know what is necessary in the right of our Master, Mighty and Majestic, and what is impossible, and what is possible; and likewise it is obligatory upon him to know the like of that regarding the messengers, prayers and peace be upon them. Meaning: that it is obligatory by sacred law upon every legally responsible person—and he is the adult, sane person—that he know what was mentioned; because by knowing that he becomes a believer verifying his faith, with insight into his religion. And he only said: 'he knows' and did not say: 'he is certain' as an indication that the required thing in the beliefs of faith is knowledge (ma‘rifa); and it is decisive conviction conforming to reality based on evidence. And blind imitation (taqlīd) is not sufficient in it; and it is decisive conviction conforming to reality in the beliefs of faith without evidence.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the obligation of knowledge. It begins by stating that every legally responsible person must know what is necessary, impossible, and possible regarding Allah and the prophets. It then defines 'knowledge' (ma‘rifa) as decisive conviction that matches reality and is based on evidence, and distinguishes it from blind imitation (taqlīd), which is decisive conviction without evidence. The commentator emphasizes that the word 'know' is used instead of 'be certain' to indicate that the required thing is knowledge, which includes evidence.

Key Terms
mukallaf
ma‘rifa
taqlīd

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that simply believing in Allah and the prophets without evidence is enough for faith.
Correction: This passage shows that faith requires knowledge (ma'rifa) which is decisive conviction based on evidence; blind imitation (taqlid) without evidence is insufficient.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of ma‘rifa (knowledge) in the context of faith?
A) Blind imitation without evidence
B) Decisive conviction conforming to reality based on evidence
C) Mere certainty without evidence
D) Following the majority of scholars

Key Takeaway
True faith in Islamic theology requires decisive conviction based on evidence (ma'rifa), not blind imitation (taqlid).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that true faith requires knowledge based on evidence, not blind imitation. In your own life, is there any belief you hold about Allah simply because you were told it, without having examined the evidence? What would it take for you to transform that into ma'rifa?
?
Sanusi says that by knowing the necessary, impossible, and possible regarding Allah, one becomes a believer with insight. Have you ever actually sat down to learn and verify these categories? If not, what is stopping you from fulfilling this obligation today?
?
If you were to die right now, would your faith be considered ma'rifa or taqlid? Because the passage says taqlid is not sufficient. What concrete step will you take this week to move from imitation to verified knowledge?

The Obligation and Definition of Nazar
Sanusi states the correct view and provides the definition of nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct Islamic view that rational investigation (nazar) is obligatory and its definition as orderly reflection leading to knowledge or dominant opinion.

Core Idea
Nazar is the only path to knowledge of God and is the first obligation.

Sanusi begins by stating the correct view from the Qur'an and Sunna: nazar is obligatory. He then cites Ibn al-Arabi's definition, which clarifies what nazar means and its purpose. This lesson lays the foundation for the subsequent refutation of alternative paths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the correct view
Read the passage and note that Sanusi states the correct view (al-haqq) is the obligation of nazar, with the preponderant view being that it is a condition for the validity of faith.
2
Focus on the definition of nazar
Find the definition of nazar within the quote from Ibn al-Arabi: it is orderly reflection in the soul along a path that leads to knowledge or dominant opinion.
The definition is precise and technical.
3
Understand nazar as the first obligation
Reflect on why nazar is the first obligation: it is the means to the end of knowing God, which is the primary duty of every responsible person.
This sets the stage for the rest of the discussion.

والحقُّ الذي يدلُّ عليهِ الكتاب والسنة : وجوب النظر ، مع التردُّدِ في كونه شرطاً في صحة الإيمان أو لا ، والراجح : أنَّه شرط ، وقد عزا ابنُ العربي القول بأنَّهُ تعالى يُعلَمُ بالتقليد . . إلى المبتدعة ، ونصه في كتابه ( المتوسط في الاعتقادِ ) : ( اعلموا علمَكمُ الله تعالى : أنَّ هذا العلم المكلَّفَ به لا يحصلُ ضرورةً ولا إلهاماً ، ولا يصح التقليدُ فيهِ ، ولا يجوزُ أنْ يكونَ الخبرُ طريقاً إليه ، وإنَّما الطريقُ إليه النظرُ ، ورسمه : أَنَّهُ الفكر المرتب في النفس على طريق يفضي إلى العلم أوِ الظَّنِّ ، يطلبُ بِهِ مَنْ قَامَ بِهِ علماً في العلميَّاتِ ، أو غلبة ظنّ في المظنوناتِ ولو كان هذا العلم يحصلُ ضرورةً لأدرك ذلك جميعُ العقلاء ، أو إلهاماً لوضعَهُ الله تعالى في قلب كلِّ حيٍّ ؛ ليتحقق به التكليف ، وأيضاً : فإنَّ الإلهام نوع ضرورة ، وقد أبطلنا الضرورة ، فلا يصح أَنْ يُقالَ : إِنَّهُ يُعلَمُ بالتقليدِ كما قالَتْهُ جماعةٌ مِنَ المبتدعة ؛ لأنَّهُ لو عُرِفَ بالتقليدِ لَمَا كانَ قولُ واحدٍ مِنَ المقلدين أولى بالاتباع والانقيادِ إليهِ مِنَ الآخر ، وأقوالهم متضادةٌ ومختلفة ولا يجوز أيضاً أن يُقالَ : إِنَّهُ يُعلَمُ بالخبر ؛ لأنَّ مَنْ لم يعلمه فكيف يعلمُ أَنَّ الخبر خبره ؟! فثبتَ أنَّ طريقه النظرُ ؛ وهو أوَّلُ واجب على المكلَّف؛ إذ المعرفةُ أوَّلُ الواجباتِ ، ولا تحصل إلا بهِ ، فبضرورة تقديمه عليها تثبتُ له صفة الوجوب قبلها ، وإيجابُ المعرفة بالله معلومٌ مِنْ دينِ الأمة ضرورة .
The correct view indicated by the Book and the Sunna is the obligation of rational investigation (nazar), with hesitation about whether it is a condition for the validity of faith or not. The preponderant view is that it is a condition. Ibn al-Arabi attributed the claim that God Most High is known by blind imitation (taqlid) to the innovators, and his text in his book 'al-Mutawassit fi al-I'tiqad' is: 'Know—may God teach you—that this obligatory knowledge is not obtained by necessity (darura) nor by inspiration (ilham), nor is blind imitation (taqlid) valid in it, nor may report (khabar) be a path to it. The only path to it is rational investigation (nazar), and its definition is: it is orderly reflection in the soul along a path that leads to knowledge or dominant opinion, by which the one performing it seeks knowledge in theoretical matters or dominant opinion in probable matters. If this knowledge were obtained by necessity, all rational beings would perceive it; or if by inspiration, God would have placed it in the heart of every living being to realize the obligation. Also, inspiration is a type of necessity, and we have refuted necessity. Thus it is not valid to say that He is known by blind imitation (taqlid), as a group of innovators have said, because if He were known by blind imitation, then no statement of one blind imitator would be more worthy of being followed and submitted to than another, and their statements are contradictory and diverse. Nor is it permissible to say that He is known by report (khabar), because one who does not know Him—how can he know that the report is His report? Thus it is established that His path is rational investigation (nazar); it is the first obligation upon the legally responsible person, since knowledge (ma'rifa) is the first of obligations, and it is not obtained except through it. Therefore, by the necessity of giving it precedence over knowledge, the attribute of obligation is established for it before knowledge. And the obligation of knowledge of God is known from the religion of the community by necessity.'
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a combined definition, proof, and refutation. It begins by stating the correct view (al-haqq) from Qur'an and Sunna: rational investigation (nazar) is obligatory, and the preponderant view is that it is a condition for the validity of faith. It then cites Ibn al-Arabi's definition of nazar as ordered reflection leading to knowledge or dominant opinion. The proof then refutes four alternative paths: (1) necessity (darura) – if knowledge were innate, all rational beings would have it; (2) inspiration (ilham) – if God inspired it, everyone would be inspired, but inspiration is a type of necessity already refuted; (3) blind imitation (taqlid) – because blind followers hold contradictory views, so no one could be preferred; (4) report (khabar) – one who does not know God cannot verify the report is from Him. The conclusion is that nazar is the only path and that it is the first obligation (awwal wajib) because knowledge (ma'rifa) is the first obligation, and it does not occur without nazar. Thus nazar is obligatory as a means before the end.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn al-Arabi's definition, what is nazar?
A) Any form of thinking
B) Orderly reflection leading to knowledge or dominant opinion
C) Blind acceptance of authority
D) A type of inspiration

Key Takeaway
Nazar is the first obligation and is defined as systematic reflection leading to knowledge or sound opinion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever truly engaged in nazar—ordered reflection about your Creator—or have you relied on what you were taught without personal verification? What would it mean for you to begin this obligation today?
?
If nazar is the first obligation and the only path to certainty about Allah, then what excuse do you have for not dedicating serious, structured thought to this matter? Will you schedule time this week to reflect deeply on the proofs of His existence?
?
You have heard that taqlid is insufficient for faith. Examine your own heart: do you believe in Allah because you have personally reasoned through the evidence, or because you inherited belief from your family? If it is the latter, are you willing to risk standing before Him with a borrowed faith?

Rational Judgments
intermediate
4 min
Refutation of Necessity and Inspiration
Sanusi refutes darura and ilham as paths to knowledge of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge of God cannot be obtained by necessity (darura) or inspiration (ilham), and how these refutations strengthen the case for nazar.

Core Idea
If knowledge of God were innate or inspired, everyone would have it—but they don't.

Sanusi now refutes the first two alternative paths: necessity and inspiration. He argues that if knowledge were necessary, all rational beings would possess it; and if it were inspiration, God would have placed it in every heart. Since this is not the case, these paths fail. This lesson shows the logical basis for rejecting darura and ilham.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of darura
Read the first argument: if knowledge of God were necessary (darura), all rational people would perceive it. But they do not, so it is not necessary.
2
Read the refutation of ilham
Read the second argument: if it were inspiration (ilham), God would have placed it in every living being to fulfill the obligation. Since that has not happened, ilham is invalid.
3
Understand the link between darura and ilham
Note that Sanusi says ilham is a type of darura, so the refutation of darura applies to ilham as well.
This shows the refutation is comprehensive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحقُّ الذي يدلُّ عليهِ الكتاب والسنة : وجوب النظر ، مع التردُّدِ في كونه شرطاً في صحة الإيمان أو لا ، والراجح : أنَّه شرط ، وقد عزا ابنُ العربي القول بأنَّهُ تعالى يُعلَمُ بالتقليد . . إلى المبتدعة ، ونصه في كتابه ( المتوسط في الاعتقادِ ) : ( اعلموا علمَكمُ الله تعالى : أنَّ هذا العلم المكلَّفَ به لا يحصلُ ضرورةً ولا إلهاماً ، ولا يصح التقليدُ فيهِ ، ولا يجوزُ أنْ يكونَ الخبرُ طريقاً إليه ، وإنَّما الطريقُ إليه النظرُ ، ورسمه : أَنَّهُ الفكر المرتب في النفس على طريق يفضي إلى العلم أوِ الظَّنِّ ، يطلبُ بِهِ مَنْ قَامَ بِهِ علماً في العلميَّاتِ ، أو غلبة ظنّ في المظنوناتِ ولو كان هذا العلم يحصلُ ضرورةً لأدرك ذلك جميعُ العقلاء ، أو إلهاماً لوضعَهُ الله تعالى في قلب كلِّ حيٍّ ؛ ليتحقق به التكليف ، وأيضاً : فإنَّ الإلهام نوع ضرورة ، وقد أبطلنا الضرورة ، فلا يصح أَنْ يُقالَ : إِنَّهُ يُعلَمُ بالتقليدِ كما قالَتْهُ جماعةٌ مِنَ المبتدعة ؛ لأنَّهُ لو عُرِفَ بالتقليدِ لَمَا كانَ قولُ واحدٍ مِنَ المقلدين أولى بالاتباع والانقيادِ إليهِ مِنَ الآخر ، وأقوالهم متضادةٌ ومختلفة ولا يجوز أيضاً أن يُقالَ : إِنَّهُ يُعلَمُ بالخبر ؛ لأنَّ مَنْ لم يعلمه فكيف يعلمُ أَنَّ الخبر خبره ؟! فثبتَ أنَّ طريقه النظرُ ؛ وهو أوَّلُ واجب على المكلَّف؛ إذ المعرفةُ أوَّلُ الواجباتِ ، ولا تحصل إلا بهِ ، فبضرورة تقديمه عليها تثبتُ له صفة الوجوب قبلها ، وإيجابُ المعرفة بالله معلومٌ مِنْ دينِ الأمة ضرورة .

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think inspiration is a valid source of knowledge of God independent of reason.
Correction: Sanusi shows inspiration is a type of necessity, and since necessity is refuted, inspiration is also invalid.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument against darura (necessity) in this passage?
A) It leads to contradictions.
B) All rational people would have knowledge of God, but they do not.
C) It requires blind imitation.
D) It is not mentioned in the Qur'an.

Key Takeaway
Both necessity and inspiration are invalid because they would result in universal knowledge, which is not observed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If knowledge of God were necessary, you would have no excuse to ignore Him. But you know it is not necessary, so why do you still live as if you can afford to postpone seeking that knowledge?
?
Sanusi refutes inspiration—but have you ever relied on a 'feeling' or 'spiritual experience' as your primary proof of faith, rather than rational certainty? How would you defend your belief when that feeling is absent?
?
The obligation (taklīf) demands knowledge. Are you actively pursuing the knowledge of God through the proper means, or are you assuming it will come to you without effort, as in the false paths of necessity or inspiration?

Refutation of Taqlid and Khabar; Nazar as First Obligation
Sanusi refutes taqlid and khabar, then concludes that nazar is the first obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why blind imitation (taqlid) and report (khabar) cannot lead to knowledge of God, and you will see that nazar is the first obligation.

Core Idea
Contradictory claims of imitators and the impossibility of verifying a report from God prove that only nazar works.

Sanusi continues his refutation, now taking on taqlid and khabar. He shows that blind imitation leads to contradictory claims with no way to prefer one; and report fails because one cannot verify the source without prior knowledge of God. This establishes that nazar is the only path and the first obligatory act.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of taqlid
Read the argument: if knowledge of God came through taqlid, then every imitator's claim would be equally valid, but their statements are contradictory, so no one can be preferred.
2
Read the refutation of khabar
Read the argument: one who does not know God cannot know that a report is from God, so khabar is circular.
3
Understand the conclusion
Read the conclusion: since all other paths fail, nazar is the only path, and it is the first obligation because knowledge (ma'rifa) is the first obligation and requires nazar.
This highlights the preeminence of nazar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
Why is khabar (report) invalid as a path to knowledge of God according to this passage?
A) Because reports are always unreliable.
B) Because one who does not know God cannot verify that the report is from Him.
C) Because reports contradict each other.
D) Because reports require blind imitation.

Key Takeaway
Only nazar leads to knowledge of God, and it is the first duty of every accountable person.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your beliefs concerning God, do they come from your own rational investigation or from simply following what you were taught? If from the latter, are you willing to examine them critically as Sanusi demands?
?
The passage insists that knowing God is your first duty—before any ritual act. Have you ever truly devoted time to thinking deeply about His existence, attributes, and what He requires of you? If not, what is holding you back?
?
Sanusi says that without prior knowledge, you cannot verify a report from God. How many of your religious convictions are based on reports you have never personally justified? Will you continue to accept them blindly, or will you take up the responsibility of nazar?

Rational Judgments
beginner
4 min
Faith Cannot Precede Knowledge
Sanusi refutes faith before knowledge and outlines procedure for those who delay.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why faith cannot be imposed before rational inquiry and how to respond to someone who asks for time to reflect, according to Sanusi's argument.

Core Idea
Faith without knowledge leads to confusion and cannot distinguish truth from falsehood.

This lesson explores a key objection in Islamic theology: can faith be required before understanding? Sanusi argues that such a demand is logically weak and provides a practical method for dealing with those who request time for reflection. Through his reasoning, we see the importance of rational inquiry as a foundation for genuine faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text carefully. It begins with an objection and then provides Sanusi's answer. Notice the two main parts: refutation of faith before knowledge, and the treatment of one who asks for time.
The passage uses a concise argumentative style.
2
Identify the key argument
Focus on why obligating belief without knowledge is weak. The reason given is that it leads to equating a true prophet with a false pretender, and forces one to believe in something that might later be found false.
This logical consequence is central.
3
Understand the practical response
Sanusi then addresses the case of a person who requests time to reflect. He gives a clear procedure: if the person knows how to reflect, let him do so; if not, let him listen. Then, if he believes, his sincerity is confirmed; if he refuses, he is obstinate and must be compelled.
This shows a balanced approach between reason and authority.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this argument defends the necessity of rational inquiry before faith. How does it relate to the broader Islamic emphasis on knowledge?

نظرِهِ إلى حد يتطاول بهِ المدى فيهِ ، أم تُقدِّرونَهُ بمقدارِ فتحكمونَ فيهِ بغير نص ؟ فالجواب : أنَّا نقولُ : أمَّا القولُ بوجوب الإيمانِ قبل المعرفة فضعيفٌ ؛ لأنَّ إلزام التصديق بما لا تُعلَمُ صحَتُهُ يؤدي إلى التسويةِ بينَ النبي والمتنبي ، وأنَّهُ يؤمنُ أولاً فينظرُ ؛ فإنْ تبيَّنَ لَهُ الحقُّ فيتمادى ، وإن تبيَّنَ لهُ الباطل فيرجعُ وقد اعتقد الكفر . وأمَّا إذا دعا المطلوب بالإيمان إلى النظر . . فيُقالُ له : إِنْ كنتَ تعلمُ النظر فاسرُده ، وإنْ كنتَ لا تعلمه فاسمعه ، ويُسرَدُ في ساعةٍ عليهِ ؛ فإِنْ آمَنَ تُحقَّقَ استرشادُهُ ، وإن أبى تبيَّنَ عنادُهُ ، فوجبَ استخراجه منه بالسيف أو يموت
Your consideration [extends] to a limit that the distance becomes long thereby, or do you estimate it by a measure and thus judge it without text? The answer: We say: As for the statement that faith is obligatory before knowledge, it is weak; because obligating affirmation of what one does not know the soundness of leads to equating the prophet and the pretender, and that one believes first then reflects; if the truth becomes clear to him he persists, and if falsehood becomes clear to him he returns and has indeed believed in disbelief. And as for when the one asked for faith requests time for reflection, it is said to him: If you know how to reflect, then do so, and if you do not know it, then listen to it, and it will be recited to him within an hour; if he believes, his seeking guidance is realized, and if he refuses, his obstinacy becomes clear, so it is obligatory to extract it from him by the sword or he dies.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer format. It begins with an objection that asks whether one is setting an arbitrary limit or judging without scriptural evidence. The author then gives the answer. He states that the claim that faith (iman) is obligatory before knowledge (ma'rifa) is weak. The reason given is that requiring belief in something whose correctness is unknown leads to equating a true prophet with a false pretender (al-mutanabbi). Furthermore, if someone believes first and then later investigates, if they discover the truth they continue, but if they discover falsehood they would have to recant after having already held belief (which is a state of disbelief). The author then addresses the case where a person asked to believe requests time for investigation (nazar). The proper response is: if he knows how to investigate, let him do so; if not, let him listen to the demonstration. It is presented to him in a short time. If he then believes, his sincerity is confirmed; if he refuses, his stubbornness is clear, and the sword must be used to compel him, or he dies. This passage defends the necessity of rational investigation before faith and presents practical jurisprudence for dealing with those who delay.

Key Terms
إيمان
معرفة
نظر
متنبي
سيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that faith can be imposed without understanding.
Correction: This passage shows that imposing faith without knowledge is logically and theologically invalid because it fails to distinguish true from false and can lead to believing in falsehood.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the claim that faith must precede knowledge considered weak?
A) Because it contradicts the Quran.
B) Because it leads to equating the prophet with a pretender and forces belief in something not yet known to be true.
C) Because it is impossible to have faith without knowledge.
D) Because it was rejected by early Muslims.

Key Takeaway
Faith must be based on knowledge, not blind imposition; rational inquiry is necessary to distinguish true belief from falsehood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief about your religion simply because you were raised with it, without ever critically examining its proofs? In light of this lesson's warning that faith without knowledge leads to equating truth and falsehood, how does that sit with you now? What step will you take to replace blind custom with informed conviction?
?
The passage offers a procedure: if you know how to reflect, do so; if not, listen to the demonstration. Are you truly seeking knowledge to ground your faith, or are you avoiding reflection out of comfort or fear? Can you identify one area of your faith that you have not subjected to rigorous inquiry, and commit to exploring it this week?
?
If someone presented you with a clear, rational proof for the truth of Islam, would you accept it? Or would you, like the obstinate in the passage, refuse due to pride or stubbornness? Examine your heart: is there any part of your submission that is resistant, and how does that affect your standing before God?

The Twenty Necessary Attributes: Scope and Limitation
Sanusi introduces the twenty necessary attributes and explains they are not exhaustive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the twenty necessary attributes are not all of Allah's attributes, but only those accessible to human reason, and that we are not responsible for knowing what is not supported by proof.

Core Idea
The twenty attributes are a sample, not the full set of Allah's perfections.

Sanusi begins by listing twenty necessary attributes of Allah, but immediately warns us that these are not exhaustive. He uses the word 'min' (from/among) to indicate that these are only a part of His infinite perfections. This lesson clarifies the scope of our knowledge and responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall meaning. Notice the opening phrase including 'min' and the footnote that expands on the idea.
The footnote is part of the original text.
2
Understand the significance of 'min'
The word 'min' here indicates partiality (tab'idiyya). It shows that the twenty attributes are only a part of the necessary attributes, not all of them.
3
Grasp the principle of no accountability without proof
The passage states that we are not held accountable for what no rational or transmitted proof has established. This is important for the scope of our knowledge.
This principle is a mercy from Allah.
4
Reflect on the limitation of human perception
The footnote explains that the twenty attributes are what human powers have reached; beyond that, Allah's perfections are infinite and not encompassed by us.

الصفات الواجبة الله تعالیٰ
فَمِمَّا يَجِبُ لِمَوْلانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ : عِشْرُونَ صِفَةٌ
أشارَ بـ ( مِنْ ( التبعيضيَّةِ إلى أنَّ صفاتِ مولانا جل وعزَّ الواجبة لهُ لا تنحصر في هذه العشرينَ ؛ إذْ كمالاتُهُ تعالى لا نهاية لها ، لكنَّ العجز عن معرفة ما لم يُنصَبْ عليهِ دليل عقلي ولا نقلى لا نُوَاخَذُ بِهِ بفضل الله تعالى(۱)
(۱) عبارة بعضهم : وهي ـ أي : العشرون صفة ما انتهى إليه إدراك القوى البشرية ، وإلا فصفات كمالاته ونعوت جلاله مما يفوت العدَّ ، ولا يحيط به الحد ، لكنَّا لسنا مكلفين بما لم ينصب عليه دليلاً يوصلنا إليه . « ياسين » ( ق ١٠٧ ) ، وكذا سيذكر المصنف ( ص ١٦٨ ( مثل هذا عند حديثه عن الكلام ، وانظر تقسيم هذه الصفات في ( شرح المقدمات » ( ص ٢٣٥ ) .

الصفة النفسية
معناه ظاهر (۱) ، وفي عدّ الوجودِ صفةً على مذهب الشيخ الأشعري تسامح (۲) ؛ لأنَّهُ عندَهُ عينُ الذاتِ ، ليسَ بزائدِ عليها ، والذات ليسَتْ
(۱) لأنه بديهي التصور ، ومعناه : التحقق والثبوت في الخارج ، وهو صفة نفسية ، ومعنى الصفة النفسية كما قال إمام الحرمين في « الإرشاد » ( ص ۳۰ ) : ( كل صفةِ إثباتٍ لنفس ، لازمةٍ ما بقيت النفس ، غير معللة بعلل قائمة بالموصوف ) ، ولما عجزت العقول عن تصور ذاته تعالى عجزاً لا مطمع لرفعه .. بقي لها من إدراك صفات النفس له سبحانه صفة الوجود ، مع إقرارها أن وجوده تعالى ليس كوجودنا ، وفي كلام إمام الحرمين في « أجوبته لأسئلة الإمام الصقلي » ( ص ٥١ ) ما يوحي بأن عدم التحيز لذاته تعالى من صفاته النفسية ، ولكن قال بعدها ( ص ٥٥ ) : ( الجهل بالصفة النفسية إن صح رجوع صفة النفس إلى غير الوجود . . . ) ، فالتحقيق أن عدم التحيز راجع للصفات السلبية عند التفصيل ، ولكن نبَّه العلامة السكتاني في ( حاشيته على شرح العقيدة الصغرى » ( ق ٤۳ ( على أنه قيل : إن السلوب تسمى حالاً نفسية أو صفة نفسية (۲) وهو مذهب إمام الحرمين أيضاً ، قال في الإرشاد » ( ص ۳۱ ) : ( والوجه « المرضي : ألا يعد الوجود من الصفات ؛ فإن الوجود نفس الذات ) ، ثم قال : ) والأئمة رضي الله عنهم متوسعون في عد الوجود من الصفات ، والعلم به علم بالذات ) .
The necessary attributes of Allah, may He be exalted. Among that which is necessary for our Master, may He be glorified and exalted: twenty attributes. He indicated by (min) of partiality that the attributes of our Master, may He be glorified and exalted, necessary for Him, are not confined to these twenty; for His perfections, may He be exalted, have no end, but inability to know what no rational or transmitted proof has been established for is not held against us, by the grace of Allah, may He be exalted. (1) The expression of some of them: they are—that is, the twenty attributes—what the perception of human powers has reached, otherwise the attributes of His perfections and the qualities of His majesty are beyond counting, and no limit encompasses them, but we are not obligated with what no proof has been established to lead us to it. 'Yasin' (q 107), and likewise the author will mention (p 168) similar to this when he discusses speech, and see the division of these attributes in 'Sharh al-Muqaddimat' (p 235). And it is existence. The self-attribute. Its meaning is apparent (1), and counting existence as an attribute according to the school of Shaykh al-Ash'ari is leniency (2); because according to him it is identical to the essence, not additional to it, and the essence is not (1) Because it is self-evident in conception, and its meaning: actuality and stability in the external, and it is a self-attribute, and the meaning of self-attribute as Imam al-Haramayn said in 'al-Irshad' (p 30): 'Every attribute of affirmation for a self, necessary as long as the self remains, not caused by causes subsisting in the described.' And when the intellects were unable to conceive of His essence, may He be exalted, an incapacity with no hope of removal... there remained for them, from the perception of His self-attributes, may He be glorified, the attribute of existence, with their acknowledgment that His existence, may He be exalted, is not like our existence. And in the words of Imam al-Haramayn in his 'Answers to the Questions of Imam al-Siqilli' (p 51) is something suggesting that non-spatiality for His essence, may He be exalted, is among His self-attributes, but he said after it (p 55): 'Ignorance of the self-attribute if it is correct that the self-attribute returns to other than existence...' So the verified position is that non-spatiality refers to the negative attributes in detail, but the scholar al-Sakatani pointed out in his 'Hashiya on Sharh al-`Aqida al-Sughra' (q 43) that it is said: the negations are called a self-state or self-attribute (2) and it is also the school of Imam al-Haramayn. He said in 'al-Irshad' (p 31): 'The acceptable view is that existence should not be counted among the attributes; for existence is the essence itself.' Then he said: 'And the imams, may Allah be pleased with them, are lenient in counting existence among the attributes, and knowledge of it is knowledge of the essence.'
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage begins by introducing the twenty necessary attributes of Allah, but immediately clarifies that these are not exhaustive; they represent the limit of human perception. The author uses the phrase 'min at-tab`iḍiyya' (of partiality) to indicate that the twenty are only a part of Allah's infinite perfections. A footnote expands on this, stating that the twenty are what human powers have reached, and we are not responsible for knowing what no proof (rational or transmitted) has led to. The passage then turns to the first attribute: existence (al-wujud). It notes that counting existence as an attribute is a leniency in the Ash'ari school, because according to Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, existence is identical to the essence, not additional. The text then delves into the concept of a 'self-attribute' (ṣifa nafsiyya), explaining that it is self-evident, means actuality (taḥaqquq) and stability (thubūt) in the external world. It quotes Imam al-Haramayn's definition: a self-attribute is an affirmative quality necessary for the self as long as the self remains, not caused by causes subsisting in the described. The discussion then addresses the limitation of human intellect in conceiving Allah's essence, but the intellect can grasp the attribute of existence while acknowledging His existence is unlike ours. A side note mentions a debate about whether non-spatiality (عدم التحيز) is a self-attribute or a negative attribute, concluding that it is properly a negative attribute. The passage ends by citing Imam al-Haramayn's preferred view that existence should not be counted as an attribute since it is the essence itself, but he notes that the imams are lenient in doing so, and knowledge of existence is knowledge of the essence.

التبعضيَّة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The twenty necessary attributes are all of Allah's attributes.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that these twenty are not exhaustive; Allah's perfections are infinite and these are only what human reason can reach.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are we not held accountable for knowing attributes beyond the twenty?
A) Because they are not necessary
B) Because no rational or transmitted proof has been established for them
C) Because they are negative attributes
D) Because the twenty are sufficient

Key Takeaway
The twenty attributes are a limited set; Allah's perfections are infinite and we only know what proof has revealed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The First Necessary Attribute: Existence (al-Wujud)
Sanusi defines existence as a self-attribute and explains the Ash'ari stance that it is identical to the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of existence as a self-attribute, its meaning, and the Ash'ari position that existence is identical to the essence.

Core Idea
Existence is not an added property; it is the reality of Allah's essence itself.

Sanusi now discusses the first of the twenty necessary attributes: existence. He explains that in the Ash'ari school, existence is considered a 'self-attribute' and is actually identical to the essence. This lesson unpacks the meaning of self-attribute, the debate about counting existence as an attribute, and related discussions about negativity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first attribute and its classification
Read the passage to see that Sanusi identifies existence as the first attribute and calls it a self-attribute (ṣifa nafsiyya).
2
Understand the Ash'ari view on existence
Notice that counting existence as an attribute is considered leniency (tasamuh) because according to al-Ash'ari, existence is identical to the essence, not additional to it.
3
Grasp the definition of self-attribute
The passage quotes Imam al-Haramayn's definition: an affirmative attribute necessary for the self, not caused by other attributes. Also note that the intellect cannot fully know Allah's essence but can affirm existence.
4
Explore the debate on non-spatiality and conclusion
The passage discusses whether non-spatiality is a self-attribute or negative attribute, concluding it is negative. It ends with Imam al-Haramayn's preferred view not to count existence as an attribute, but scholars allow it for convenience.

The necessary attributes of Allah, may He be exalted. Among that which is necessary for our Master, may He be glorified and exalted: twenty attributes. He indicated by (min) of partiality that the attributes of our Master, may He be glorified and exalted, necessary for Him, are not confined to these twenty; for His perfections, may He be exalted, have no end, but inability to know what no rational or transmitted proof has been established for is not held against us, by the grace of Allah, may He be exalted. The self-attribute. Its meaning is apparent (1), and counting existence as an attribute according to the school of Shaykh al-Ash'ari is leniency (2); because according to him it is identical to the essence, not additional to it, and the essence is not (1) Because it is self-evident in conception, and its meaning: actuality and stability in the external, and it is a self-attribute, and the meaning of self-attribute as Imam al-Haramayn said in 'al-Irshad' (p 30): 'Every attribute of affirmation for a self, necessary as long as the self remains, not caused by causes subsisting in the described.' He said in 'al-Irshad' (p 31): 'The acceptable view is that existence should not be counted among the attributes; for existence is the essence itself.' Then he said: 'And the imams, may Allah be pleased with them, are lenient in counting existence among the attributes, and knowledge of it is knowledge of the essence.'

Commentary
This passage begins by introducing the twenty necessary attributes of Allah, but immediately clarifies that these are not exhaustive; they represent the limit of human perception. The author uses the phrase 'min at-tab`iḍiyya' (of partiality) to indicate that the twenty are only a part of Allah's infinite perfections. A footnote expands on this, stating that the twenty are what human powers have reached, and we are not responsible for knowing what no proof (rational or transmitted) has led to. The passage then turns to the first attribute: existence (al-wujud). The text then delves into the concept of a 'self-attribute' (ṣifa nafsiyya), explaining that it is self-evident, means actuality (taḥaqquq) and stability (thubūt) in the external world. It quotes Imam al-Haramayn's definition: a self-attribute is an affirmative quality necessary for the self as long as the self remains, not caused by causes subsisting in the described. The discussion then addresses the limitation of human intellect in conceiving Allah's essence, but the intellect can grasp the attribute of existence while acknowledging His existence is unlike ours. A side note mentions a debate about whether non-spatiality (عدم التحيز) is a self-attribute or a negative attribute, concluding that it is properly a negative attribute. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's existence is identical to His essence and your intellect cannot grasp His essence, how will you humbly affirm His existence without demanding to understand 'how'? What concrete step can you take today to worship Allah based on submission rather than comprehension?
?
The scholars count existence as an attribute for pedagogical convenience, though they know it is the essence. Are you willing to accept theological simplifications in your own faith, or do you insist on the exact truth? Where else in your beliefs might you be settling for convenience over precision?
?
This lesson reminds you that your intellect is incapable of fully knowing Allah. When was the last time you felt arrogant about your understanding of Allah? What can you do today to replace that arrogance with humble awe at His unfathomable essence?
