The Rational Proof for Allah's Self-Subsistence
Sanusi defines qiyam bi nafs and proves it rationally using the concept of mukhassis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for Allah's self-subsistence (qiyam bi nafs) and why only Allah can be truly self-sufficient.

Core Idea
Only Allah is absolutely self-sufficient; everything else needs a determiner.

This lesson explores Sanusi's proof that Allah is self-subsistent (qiyam bi nafs). We'll see how the concepts of substrate (mahall) and determiner (mukhassis) help establish that Allah alone is absolutely self-sufficient. The passage clarifies why even bodies that are independent of a substrate still need a creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical structure and key terms.
Pay attention to the definitions of mahall and mukhassis.
2
Understand the key principle
Identify the principle: only what admits non-existence needs a mukhassis. Since Allah does not admit non-existence, He needs no mukhassis.
3
Follow the two implications
See how the lack of need for a substrate implies Allah is an essence, not an attribute. The lack of need for an agent implies His essence is unlike other essences.
Use the definitions given.
4
Grasp the final conclusion
The passage concludes that qiyam bi nafs is absolute self-sufficiency, belonging only to Allah.
This is the main takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبقاء لذاتِهِ تعالى ولجميع صفاتِهِ ، وإنَّما يحتاج إلى المخصص - أي : الفاعل - مَنْ يقبلُ العدمَ ، ومولانا جل وعزَّ لا يقبلُه ، فإذا يستحيل على مولانا جل وعزَّ الافتقار عموماً
وبهذا تعرفُ : أنَّ مرادَنا بالمحل في العقيدة الذاتُ ، ومرادَنا بالمخصص الفاعلُ ؛ فبعدم افتقاره تعالى إلى محل - أي : ذاتِ أخرى - لزمَ أَنَّهُ جلَّ وعزَّ ذات لا صفةٌ ، و بعدم افتقاره تعالى إلى مخصص - أي : فاعل - لزمَ أَنَّ ذاتَهُ العليَّةَ جَلَّ وعزَّ ليسَتْ كسائر الذوات التي لا تفتقرُ هي أيضاً إلى محل ؛ كالأجرام مثلاً ؛ لأنَّ هذهِ وإنْ كانَتْ مستغنيةً عن المحل - أي : عن ذات تقوم بها - فهي مفتقرة ابتداءً ودواماً افتقاراً ضرورياً لازماً إلى المخصص ؛ أي : الفاعل ؛ وهو مولانا جل وعزَّ .
فإذا ؛ القيام بالنفس : هو عبارةٌ عن الغنى المطلق ، وذلكَ لا يمكنُ أنْ يكونَ إلا لمولانا تبارك وتعالى
And eternity belongs to His essence, may He be exalted, and to all His attributes. Rather, only that which admits non-existence needs the mukhassis (determiner), i.e., the agent; and our Lord, may He be glorified and exalted, does not admit it. Thus, it is impossible for our Lord, may He be glorified and exalted, to be in need generally. And by this you know: that our intended meaning by 'al-mahall' (substrate) in the creed is the essence (dhat), and our intended meaning by 'al-mukhassis' (determiner) is the agent (fa'il). So by His lack of need, may He be exalted, for a substrate – i.e., another essence – it necessarily follows that He, may He be glorified and exalted, is an essence, not an attribute. And by His lack of need, may He be exalted, for a mukhassis – i.e., an agent – it necessarily follows that His sublime essence, may He be glorified and exalted, is not like other essences which also are not in need of a substrate, such as bodies for example; because these, even though they are independent of a substrate – i.e., of an essence in which they subsist – yet they are in need, initially and perpetually, with a necessary and inherent need, of the mukhassis – i.e., the agent – and He is our Lord, may He be glorified and exalted. Therefore, al-qiyam bi nafs (self-subsistence) is an expression of absolute self-sufficiency, and that cannot be except for our Lord, Blessed and Most High.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof for the attribute of self-subsistence (qiyam bi nafs) for Allah. The author begins by stating that eternity belongs to Allah's essence and all His attributes. Then he establishes a key principle: only that which admits non-existence requires a mukhassis (determiner/agent). Since Allah does not admit non-existence, He has no need of a mukhassis at all. Next, the author defines two important terms for the creed: 'al-mahall' (substrate) means essence (dhat), and 'al-mukhassis' means agent (fa'il). Using these definitions, he argues that because Allah does not need a substrate, He is an essence and not an attribute. Because He does not need an agent, His essence is not like other essences that are independent of a substrate (e.g., bodies) but still need an agent initially and perpetually. The author then concludes: qiyam bi nafs is absolute self-sufficiency, and it belongs solely to Allah.

Key Terms
المخصص
المحل
القيام بالنفس
الغنى المطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think qiyam bi nafs can be attributed to beings like bodies because they are independent of a substrate.
Correction: The author clarifies that bodies still need a mukhassis (agent) initially and perpetually, so only Allah is truly self-subsistent.

Checkpoint

A) Because He is eternal and does not admit non-existence
B) Because He is independent of a substrate
C) Because He is not an attribute
D) Because He is self-sufficient

Key Takeaway
Only Allah is absolutely self-sufficient; everything else depends on Him for its existence.

The Twenty Impossible Attributes and the Four Types of Opposition
Sanusi enumerates the twenty impossible attributes of God and classifies opposition into four logical types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the twenty impossible attributes are the linguistic opposites of the necessary attributes, and how opposition is classified into four logical types.

Core Idea
The impossible attributes are the opposites of the necessary ones, and opposition has four logical forms.

In this passage, Sanusi completes the picture of divine attributes by presenting the twenty attributes that are impossible for God. He clarifies that 'opposite' here is a broad linguistic term, not just logical contradiction. He then explains why these opposites are impossible based on the nature of the Necessary Being, and finally introduces the logical classification of opposition into four types, which provides a framework for understanding how the impossible attributes relate to the necessary ones.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overall sense of the argument. Notice that it begins with a list, then a definition, then a reasoning, then a classification.
The passage is dense but logically structured.
2
Understand the definition of 'opposite'
Sanusi specifies that 'opposite' here is the linguistic opposite, which includes both existential and non-existential contraries. This is broader than mere logical contradiction. Understand why this breadth is needed.
This definition is key to avoiding misunderstanding.
3
Identify the four types of opposition
Find and memorize the four types: contradiction, privation/possession, contraries, and correlatives. Think of an example for each from everyday reasoning.
These types come from classical logic and are used in kalam arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِمَّا يَسْتَحِيلُ فِي حَقِّهِ تَعَالَى : عِشْرُونَ صِفَةٌ ; وَهِيَ أَضْدَادُ الْعِشْرِينَ الْأُولَى. واعلم أن الضد هنا : الضد اللغوي ; وهو كل مناف، سواء كان وجودياً أو عدمياً، فكأنه يقول : يستحيل في حقه تعالى كل ما ينافي صفة من الصفات الأولى ; لأن الصفات الأولى لما تقرر وجوبها له تعالى عقلاً وشرعاً، وقد عرفت أن حقيقة الواجب : ما لا يتصور في العقل عدمه .. لزم ألا يقبل جل وعز الاتصاف بما ينافي شيئاً منها. وأنواع المنافاة على ما تقرر في المنطق أربعة : تنافي النقيضين وتنافي العدم والملكة، وتنافي الضدين، وتنافي المتضايفين.
And among that which is impossible with respect to Him, exalted is He: twenty attributes; and they are the opposites of the twenty first. And know that 'opposite' here is the linguistic opposite; and it is everything that is contrary, whether it is existential or non-existential. So it is as if He says: It is impossible with respect to Him, exalted, everything that contradicts any of the first attributes; because the first attributes, once their necessity for Him, exalted, was established by reason and revelation, and you have known that the reality of the Necessary is that whose non-existence is inconceivable in the mind... it follows that He, majestic and exalted, does not accept being described with anything that contradicts any of them. And the types of opposition, as established in logic, are four: opposition of contradictories, opposition of privation and possession, opposition of contraries, and opposition of correlatives.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage opens by stating that the attributes which are impossible for God are twenty in number, and these are the opposites (أضداد) of the twenty necessary attributes mentioned earlier. The author clarifies that 'opposite' here is the linguistic opposite (الضد اللغوي), meaning anything that is contrary, whether it is a positive quality or a negation. The reasoning is that since the necessary attributes are established as necessarily true for God by both reason and revelation, and the Necessary Being is that whose non-existence cannot be conceived, He cannot possess any attribute that contradicts any of those necessary attributes. Then the author introduces the logical classification of opposition (المنافاة) into four types as established in logic: contradiction (تنافي النقيضين), privation and possession (تنافي العدم والملكة), contraries (تنافي الضدين), and correlatives (تنافي المتضايفين). This classification provides the framework for understanding how the impossible attributes relate to the necessary ones.

Misconception: Some may think 'opposite' here means only logical contradiction, such as existence vs. non-existence.
Correction: Sanusi specifies that 'opposite' here is the linguistic opposite, which includes both existential and non-existential contraries, so it is broader than just logical contradiction.

Opposition of Contradictories
Sanusi defines opposition of contradictories and notes the impossibility of both terms being absent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of opposition of contradictories and why both sides cannot be simultaneously absent.

Core Idea
Contradictories cannot both be false; one must be true.

This passage introduces the first type of opposition, that of contradictories. This is a fundamental logical concept used throughout Islamic theology. The author defines what makes a pair contradictory and highlights a key difference from other oppositions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the concise definition and the example of motion.
The author uses a very compressed style.
2
Identify the definition of contradictories
Find the phrase that defines what contradictories are: the affirmation of a thing and its negation. Contrast this with other types of opposition.
3
Understand the unique rule
The passage states that for contradictories, both sides cannot be absent simultaneously. Reflect on why this is: one must be true and the other false. Consider examples like 'motion exists' vs 'motion does not exist'.
This distinguishes contradictories from contraries or privation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما تنافي النقيضين : فهما ثبوت أمر ونفيه ; كثبوت الحركة ونفيها. ولا يمكن أيضاً ارتفاع الطرفين بالنسبة للنقيضين، وأما بالنسبة لغيرهما فيمكن ارتفاعهما.
As for the opposition of contradictories: they are the affirmation of a thing and its negation; like the affirmation of motion and its negation. And it is also not possible for the two sides to be simultaneously absent with respect to contradictories; but with respect to others, their simultaneous absence is possible.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage defines the first type of opposition, known as contradiction (تنافي النقيضين). Contradictories are a pair consisting of the affirmation of a thing and its negation, such as 'motion exists' and 'motion does not exist'. The key characteristic of contradictories is that they cannot both be true together nor can they both be false together; one must be true and the other false. This is what the author means by saying it is not possible for both sides to be absent. In other types of opposition, such as privation and possession or contraries, both sides can be false simultaneously (e.g., a wall is neither blind nor seeing). This distinction is crucial for understanding the logical relationships between attributes.

Key Terms
تنافي النقيضين
ثبوت
نفي
ارتفاع الطرفين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the unique characteristic of contradictories (النقيضين) compared to other types of opposition?
A) Both sides can be true.
B) Both sides cannot be simultaneously absent.
C) One side is always a privation.
D) They are both existential.

Key Takeaway
The opposition of contradictories requires that one of the two statements be true and the other false; they cannot both be false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily conversations, do you often speak in vague terms to avoid committing to a clear truth or falsehood? How does the logical impossibility of both absence and presence in a contradictory challenge your tendency to be non-committal? Will you commit to speaking more clearly even when it costs you comfort?
?
Consider an area of your faith where you have been avoiding a clear affirmation or denial—like a doubt you haven't faced. The principle of contradictories shows that either the truth is affirmed or it is not; there is no safe middle. What step will you take today to resolve that uncertainty and affirm what is true?
?
Think of a time when you accepted a false statement as possibly true because you thought 'both sides could be wrong.' Did that excuse allow you to avoid taking a stand? How will you apply the lesson that for contradictories, one must be true, to guard your mind and heart from complacency in error?

The Second Type: Opposition of Privation and Possession
Sanusi defines privation and possession and distinguishes it from contradiction by the subject's capacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of privation and possession and its distinction from contradiction based on the subject's natural capacity.

Core Idea
Privation is not mere absence; it is absence where possession is naturally expected.

Sanusi now defines the second type of opposition: privation and possession. Unlike contradiction, which negates without restriction, this type requires that the subject be naturally capable of possessing the quality. This distinction is crucial for precise theological and logical reasoning. Let's read his careful definition and example.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to hear the author's rhythm, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: definition, example, contrast.
The passage is about 6 lines of Arabic; take your time.
2
Understand the definition
Privation and possession are defined as the affirmation of a thing and its negation (i.e., the quality and its absence) in that which is disposed to be described by it. Key: the quality (possession) is existential; its absence is privation only if the subject could naturally have it.
Sanusi says 'ثبوت أمر ونفيه عما من شأنه أن يتصف به'.
3
Analyze the example
Sight (possession) and blindness (privation). Blindness is not just lack of sight; it is lack of sight in a being that normally sees. A wall cannot be called blind because it is not disposed to sight.
This clarifies the condition of natural capacity.
4
Contrast with contradiction
In contradiction, negation is unrestricted. For example, 'the wall is not seeing' is a true negation, but it is not privation because the wall lacks the capacity. In privation, negation is restricted to subjects that can possess the quality.
This distinction is the key takeaway.
5
Reflect on the importance
This concept prevents misattribution: we only speak of 'blindness' where sight is possible. In theology, this helps avoid false analogies about divine attributes.
Consider how this applies to discussions about God: we do not say God is 'blind' because He is not subject to sight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العدم والملكة : فهما ثبوت أمر ونفيه عما من شأنه أن يتصف به ; كالبصر والعمى مثلا، فالبصر وجودي وهو الملكة، والعمى نفيه عما من شأنه أن يتصف به، ولهذا لا يقال في الحائط: أعمى ; لأنه ليس من شأنه أن يتصف بالنظر عادة. وبهذا فارق هذا النوع النقيضين ; فإن كلا من النوعين وإن كان هو ثبوت أمر ونفيه، لكن النفي في تقابل العدم والملكة مقيد بنفي الملكة عما من شأنه أن يتصف بها، وفي النقيضين لا يتقيد بذلك.
As for privation and possession: they are the affirmation of a thing and its negation in that which is disposed to be described by it; like sight and blindness for example. Sight is existential and it is possession, and blindness is its negation in that which is disposed to be described by it. For this reason, one does not say of a wall 'blind', because it is not in its nature to be described with sight usually. And by this this type differs from contradictories; for each of the two types, although it is the affirmation of a thing and its negation, however the negation in the opposition of privation and possession is restricted to the negation of possession from that which is disposed to be described by it, while in contradictories it is not restricted by that.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of opposition, privation and possession (العدم والملكة). Privation is not merely the absence of a quality; it is the absence of a quality from a subject that is naturally capable of possessing that quality. For example, blindness is the lack of sight in a being that normally has sight, like a human. A wall is not called blind because it is not capable of sight. This is what distinguishes it from contradiction: in contradiction, the negation is not restricted to subjects that are disposed to possess the quality. For example, the negation of motion can be applied to anything, whether it can have motion or not. The author emphasizes that the 'possession' (الملكة) is the existential quality that a subject may have, and 'privation' is the absence of that quality from a subject that would normally have it. The condition of the subject's natural capacity is crucial.

Key Terms
العدم والملكة
الملكة
العمى
ثبوت
نفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think privation is simply the absence of any quality, without considering the subject's capacity.
Correction: Privation specifically requires that the subject be naturally disposed to possess the quality. A wall lacks sight, but it is not blind because it has no capacity for sight.

Checkpoint
Why is a wall not called 'blind' even though it lacks sight?
A) Because it has no eyes.
B) Because it is not disposed to possess sight.
C) Because blindness is an existential attribute.
D) Because sight is not a possession.

Key Takeaway
Privation and possession require the subject's natural capacity for the possession; without that capacity, the opposition is not of this type.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Allah created you with a natural disposition (fitrah) to recognize His signs and worship Him. Reflect: have you allowed a 'privation' to set in where you no longer feel the grief of neglecting that capacity? What would it take for you to re-awaken that possession today?
?
The example of the wall reminds us that we only call something blind if it was meant to see. Are there sins or habits you have normalized to the point that you no longer see them as lacks in your soul? Ask Allah to restore your natural spiritual vision and to make you grieve over what you have lost.
?
Sanusi uses sight as a material example. But think of a spiritual 'possession' like khushu‘ in prayer or love for the Quran. When did you last feel its absence? If you feel nothing, you may have become like the wall—no longer disposed. What concrete step will you take today to rebuild that capacity?

Definition of Khilafan and Mutadhaifan
Sanusi defines khilafan and mutadhaifan with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of 'khilafan' and 'mutadhaifan' and the key concept of 'utmost opposition' (ghayat al-khilaf).

Core Idea
Utmost opposition means two things cannot coexist in one subject.

In this passage, Sanusi lays the groundwork for understanding rational opposition by defining two key terms: al-khilafan (the two different things) and al-mutada'ifan (the two correlates). He introduces the crucial condition of 'utmost opposition' and clarifies what it means through examples. This foundational distinction is essential for later discussions in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the literal translation. Let the definitions and examples sink in.
2
Understand the definition of khilafan
Identify that khilafan are two existent matters with utmost opposition, meaning they cannot coexist. Note the example of whiteness and blackness.
Contrast with whiteness and motion, which can coexist.
3
Understand the definition of mutadhaifan
Note that mutadhaifan have utmost opposition but also interdependence of intelligibility. The example is fatherhood and sonship.
Clarify that 'existence' here is mental, not external.
4
Reflect on the examples
Think of your own examples of things that are in utmost opposition and things that are correlates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تتوقَّفُ عقليَّةُ أحدهما على عقليَّةِ الآخرِ، مثالهما: البياضُ والسواد، ومرادنا بغاية الخلافِ: التنافي بينهما؛ بحيثُ لا يصح اجتماعهما، واحترزَ بذلكَ مِنَ البياضِ مع الحركة مثلاً؛ فإنَّهما أمرانِ وجوديَّانِ مختلفان في الحقيقة، لكن ليس بينهما غايةُ الخلافِ التي هي التنافي؛ لصحة اجتماعِهما؛ إذ يمكنُ أنْ يكونَ المحل الواحد متحركاً أبيض. وأما المتضايفان: فهما الأمرانِ الوجوديَّانِ اللذان بينهما غايةٌ الخلافِ، وتتوقَّفُ عقليَّةُ أحدهما على عقليَّةِ الآخرِ؛ كالأبوَّةِ والبنوةِ مثلاً، والمراد بالوجودِ في المتضايفينِ: أَنَّ كلاً منهما ليس معناه عدمَ كذا، لا أنَّهما موجودان في الخارج عنِ الذهنِ؛ إِذْ مِنَ المعلوم عندَ المحققينَ: أنَّ الأبوَّةَ والبنوَّةَ أمرانِ اعتباريان لا وجود لهما في الخارج عن الذهن. أقسام المنافاة عند الأصوليين: وأهلُ الأصولِ يجعلون أقسام المنافاة اثنين: تنافي النقيضين، وتنافي الضدَّينِ، ويجعلون العدم والملكة داخلين في النقيضين، والمتضايفين داخلين في الضدين.
And the intelligibility of one does not depend on the intelligibility of the other; their example is whiteness and blackness. Our meaning by 'utmost opposition' is mutual repugnancy between them such that their combination is not valid. By that [expression] he guarded against whiteness with motion, for example; for they are two existent matters different in reality, but there is not between them utmost opposition which is mutual repugnancy, due to the validity of their combination, since it is possible for one subject to be moving and white. As for the two correlates (al-mutada'ifan), they are the two existent matters between which there is utmost opposition, and the intelligibility of one depends on the intelligibility of the other, such as fatherhood and sonship, for example. And what is meant by 'existence' in the two correlates is that each of them is not a meaning of non-existence of such-and-such, not that they are existent in the external [world] outside the mind; for it is known to the verifiers (al-muhaqqiqun) that fatherhood and sonship are mental considerations (i'tibariyan) which have no existence in the external [world] outside the mind. Sections of opposition according to the usuliyyun: And the scholars of usul make the sections of opposition two: opposition of contradictories (naqidayn) and opposition of contraries (diddayn), and they make non-existence and capacity (al-'adam wa al-mulka) included within the contradictories, and the two correlates included within the contraries.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage defines two types of opposition: al-khilafan (the two different things) and al-mutada'ifan (the two correlates). The author begins by defining 'ghayat al-khilaf' (utmost opposition) as mutual repugnancy that prevents coexistence. He gives whiteness and blackness as an example of two things that cannot coexist in one subject. He contrasts this with whiteness and motion, which can coexist in one subject (e.g., a moving white object). Next, he defines the two correlates: they are two existent matters between which there is utmost opposition, but with the additional condition that the intelligibility of one depends on the intelligibility of the other, like fatherhood and sonship. He clarifies that by 'existent' he does not mean external reality; rather, these are mental considerations (i'tibariyat) according to the verifiers. Finally, he states that the scholars of usul divide opposition into two types: opposition of contradictories (which includes non-existence and capacity) and opposition of contraries (which includes the two correlates). This is the foundation for the subsequent classification.

Key Terms
الخلافان
المتضايفان
غاية الخلاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any two different things are in utmost opposition.
Correction: The passage distinguishes between mere difference and true mutual exclusivity; whiteness and motion differ but can coexist.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes al-mutada'ifan from al-khilafan according to the passage?
A) They cannot coexist.
B) The intelligibility of one depends on the other.
C) They are both non-existent.
D) They are both external existents.

Key Takeaway
Khilafan and mutadhaifan are two types of opposition distinguished by whether the intelligibility of one depends on the other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of your relationship with your parents, do you instinctively treat 'fatherhood' or 'motherhood' as something physically real? The passage teaches that such relations are mental considerations. How does this truth affect your gratitude toward your parents, knowing that their roles are not fixed external facts but meanings carried in hearts and minds? Are you willing to consciously renew that meaning daily?
?
In your life, when you encounter two opposing ideas or people, do you automatically assume they cannot coexist? The passage shows that whiteness and motion can coexist without conflict. Think of a disagreement you currently have—is it a true opposition of mutual repugnancy, or simply a difference that can coexist? Will you take one concrete step today to separate real opposition from mere difference?
?
Sanusi says that fatherhood and sonship are mutual opposites yet their meanings depend on each other. Consider your own identity: Is there a part of your self-understanding that only exists in relation to someone else—like being a student, a child, or a friend? Does this lesson reveal to you that such identities are not just labels but are divinely structured meanings? How will you honor those relationships as more than social constructs?

Classification of Opposition According to the Usuliyyun
Sanusi states the usuliyyun's two types of opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of opposition recognized by the scholars of usul: opposition of contradictories (naqidayn) and opposition of contraries (diddayn), and how they include other categories.

Core Idea
Usuliyyun divide opposition into contradictories and contraries, with subcategories.

After defining the basic terms, Sanusi presents the classification of opposition used by the scholars of usul. They identify two main categories: contradictories (naqidayn) and contraries (diddayn). They further include non-existence and capacity under contradictories, and the two correlates under contraries. This classification systematizes the various forms of opposition discussed in logic and theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the short Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the classification.
2
Identify the two main categories
Spot that the usuliyyun divide opposition into opposition of contradictories and opposition of contraries.
3
Understand the inclusions
Note that 'adam wa mulka is included under contradictories, and mutadhaifan under contraries.
This shows how earlier definitions fit into this framework.
4
Reflect on the significance
Think about why this classification matters for kalam and logic.

The Fourfold Classification of Known Things
Sanusi derives the fourfold classification from the two types of opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four types of relations between two known things — similars, contraries, different things, and contradictories — and how they are defined by the criteria of coexistence and co-absence.

Core Idea
Opposition comes in four types, not just one.

This passage from Sanusi presents a foundational logical classification used in Islamic theology and jurisprudence. By analyzing whether two things can exist together or both be absent, we get four clear categories. Understanding these categories is essential for grasping arguments about divine attributes and created things.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before interpreting.
Notice how Sanusi uses a compressed style.
2
Identify the two criteria
The classification relies on whether two things can coexist (ijtima') and whether they can both be absent (irtifa'). Spot these terms in the Arabic.
3
Map the four categories
Based on the criteria, list the four categories: khilafan, naqidan, diddan, mithlan. Understand the logical conditions for each.
The passage derives them step by step.
4
Study the examples
Look at the examples for each category: speech/sitting, existence/non-existence, motion/rest, whiteness/whiteness. See how they fit the criteria.
5
Reflect on the importance
Think about why this classification matters in kalam and usul al-fiqh. It underlies many arguments about divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهذا يقولون: المعلومات منحصرةٌ في أربعة أقسام: المثلينِ، والضدين، والخلافين، والنقيضين؛ لأنَّ المعلومين إن أمكن اجتماعهما فهما الخلافانِ، وإن لم يمكن معَ ذلك ارتفاعُهما فهما النقيضانِ، وإن أمكن معَ ذلكَ ارتفاعُهما؛ فإما أن يختلفا في الحقيقة أم لا، فالأوَّلُ الضدانِ، والثاني المثلانِ. فخرجَ مِنْ هذا: أنَّ القسمَ الأَوَّلَ مِنْ هذه الأقسام الخلافانِ وهما يجتمعان ويرتفعانِ؛ كالكلام والقعود، والثاني النقيضان لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعانِ؛ كوجودِ زيد وعدمه، والثالث الضدانِ لا يجتمعان وقد يرتفعانِ؛ كالحركة والسكون؛ فإنَّهما لا يجتمعان وقد يرتفعان بعدم محلهما الذي هو الجرم، والرابع المثلان، لا يجتمعان وقد يرتفعان؛ كالبياض والبياض.
And because of this they say: known things are confined to four divisions: the two similars (mithlan), the two contraries (didan), the two different things (khilafan), and the two contradictories (naqidan). Because the two known things, if their combination is possible, then they are the two different things; if their removal (i.e., both being absent) is not possible along with that, then they are the two contradictories; if their removal is possible along with that, then either they differ in reality or not; the first are the two contraries, and the second are the two similars. Hence it emerges from this that the first of these divisions are the two different things: they combine and are removed (i.e., can both be absent) together, like speech and sitting. The second are the two contradictories: they neither combine nor are removed together, like the existence of Zayd and its non-existence. The third are the two contraries: they do not combine and yet may be removed together, like motion and rest; for they do not combine and may be removed together by the non-existence of their subject, which is the body. And the fourth are the two similars: they do not combine and may be removed together, like whiteness and whiteness.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents a logical classification of all known things into four categories based on two criteria: whether the two items can coexist (ijtima') and whether they can both be absent (irtifa'). The usuliyyun derive these four from the two types of opposition. They use the term 'ma'luman' (two known things) as the object of comparison. The reasoning: if they can coexist, they are 'khilafan' (different things). If they cannot coexist, then check if they can both be absent. If they cannot both be absent (i.e., one must always be present), they are 'naqidan' (contradictories), like existence and non-existence. If they can both be absent, then either they are different in reality or not. If they are different, they are 'diddan' (contraries), like motion and rest (which can both be absent if the body does not exist). If they are the same in reality (identical), they are 'mithlan' (similars), like whiteness and whiteness (which cannot coexist in one subject because they are identical, but can both be absent). The passage then gives examples for each. This classification is fundamental in logic and usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms
المعلومات
المثلان
الضدان
الخلافان
النقيضان
اجتماع
ارتفاع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All opposites are the same; they are all contradictories.
Correction: This passage shows that there are multiple types of opposition: different things, contradictories, contraries, and similars, each with different logical properties.

Checkpoint
Which of the following pairs are 'naqidan' (contradictories) according to this classification?
A) Whiteness and blackness
B) Motion and rest
C) Existence and non-existence
D) Speech and sitting

Key Takeaway
Known things are classified into four types based on whether they can coexist and whether they can both be absent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own beliefs, are there areas where you treat two options as the only possible contradictory opposites (like either/always or never) when actually there might be a middle ground (like they can both be absent)? How does recognizing the third category of contraries free you from false dichotomies?
?
This lesson shows that even things that are identical in reality can be opposites if they cannot coexist. In your life, have you ever experienced that two good things (like two different acts of worship) cannot be done at the same time? How do you choose between similars without denying the value of one?
?
The classification depends on precise logical criteria. Examine your own patterns of thinking: do you often confuse contradictories (cannot both be false) with contraries (can both be false)? How might this confusion lead you to judge others or situations wrongly? Make a concrete commitment to pause and analyze before assuming two options exhaust all possibilities.

The Impossibility of Two Similars Coexisting
Sanusi proves that two similars cannot coexist in a single substrate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that two similars cannot coexist in the same substrate and why this principle is fundamental in kalam.

Core Idea
Two similars cannot coexist because that would force two opposites together.

This passage presents a concise proof from the early kalam tradition against the possibility of two mutually alike qualities occupying the same locus. The argument draws on the logical relationship between a thing, its similar, and its opposite. Understanding this proof is essential for grasping later discussions on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the argument uses the exclusion of other possibilities.
2
Understand the logical chain
The proof assumes a substrate can only be qualified by a thing, its similar, or its opposite. Then it shows that accepting two similars leads to opposites coexisting.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Consider the implications
Reflect on why this principle matters for theology, especially for discussions about God's attributes.
It establishes a key rule about compatibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج أصحابنا على أن المثلين لا يجتمعان ؛ بأنَّ المحل لو قبل المثلين للزمَ أنْ يقبلَ الدَّينِ ؛ فإنَّ القابل للشيء : لا يخلو عنه ، أو مثله ، أو عن ضدّهِ ، فلو قَبِلَ المثلين لجازَ وجود أحدهما في المحل معَ انتفاء الآخرِ ، فيخلفه ضدُّه ، فيجتمع الضدانِ ، وهو محال
المتنافيات : ( فائدة : حصر المعلومات كلها في هذه الأربعة الأقسام حقٌّ ، لا يخرج منها شيء إلا ما توحد الله تعالى به وتفرد به ؛ فإنه ليس ضد الشيء ولا نقيضاً ولا مثلاً ولا خلافاً ؛ لتعذر الرفع ، وهذا حكم عام في ذاته تعالى وصفاته العُلا ؛ لتعدُّر رفعها بسبب وجوب وجودها ) .
Our companions argued that two similars cannot coexist: because if a substrate could accept two similars, it would necessarily accept two opposites; for that which accepts a thing is not free from it, or its similar, or its opposite. So if it accepted two similars, it would be possible for one of them to exist in the substrate while the other is absent, then its opposite would take its place, so the two opposites would coexist, and that is impossible. Contradictories: (Benefit: The classification of all known things into these four divisions is true; nothing falls outside them except what Allah the Exalted is unique and alone in; for He is neither an opposite of a thing, nor a contradictory, nor a similar, nor a different; because there is no possibility of raising [something against Him], and this is a general ruling regarding His Exalted Essence and His High Attributes, because it is impossible to raise [something against them] due to the necessity of their existence.)
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage contains two parts. First, a proof that two similars (مثلين) cannot coexist in the same substrate. The argument: if a substrate could accept two similars, it would necessarily accept two opposites (ضدين) because for any attribute, the substrate is either occupied by it, its similar, or its opposite. If two similars were possible, one could exist without the other, allowing the opposite to take its place, resulting in two opposites coexisting, which is impossible. Second, a benefit (فائدة) concerning the classification of all known things: they are either opposite (ضد), contradictory (نقيض), similar (مثل), or different (خلاف). However, Allah and His attributes are excluded from this classification because there is nothing that is opposite, contradictory, similar, or different to Him, since there is nothing like Him and none can be raised in opposition.

Key Terms
مثل
ضد
محل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that two similar qualities can occupy the same thing at once, like two instances of whiteness.
Correction: The proof shows that if two similars could coexist, then opposites could also coexist, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what would happen if a substrate could accept two similars?
A) The substrate would be empty.
B) The substrate would then accept two opposites.
C) The substrate would contain both similars simultaneously.
D) The proof does not say.

Key Takeaway
Two similars cannot coexist in the same substrate because that would logically entail the coexistence of opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a belief you hold about Allah that you have never questioned. Does this proof challenge you to examine whether that belief could lead to a logical contradiction? If so, how will you investigate it?
?
The proof shows that accepting two similars forces opposites to coexist. In your own life, when you try to reconcile two opposing views about Allah's attributes (e.g., mercy and justice), do you rely on rational consistency? Or do you accept contradictions? How might this proof guide you to a deeper understanding of tawheed?
?
Think of a time you assumed that a similar quality could be shared between created beings and the Creator (e.g., knowledge). How does the impossibility of two similars coexisting in one substrate refute that assumption? And what is the personal cost of failing to uphold this truth in your worship?

The Four Categories of All Created Things and Allah's Exemption
Sanusi classifies all things into four categories and exempts Allah and His attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that all known things fall into four categories (opposite, contradictory, similar, different) except Allah and His attributes, who are beyond such classification.

Core Idea
Everything is either opposite, contradictory, similar, or different — except Allah.

This benefit (فائدة) by the author classifies all conceivable things into four categories. However, Allah the Exalted and His attributes are exempt because there is nothing comparable to them. This classification is crucial for understanding the uniqueness of God in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories
Read the passage and note the four terms: ضد (opposite), نقيض (contradictory), مثل (similar), خلاف (different). Understand what each means.
These are technical terms in kalam logic.
2
Understand the exception for Allah
The passage explains why Allah and His attributes cannot be placed in any of these categories. Focus on the reason given.
It is due to the impossibility of raising something against Him (تعذر الرفع).
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this classification matters for tawhid. It underscores God's absolute uniqueness.
Nothing is like unto Him.

Our companions argued that two similars cannot coexist: because if a substrate could accept two similars, it would necessarily accept two opposites; for that which accepts a thing is not free from it, or its similar, or its opposite. So if it accepted two similars, it would be possible for one of them to exist in the substrate while the other is absent, then its opposite would take its place, so the two opposites would coexist, and that is impossible. Contradictories: (Benefit: The classification of all known things into these four divisions is true; nothing falls outside them except what Allah the Exalted is unique and alone in; for He is neither an opposite of a thing, nor a contradictory, nor a similar, nor a different; because there is no possibility of raising [something against Him], and this is a general ruling regarding His Exalted Essence and His High Attributes, because it is impossible to raise [something against them] due to the necessity of their existence.)
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage contains two parts. First, a proof that two similars (مثلين) cannot coexist in the same substrate. The argument: if a substrate could accept two similars, it would necessarily accept two opposites (ضدين) because for any attribute, the substrate is either occupied by it, its similar, or its opposite. If two similars were possible, one could exist without the other, allowing the opposite to take its place, resulting in two opposites coexisting, which is impossible. Second, a benefit (فائدة) concerning the classification of all known things: they are either opposite (ضد), contradictory (نقيض), similar (مثل), or different (خلاف). However, Allah and His attributes are excluded from this classification because there is nothing that is opposite, contradictory, similar, or different to Him, since there is nothing like Him and none can be raised in opposition.

Key Terms
ضد
نقيض
مثل
خلاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Allah can be described as being the opposite of creation, e.g., Eternal versus temporal.
Correction: Allah is not an opposite; opposites share a genus, but Allah is unique in essence.

Checkpoint
According to the benefit, what is the reason Allah and His attributes are not included in the four categories?
A) Because they are too great to be classified.
B) Because there is no possibility of raising something against Him (تعذر الرفع).
C) Because they are similar to each other.
D) Because they are opposites of each other.

Key Takeaway
Allah and His attributes transcend all relational categories of opposition, contradiction, similarity, and difference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe Allah's attributes, do you catch yourself using comparisons (e.g., 'His mercy is greater than...') that subtly limit Him? What would it mean to truly believe He is beyond all categories?
?
If Allah is not even 'different' from creation, then He is not merely unlike us, but wholly other. How does that realization change the way you approach Him in your prayers?
?
The lesson says nothing in creation can be compared to Allah. Is there any area of your life where you still think of Allah in terms similar to human beings? What would it look like to purify that belief today?

Definition of the Permissible in God's Right
Sanusi defines the permissible for God as doing or leaving every possible thing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definition of the permissible for God and the nuance that 'nothing is obligatory upon God' means by essence, not absolutely.

Core Idea
The permissible for God is doing or leaving any possible thing.

Sanusi now turns to the third rational judgment after necessary and impossible: the permissible. He defines it as whatever God can do or not do. However, this raises questions about divine obligations. Ash'ari theologians clarify that God is not obligated by His essence, but may be by His promise or wisdom. This passage lays out that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the full translation. Notice the definition and the examples given.
The passage includes a footnote clarifying the Ash'ari position.
2
Identify the definition and examples
Find the precise definition of al-ja'iz in God's right. Then list the examples: reward, punishment, sending prophets, and what is good or most good for creation.
These examples are chosen because they are points of disagreement with the Mu'tazila.
3
Understand the obligation nuance
The passage explains that nothing is obligatory upon God by His essence, but obligations can arise from His promise, wisdom, or eternal knowledge. Grasp this distinction.
This clarifies the common misunderstanding about God's actions being arbitrary.

The permissible things in His right, may He be exalted. And as for the permissible in His right, may He be exalted, it is the doing of every possible thing or its leaving. When he finished mentioning what is necessary in His right, may He be exalted, and what is impossible, he mentioned here the third division: that which is permissible in His right, may He be exalted. So he mentioned that the permissible in His right, may He be exalted, is the doing of every possible thing or its leaving. Thus enters into that reward and punishment (1), the sending of prophets, peace and blessings be upon them, and what is good and most good for creation; since nothing of that is obligatory upon Allah, may He be exalted, nor impossible (2); for if the doing of what is good and most good for creation were obligatory upon Him, just as (1) He only specified these mentioned things because they are a locus of disagreement between the People of the Sunnah and the Mu'tazila. (2) That is, in consideration of the essence of Allah, so it does not contradict its being obligatory due to His promise, may He be exalted, which does not fail, or due to His wisdom requiring its existence, or due to His eternal knowledge being attached to its existence. The upshot: it is not the intention of al-Ash'ari in his statement, 'Nothing is obligatory upon Allah,' to negate obligation absolutely, but rather the intention is to negate obligation in consideration of His essence, may He be exalted. And this does not contradict that it may be obligatory upon Him in consideration of His attributes. » Dusuqi » (p. 171). And if he said, 'obligatory from Him' as is the way of the Maturidis in expression in such a statement, it would be better. And for this meaning, the knower of Allah, may He be exalted, al-Nabulusi said in 'al-Anwar al-Ilahiyya' (p. 102): 'Thus possibility then is a description of the possible always in consideration of itself, in consideration of the attachment of power to it and its non-attachment, so it revolves between obligation and impossibility, not separating from one of them.' Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the permissible (al-ja'iz) in God's right. It begins by stating that after discussing what is necessary (wajib) and impossible (mustahil) for God, the author now turns to the third category: the permissible. He defines it as 'the doing of every possible thing or its leaving.' This definition includes actions like rewarding and punishing, sending prophets, and what is good or most good for creation. The key point is that none of these are inherently obligatory upon God from the perspective of His essence, nor are they impossible. The passage includes an important clarification from Dusuqi about the Ash'ari position: when they say 'nothing is obligatory upon God,' they mean by essence, not absolutely. Obligation can still arise from God's promise, wisdom, or eternal knowledge. The passage ends with a quote from al-Nabulusi about possibility being a description of contingent things in themselves, revolving between obligation and impossibility. This clarifies that when act is not done, it is impossible, not merely permissible.

Misconception: God has no obligations at all, so His actions are arbitrary and unpredictable.
Correction: Ash'ari theologians mean no obligation by essence, but obligation can arise from His promise or wisdom, so His actions are not arbitrary.

Key Takeaway
The permissible for God is the doing or leaving of every possible thing, and nothing is obligatory on God by essence, though it may be by His promise or wisdom.

The Permissible in God's Right: Definition and Refutation of the Mu'tazila
Sanusi defines al-ja'iz and refutes the Mu'tazilite view on al-aslah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-ja'iz (the permissible) in relation to God and why the Mu'tazilite view that God must do what is most beneficial is rationally untenable.

Core Idea
God is not obligated to create the best; His actions are free yet wise.

In the previous lesson, you learned what is obligatory and impossible for God. Now we turn to the third category: the permissible (al-ja'iz). Sanusi defines it as doing or leaving any possible thing, then defends this doctrine against the Mu'tazilite claim that God must always do what is most beneficial for creation. This lesson refutes that view with rational arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note how Sanusi defines al-ja'iz and then lists examples: reward and punishment, sending prophets, and what is beneficial.
Pay attention to the phrase 'فعل كل ممكن أو تركه'.
2
Understand the three rational divisions
Recall that the three judgments are wajib, mustahil, and ja'iz. Here Sanusi explains ja'iz in God's right. Understand why this definition is necessary.
3
Analyze the refutation
Follow the two arguments refuting the Mu'tazilite position: (1) If al-aslah were obligatory, trials and commands would not occur—but they do. (2) God can deliver benefits without hardship, and trials are not beneficial for all.
Notice the appeal to observable reality: 'باطل بالمشاهدة'.
4
Consider the clarifying footnotes
The footnotes (especially footnote 2) clarify that 'nothing is obligatory upon God' is a statement about His essence, not His attributes. This resolves misunderstandings.
Also note the distinction between absolute al-aslah and relative al-aslah.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Permissibles in His right, may He be exalted. As for the permissible (al-ja'iz) in His right, may He be exalted, it is doing every possible thing or leaving it. When he finished mentioning what is obligatory in His right and what is impossible, he mentioned here the third division, which is what is permissible in His right. So he mentioned that the permissible in His right is doing every possible thing or leaving it. Thus enters into that reward and punishment (1), and sending the prophets, peace be upon them, and what is beneficial and most beneficial (al-salah wa al-aslah) for creation; because nothing of that is obligatory upon God nor impossible (2); for if the doing of what is beneficial and most beneficial for creation were obligatory upon Him as the Mu'tazila claim, then no trial would have occurred in this world nor the next, and no command or prohibition would have occurred, and that is false by observation. And whatever of those benefits is estimated along with those trials and obligations, God is able to deliver those benefits without hardship or trial or obligation. Also, those benefits are not universal to all those who are tested and obligated; for it is certain that trial and obligation for one who is sealed with disbelief—may God protect us—is a punishment and exposure to eternal destruction (1). We ask God for well-being in our religion and our world, and a good ending without trial. (1) He only specified these mentioned things because they are a point of dispute between Ahl al-Sunna and the Mu'tazila. (2) That is, with respect to God's essence; so it does not contradict its being obligatory due to His promise that does not fail, or due to the requirement of His wisdom for its existence, or due to His knowledge in pre-eternity of its existence. The conclusion is that al-Ash'ari, by his statement 'Nothing is obligatory upon God,' does not intend negation of obligation absolutely, but rather negation of obligation by virtue of His essence, may He be exalted. This does not contradict that it may be obligatory upon Him by virtue of His attributes. (al-Dasuqi, p. 171) And if he had said 'from Him' as is the habit of the Maturidis in expression in such an explanation, it would have been better. And for this meaning, the gnostic of God, al-Nabulusi, said in al-Anwar al-Ilahiyya (p. 102): 'Thus possibility is a description of the possible eternally by virtue of itself, by virtue of power's relation to it and lack of relation to it, so it oscillates between obligation and impossibility, never free from one of them.' (1) This is based on the meaning being the absolute most beneficial, not relative to the person. (Mufad Yasin, fol. 220)
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage defines the category of the permissible (al-ja'iz) in relation to God, illustrates it with examples, and refutes the Mu'tazilite position that God must do what is most beneficial for creation. The author begins by stating that the permissible in God's right is doing every possible thing (kull mumkin) or leaving it. This is the third division after the obligatory (wajib) and the impossible (mustahil). Then he clarifies that this category includes reward and punishment, sending prophets, and what is beneficial and most beneficial for creation (al-salah wa al-aslah). The reason is that none of these are obligatory upon God from the perspective of His essence, nor are they impossible. The footnote (2) explains that when Ash'aris say 'nothing is obligatory upon God,' they mean by virtue of His essence, not by virtue of His attributes (such as promise or wisdom). The passage then refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that God must always do what is most beneficial. If that were obligatory, there would be no trials or commandments in this world or the next, which contradicts observable reality. Moreover, God can deliver benefits without hardships. Additionally, trials and obligations are not universally beneficial; for those destined for disbelief, they are actually a punishment and a means to eternal destruction. The footnote at the end specifies that the discussion concerns the absolute most beneficial (al-aslah al-mutlaq), not what is most beneficial relative to each individual. The passage thus upholds God's absolute freedom and power while accounting for His wisdom and promises.

Key Terms
الجائز
الممكن
الصلاح
الأصلح
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'aris believe that nothing is ever obligatory upon God, so He may break His promises.
Correction: Sanusi's footnote explains that 'nothing is obligatory' refers to God's essence, not His attributes. Obligation can arise from His promise, wisdom, or knowledge, so He will fulfill what He has promised.

Checkpoint

C) Any action that is not forbidden.
D) Whatever is consistent with divine wisdom.

Key Takeaway
The permissible in God's right includes all possible actions, and God is not bound to do what is most beneficial for creation, as shown by observable reality and logical arguments.

?

Proof of Bodies' Temporality from Motion and Rest
Sanusi proves the temporal origination of bodies through the temporality of motion and rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the temporality of motion and rest leads to the conclusion that all bodies are temporally originated.

Core Idea
What comes and goes cannot be eternal; bodies depend on such.

This lesson presents a foundational argument from Sanusi's creed. By examining the accidents of motion and rest, we find that they are clearly temporal—they appear and disappear. Since bodies cannot exist without either motion or rest, it follows that bodies themselves must be temporal. This argument is a key step in proving the existence of a Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic source carefully. Notice the logical structure: a conditional 'if... then' followed by an observation and a conclusion.
2
Identify the key premises
Find where the text states that motion and rest are necessarily temporal, and that they are inseparable from bodies.
The proof uses the principle that if something were eternal, it could never cease to exist.
3
Follow the logical flow
Trace how the text moves from the temporality of motion and rest to the temporality of bodies. Note the summary principle about inseparable things (mutalāzimayn).
The phrase 'وبالجملة' signals the summary.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this argument is important: it establishes that the universe is not eternal, paving the way for proving a Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا شكَّ في وجوبِ الحدوثِ لكلِّ واحدٍ مِنَ السكونِ والحركةِ؛ إذ لو كان واحدٌ منهما قديماً لَمَا قَبِلَ أنْ ينعدمَ أبداً؛ لأنَّ ما ثبتَ قِدَمُه استحالَ عدمُه، ولا خفاءَ أنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ مِنَ السكونِ والحركةِ قابلٌ للعدم؛ لأنَّه قد شُوهدَ عدمُ كلِّ واحدٍ منهما بوجودِ ضده في كثيرٍ مِنَ الأجرام، فيلزمُ استواءُ الأجرامِ كلِّها في ذلك، وإذا ثبتَ حدوثُهما واستحالَ وجودُهما في الأزلِ.. لزمَ حدوثُ الأجرامِ واستحالةُ وجودِها في الأزلِ قطعاً؛ لاستحالةِ انفكاكِها عن الحركةِ والسكونِ. وبالجملة: فحدوثُ أحدِ المتلازمينَ يستلزمُ حدوثَ الآخرِ ضرورةً. (١) ما قرره المصنف مبني على إثبات أربعة من المطالب السبعة؛ وهي: إثبات زائد على الأجرام، وأنها حادثة، وغير منفكة عنها، مع استحالة حوادث لا أول لها.
There is no doubt about the necessity of temporal origination (ḥudūth) for each of rest (sukūn) and motion (ḥaraka); because if one of them were eternal (qadīm), it would never accept non-existence, since what is established as eternal is impossible to cease. And it is not hidden that each of rest and motion is susceptible to non-existence, because the non-existence of each of them has been observed by the existence of its opposite in many bodies (ajrām). So the equality of all bodies in that follows. And when their temporal origination is established and their existence in pre-eternity (azal) is impossible, the temporal origination of bodies and the impossibility of their existence in pre-eternity necessarily follow, due to the impossibility of their separation from motion and rest. In summary: the temporal origination of one of two inseparable things necessitates the temporal origination of the other necessarily. (1) What the author established is based on proving four of the seven demands (al-maṭālib al-sabʿa); namely: proving something additional to the bodies, that they are temporal, that they are inseparable from it, along with the impossibility of events without beginning.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage provides the first key proof in the argument for the existence of God: establishing that bodies (ajrām) are temporally originated (ḥādith). The proof proceeds by examining motion (ḥaraka) and rest (sukūn), which are accidents inseparable from bodies. The reasoning is: 1) Motion and rest are clearly temporal because we observe them ceasing (e.g., a moving body becomes still, and vice versa). 2) If they were eternal, they could never cease to exist, but their opposites appear. 3) Since bodies cannot exist without either motion or rest, and both are temporal, bodies must also be temporal. The summary statement formulates the logical principle: the temporality of one of two inseparable (mutalāzimayn) things entails the temporality of the other. The footnote (from a commentator) notes that this proof presupposes four of the seven demands (al-maṭālib al-sabʿa) that are established earlier in the text: that there is something additional to bodies (i.e., accidents), that bodies are temporal, that they are never free of accidents, and that there cannot be an infinite series of events without a beginning.

Key Terms
حدوث
سكون
حركة
أجرام
أزل
متلازمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that motion and rest are temporal only because they last a short time, but the argument is that they are preceded by non-existence and subject to ceasing.
Correction: Sanusi observes that each of motion and rest is 'susceptible to non-existence' because we see them cease and be replaced by their opposites. This establishes temporal origination, not merely short duration.

Checkpoint
What is the key logical principle used in this proof to conclude that bodies are temporal?
A) The temporality of one of two inseparable things necessitates the temporality of the other.
B) Whatever is preceded by non-existence is temporal.
C) Every temporal thing has a cause.
D) Motion and rest are opposites.

Key Takeaway
The temporal origination of bodies is proven because motion and rest are temporal and inseparable from them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that your own body—everything about you that you can see and touch—is temporary and will cease to exist. If you truly believed this, how would it change what you prioritize today? What would you begin doing differently with your time and energy?
?
Sanusi argues that motion and rest come and go. Your life is a series of movements and still moments. If you knew that each breath could be your last, what would you do with your next movement? Would you use it to seek Allah or to satisfy a fleeting desire?
?
If bodies are temporal, then the world you are building—your career, your home, your reputation—is built on what is passing away. What have you attached your heart to that will not survive? What would it look like to detach from it today?

Proof from Mutual Equivalence: The Final Four Types
Sanusi presents four remaining types of mutual equivalence and deduces a specific Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the final four types of mutual equivalence (quantity, place, direction, and quality) necessitate a specific Creator to break the equilibrium.

Core Idea
When equal things are equally possible, only a specific Creator can tip the balance.

Sanusi now presents the remaining four types of mutual equivalence that serve as the final pillars of his argument. Each type pairs two equally possible opposites, and if one existed without a cause, preference would violate rationality. This lesson walks you through that reasoning and its profound conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice how Sanusi lists four specific types of equivalence.
These four complement two earlier types from the previous lesson.
2
Understand the logical contradiction
Focus on the core argument: two equal things cannot have one preferred over the other without a cause. That would combine equality and preference, which is impossible.
3
Identify the conclusion
Sanusi concludes that without an external distinguisher (Allah), nothing would exist. See how he ties this to divine necessity.
The conclusion is doxological: glory to Allah who made all things dependent on Him.
4
Reflect on the takeaway
This proof shows not just that a Creator exists, but that the Creator must be specific and choosing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومقداره المخصوص مساوٍ لسائرِ المقادير ، ومكانه الذي اختص بهِ مساوٍ لسائر الأمكنة ، وجهته المخصوصة مساوية لسائرِ الجهاتِ ، وصفته المخصوصة مساوية لسائر الصفاتِ . فهذه أنواع ، كلُّ واحدٍ منها فيهِ أمرانِ متساويان(۱) ، فلو حدث أحدهما بنفسِهِ لترجّحَ على مقابله مع أنَّهُ مساوٍ له في قبول كلَّ جرم لهما على حد سواء ، فقد لزم أن لو وُجِدَ شيءٌ مِنَ العالمِ بنفسِهِ بلا مُوجِدٍ لزم اجتماع الاستواء والرجحان المتنافيين ، وذلك محال . فإذاً ؛ لولا مولانا جلَّ وعزَّ الذي خَصَّ كلَّ فردٍ مِنْ أفراد العالم بما اختص بهِ .. لَمَا وُجِدَ شيءٌ مِنَ العالم . فسبحانَ مَنْ أفصح بوجوب وجودِهِ وجوبُ افتقار الكائناتِ كلها إليه ، تبارك وتعالى وجلَّ وعلا . فقولي : ( لزم أن يكون أحد الأمرين المتساويين ( أعني بهما : الوجود والعدم ، والمقدار المخصوص ، وغير ذلكَ ممَّا ذكرناه آنفاً ، وباقي الكلام واضح ، وبالله تعالى التوفيق .
And its specific measure is equal to all other measures, and its specific place is equal to all other places, and its specific direction is equal to all other directions, and its specific quality is equal to all other qualities. These are types, each of which contains two things that are equal. If one of them came into existence by itself, it would be preferred over its counterpart despite being equal to it in acceptance of every body [being] for both equally. So it necessarily follows that if something of the world existed by itself without a cause, there would be a combination of equality and preference, which are mutually contradictory, and that is impossible. Thus, were it not for our Lord, may He be glorified and exalted, Who distinguished every individual of the world with what is specific to it, nothing of the world would exist. So glorified is He Who made the necessity of the dependency of all existences upon Him manifest in the necessity of His existence, blessed and exalted is He. So I say: 'It would necessitate that one of the two equal things (I mean: existence and non-existence, the specific measure, and other such things we mentioned earlier) be [preferred], and the rest of the speech is clear, and with Allah is success.'
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is the continuation and conclusion of the proof from mutual equivalence (burhān al-taqābul). It presents the final four of the six types of equivalence: specific quantity, specific place, specific direction, and specific quality. For each type, two opposite individuals (e.g., a specific quantity for one body vs. another, a specific place, etc.) are equally capable of receiving existence. If one came to exist without a cause, it would be arbitrarily preferred over its equal without a reason. This combination of equality and preference is contradictory and impossible. Therefore, a specific Creator must exist to break the equivalence and assign each individual its specific property. The argument concludes with praise of Allah, who made all things dependent on Him.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof from mutual equivalence merely shows that a Creator exists, without specifying that the Creator must be a choosing agent.
Correction: The argument specifically demonstrates that a cause must be able to break the symmetry among equally possible alternatives, which only a personal, choosing Creator can do.

Checkpoint
What does the proof from mutual equivalence primarily demonstrate?
A) That the world is eternal.
B) That the world must have a specific Creator who chooses among equally possible alternatives.
C) That all six types of equivalence are impossible.
D) That the world could have come into existence by itself without a cause.

Key Takeaway
The proof from mutual equivalence shows that a specific, choosing Creator is necessary to explain why any one of many equally possible things exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that every detail of your existence—your height, your birthplace, your very face—was chosen specifically by Allah. Have you ever considered that these could have been different? When you look in the mirror today, will you thank Him for His precise choice for you, or will you take it for granted?
?
If all things are equally possible and require a specific cause, then your current state of faith—being a believer—is also a result of His specific will. Are you living as if your faith is a random accident or a deliberate gift? What will you do today to show that you recognize His choice in guiding you?
?
The argument concludes that without a specific Creator, nothing would exist. Right now, you exist because He chose to bring you into being and sustain you. What one thing in your life reveals that you truly believe this? And what one area of your life still acts as if your existence and success are self-made?

Proof of God's Necessary Endurance
Sanusi proves God's endurance necessity via possibility negating pre‑eternality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi proves that God's endurance (baqa) is necessary by showing that its denial would contradict the already established necessity of His pre‑eternality.

Core Idea
God's endurance is not separate from His pre‑eternality—one entails the other.

Sanusi now builds on the previously proven necessity of God's pre‑eternality to establish His necessary endurance. He argues that if God could cease to exist, then His existence would be merely possible, contradicting the necessary pre‑eternality already proven. This lesson traces that logical chain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if annihilation were possible, then pre‑eternality would be negated'.
The passage is a single continuous proof.
2
Identify key concepts
Identify the concepts of possible (ja'iz) and necessary (wajib). Understand why a possible being's existence must be originated (hadith).
This is essential to the logic.
3
Understand the entailment
Sanusi states that necessity of pre‑eternality entails necessity of endurance. Reflect on why that must be true: if something is necessary from eternity, it cannot cease to exist.
This is the core reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُوبِ الْبَقَاءِ لَهُ تَعَالَى : فَلَأَنَّهُ لَوْ أَمْكَنَ أَنْ يَلْحَقَهُ الْعَدَمُ لَأَنْتَفَى عَنْهُ الْقِدَمُ ؛ لِكَوْنِ وُجُودِهِ حِينَئِذٍ يَصِيرُ جَائِزاً لا وَاجِباً ، وَالْجَائِزُ لا يَكُونُ وُجُودُهُ إِلا حَادِثاً ، كَيْفَ وَقَدْ سَبَقَ قَريباً وُجُوبُ قِدَمِهِ تَعَالَى ؟! لا شكٍّ أنَّ وجوبَ القدم مستلزم لوجوبِ البقاء ، فلما قامَ البرهانُ على وجوب قدمِهِ جلَّ وعزَّ وجبَ بقاؤُهُ تبارك وتعالى ؛ إذ لو جازَ أَنْ يلحقه العدمُ - تعالى عن ذلك علوّاً كبيراً - لكان وجوده جائزاً لا واجباً ؛ لصدقِ حقيقةِ الجائز حينئذٍ على ذاتِ مولانا جل وعزَّ ؛ لأنَّ الجائز ما يصح وجودُهُ وعدمُهُ وهذا التقديرُ الفاسد يستلزم صحة الوجود والعدم للذات العليَّةِ تبارك وتعالى ، فيكون جائز الوجودِ ، وذلك يستلزم حدوثه تعالى عن ذلك (١) ؛ لما عرفتَ مِن استحالة ترجيح الوجودِ الجائز على العدمِ مقابله المساوي له في القبولِ مِنْ غيرِ فاعل مرجح ، كيف وقد سبق قريباً بالبرهان القاطع وجوب قدمِهِ جَلَّ وعزَّ ؟! فإذاً ؛ يجبُ بقاؤُهُ تبارك وتعالى كما وجب قدمه (١)
And as for the proof of the necessity of endurance (wujub al-baqa) for Him, exalted: it is that if annihilation were possible to affect Him, then pre-eternality (qidam) would be negated from Him, because His existence then would become possible (ja'iz) rather than necessary (wajib), and the possible's existence is only originated (hadith). How, when the necessity of His pre-eternality (wujub qidamihi) has already preceded recently?! There is no doubt that the necessity of pre-eternality entails the necessity of endurance. So when the proof for the necessity of His pre-eternality, may He be glorified and exalted, was established, His endurance, blessed and exalted, became necessary. For if annihilation were possible to affect Him — exalted is He above that, greatly — His existence would be possible, not necessary, because the reality of the possible would then be true of the essence of our Master, may He be glorified and exalted. For the possible is that whose existence and non-existence are both valid. And this corrupt assumption entails the validity of existence and non-existence for the supreme essence, blessed and exalted. So it would be possible in existence, and that entails its origination (huduth) — exalted is He above that (1) — because you have known the impossibility of preponderating the possible existence over its opposite equal in acceptance without a preponderating agent. How, when the decisive proof for the necessity of His pre-eternality, may He be glorified and exalted, has already preceded recently?! Therefore, His endurance, blessed and exalted, is necessary just as His pre-eternality was necessary (1).
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhan) for the necessity of God's endurance (wujub al-baqa). The author argues: if annihilation were possible for God, then His pre-eternality (qidam) would be negated, because His existence would then be possible (ja'iz) rather than necessary (wajib). But a possible being's existence is only originated (hadith), not eternal, since the possible requires a preponderating cause to exist over non-existence. Moreover, the necessity of pre-eternality has already been proven in earlier discussions, and necessity of pre-eternality necessarily entails necessity of endurance—if something is necessary from eternity, it cannot cease to be. Therefore, God's endurance is necessary, just as His pre-eternality is necessary. The author repeats the argument for emphasis, concluding that since pre-eternality is proven, endurance follows.

Key Terms
wujub al-baqa
wujub al-qidam
ja'iz
wajib
hadith
burhan

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof for God's endurance is independent of the proof for His pre‑eternality.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the necessity of endurance directly follows from the necessity of pre‑eternality; they are not separate proofs.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what would follow if annihilation were possible for God?
A) His endurance would be necessary.
B) His pre‑eternality would be negated.
C) His existence would become impossible.
D) His attributes would change.

Key Takeaway
God's endurance (baqa) is necessary because its denial would contradict the already proven necessity of His pre‑eternality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God's endurance is necessary and that He cannot cease to exist, how does this affect the way you rely on Him in your daily struggles? Will you still turn to other sources of security knowing that only He necessarily endures?
?
The proof shows that if annihilation were possible for God, He would be like created things. Do you sometimes treat God as if He is just another being who could fail or be subject to change? When? What needs to change in your heart?
?
What would it mean for your faith if you could provide a logical proof for God's endurance? Does that change how you respond to doubts about God's permanence? Write down one doubt you have had and how this argument answers it.
