Clarification on the Four Modes of Existence
Al-Nabulsi explains the different modes of existence for the possible being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the possible being's originated existence applies only to actual existence, not to its existence in divine knowledge and speech, which are eternal.

Core Idea
The possible being is eternal in knowledge but originated in actuality.

Al-Nabulsi adds an important clarification to Sanusi's statement that the possible's existence is only originated. However, this applies only to actual existence. In God's knowledge and speech, the possible exists eternally. This lesson explains the distinction based on the four modes of existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the key terms for modes of existence.
2
Identify the key distinction
Al-Nabulsi distinguishes between actual existence (wujud 'ayni) and existence in knowledge and speech. Only the former is originated.
This resolves an apparent contradiction in Sanusi's text.
3
Understand the four modes
There are four modes of existence: actual, speech, knowledge, and writing. Each has a different ruling regarding eternality or origination.
The commentary explains each mode's classification.
4
Recognize the misconception
A common error is to think the possible is originated in every respect. This passage shows it is not.
The checkpoint tests this understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه : قوله فى المتن : ( والجائز لا يكون وجوده إلا حادثاً ) قال العارف بالله النابلسي في « الأنوار الإلهية ) ( ص ١٠٨ ) : ( أي : لا يتصور وجوده أبداً في عينه إلا وجوداً حادثاً ، وكذلك وجوده في الكتابة ، وأما وجوده في القول وفي العلم فهو وجود قديم ، وكلامنا الآن في الوجود العيني ؛ لأنه المقصود من معنى الوجود ، فالجائز موجود في العلم موجود في القول ، وهو بهذا الاعتبار قديم الوجود ، وموجود في الكتابة في اللوح المحفوظ موجود في عينه ، وهو بهذا الاعتبار حادث الوجود ) ، وهذا كلام مؤسس على اعتبار الوجودات الأربعة .
Warning: His saying in the text: 'And the possible's existence is only originated' — al-Arif billah al-Nabulsi said in 'al-Anwar al-Ilahiyya' (p. 108): 'That is: his existence in actuality is never conceived except as an originated existence, and similarly his existence in writing; but as for his existence in speech and in knowledge, it is an eternal existence. Our speech now is about actual existence (al-wujud al-'ayni), because it is the intended meaning of existence. So the possible exists in knowledge, exists in speech, and in this respect it is eternal in existence, and exists in writing in the Preserved Tablet, exists in actuality, and in this respect it is originated in existence.' And this speech is founded on considering the four modes of existence.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is a clarifying note (tanbih) by the commentator al-Nabulsi regarding the statement in the main text: 'the possible being's existence is only originated'. He explains that this statement applies specifically to existence in actuality (al-wujud al-'ayni), not to existence in speech (al-qawli) or in knowledge (al-'ilmi). In knowledge and speech, the possible being has an eternal (qadim) existence because God's knowledge and speech are eternal. However, in writing (kitaba) — such as in the Preserved Tablet — and in actuality (ayn), it is originated (hadith). Al-Nabulsi clarifies that the discussion in the proof is about actual existence, which is the primary meaning of existence. He then notes that the entire explanation is based on the distinction of four modes of existence: existence in actuality, in speech, in knowledge, and in writing. This resolves any apparent contradiction: a possible being can be both originated (in actuality) and eternal (in knowledge).

Key Terms
wujud 'ayni
wujud qawli
wujud 'ilmi
wujud kitabi
ja'iz
qadim
hadith

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the possible being's existence is originated in every respect.
Correction: It is only originated in actuality and writing, not in knowledge and speech.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nabulsi, in which modes of existence is the possible being's existence eternal?
A) In actuality and writing.
B) In knowledge and speech.
C) In actuality and knowledge.
D) In speech and writing.

Key Takeaway
The possible being's existence is eternal in divine knowledge and speech, but originated in actuality and writing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Before you were born, your existence was known by God from eternity. Does this make you feel significant or accountable? How does living as a being that was 'eternally known' change the weight of your choices today?
?
Al-Nabulsi teaches that your actual existence is 'originated'—it happens now. Are you living as if each moment is a fresh gift from God, or as if it is owed to you? What would change if you truly believed that your existence is a new creation every instant?
?
If the possible being is eternal in knowledge but originated in actuality, then your future is already known but still undecided. How does this paradox affect your trust in God's plan while you strive to act rightly? Are you willing to surrender your worries to the One who knew you before time?

Proof of God's Permissible Actions
Sanusi proves God's actions are permissible using logical impossibility of reversing 'possible'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that God's actions are permissible (ja'iz) and how the concept of mumkin underlies this argument.

Core Idea
If God's actions were necessary or impossible, the possible would become a contradiction.

Sanusi now presents a proof that God's actions are neither obligatory nor impossible, but permissible (ja'iz). The argument hinges on the definition of 'possible' (mumkin) in kalam: that which the intellect can accept as existing or not existing. Sanusi shows that if an action were rationally necessary or impossible, it would change the very nature of the possible—a logical impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement
Read the first part of the Arabic passage, from 'وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ' to 'وَذَلِكَ لَا يُعْقَلُ'. Notice the logical conditional: if something were obligatory or impossible, then the possible would become obligatory or impossible.
This is the core of the proof.
2
Understand the definition of mumkin
Read the second part where Sanusi defines 'al-mumkin' as synonymous with 'al-ja'iz', meaning its existence and non-existence are both rationally sound.
This definition is crucial for the proof.
3
Trace the logical contradiction
Combine the two parts: the possible (by definition) can exist or not exist. If existence became necessary or impossible, the possible would no longer be possible—a reversal of realities (قلب الحقائق). That is unintelligible.
Hence, God's actions cannot be obligatory or impossible.
4
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to verify you know the meaning of 'al-mumkin' in kalam terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ كَوْنِ فِعْل الْمُمْكِنَاتِ أَوْ تَرْكِهَا جَائِزاً فِي حَقِّهِ تَعَالَى : فَلَأَنَّهُ لَوْ وَجَبَ عَلَيْهِ تَعَالَى شَيْءٌ مِنْهَا عَقْلاً أَوِ اسْتَحَالَ عَقْلاً .. لانْقَلَبَ الْمُمْكِنُ وَاجِباً أَوْ مُسْتَحِيلاً ، وَذَلِكَ لا يُعْقَلُ لا شك أنَّ الممكن في اصطلاحِ المتكلمينَ مرادف للجائز ، فيكونُ معناه : هو الذي يصح في العقل وجوده وعدمه ؛ فإذا لو وجبَ وجوده عقلاً أو استحال عقلاً .. لزم قلب الحقائقِ ، وذلك لا يُعقَلُ .
As for the proof of the fact that performing or refraining from possible things is permissible in His right, may He be exalted: it is because if something among them were obligatory upon Him, may He be exalted, by reason, or impossible by reason, then the possible would become obligatory or impossible, and that is not intelligible. There is no doubt that the possible in the terminology of the theologians is synonymous with the permissible, so its meaning is: that which is sound in the intellect both its existence and non-existence; so if its existence were obligatory by reason or impossible by reason, it would entail the reversal of realities, and that is not intelligible.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage provides the proof that God's actions are permissible (ja'iz). It argues that if an action were necessary or impossible by reason, then the status of the possible (which is by definition that which can either exist or not exist) would change to necessary or impossible, which would be a reversal of logical categories and is unintelligible. The author then clarifies that in kalam terminology, 'possible' (mumkin) is the same as 'permissible' (ja'iz), meaning that reason accepts both its existence and non-existence. So if it were made obligatory or impossible, it would contradict its definition. This establishes that actions are permissible in God's right.

Key Terms
برهان الجائز
الممكن
الجائز
الواجب
المستحيل
قلب الحقائق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's actions can be rationally obligatory or impossible, like human actions.
Correction: The passage proves that God's actions cannot be rationally obligatory or impossible because that would contradict the definition of the possible; they are all permissible (ja'iz).

Checkpoint
According to the kalam terminology, what does 'al-mumkin' mean?
A) That which cannot exist.
B) That which must exist.
C) That which is sound in the intellect both its existence and non-existence.
D) That which is forbidden by reason.

Key Takeaway
God's actions are all permissible because the possibility of their existence or non-existence is rationally sound; otherwise, the very concept of 'possible' would be violated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you assume that God must act a certain way based on your limited understanding? Are you willing to let God be free in His actions, even when it challenges your expectations?
?
If God's actions were necessary, you could predict and even hold Him accountable to your logic. But the proof shows He is not bound by necessity. How does this truth confront your desire to control or limit God?
?
The reversal of realities would occur if God's actions were necessary. Have you ever reversed the reality of God's absolute freedom by demanding He conform to your sense of justice or fairness? Will you repent from this today?

The Three Rational Judgments for Messengers
Sanusi enumerates the necessary, impossible, and possible attributes for messengers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the attributes that are obligatory, impossible, and possible for messengers, and how these relate to their mission.

Core Idea
Messengers must be truthful, trustworthy, and deliver the message; the opposite is impossible.

Sanusi now applies the three rational judgments—obligatory, impossible, possible—to the prophets and messengers. This is not an abstract exercise; it defines the essential qualities that make a messenger trustworthy and distinguishes them from impostors. Before we read, ask yourself: what would a messenger absolutely need to have, and what could they never have?

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three categories
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi clearly divides the discussion into three parts: obligatory, impossible, and possible.
The Arabic words 'يجب', 'يستحيل', 'يجوز' mark the categories.
2
Examine the obligatory attributes
Focus on the obligatory attributes: truthfulness (الصدق), trustworthiness (الأمانة), and delivery (التبليغ). Ask yourself why each is essential for a messenger.
These are the foundation of a messenger's credibility.
3
Understand the impossible and possible
Notice that the impossible attributes are the opposites of the obligatory ones. Then see that possible attributes are human accidents that do not diminish the messenger's rank, like illness.
This shows that prophets are human but protected from faults that would undermine their message.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام على الرسل فيها يجب لهم ويستحيل عليهم ويجوز في حقهم. وَأَمَّا الرُّسُلُ عَلَيْهِمُ الصَّلاةُ وَالسَّلَامُ : فَيَجِبُ فِي حَقِّهِمُ الصِّدْقُ ، وَالأَمَانَهُ ، وَتَبْلِيغُ مَا أُمِرُوا بِتَبْلِيغِهِ لِلْخَلْقِ. وَيَسْتَحِيلُ فِي حَقِّهِمْ عَلَيْهِمُ الصَّلاةُ وَالسَّلامُ : أَضْدَادُ هَذِهِ الصِّفَاتِ ؛ وَهِيَ : الْكَذِبُ ، وَالْخِيَانَةُ بِفِعْلِ شَيْءٍ مِمَّا نُهِيَ عَنْهُ نَهْيَ تَحْرِيم أَوْ كَرَاهَةٍ ، وَكِتْمَانُ شَيْءٍ مِمَّا أُمِرُوا بِتَبْلِيغِهِ لِلْخَلْقِ . وَيَجُوزُ فِي حَقِّهِمْ عَلَيْهِمُ الصَّلاةُ وَالسَّلامُ : مَا هُوَ مِنَ الْأَعْرَاضِ الْبَشَرِيَّةِ الَّتِي لا تُؤَدِّي إِلَى نَقْصٍ فِي مَرَاتِبِهِمُ الْعَلِيَّةِ ؛ كَالْمَرَضِ وَنَحْوِهِ .
Discussion of the messengers: what is obligatory for them, what is impossible for them, and what is possible regarding them. As for the messengers, upon them be prayers and peace: obligatory regarding them is truthfulness, trustworthiness, and delivery of what they were commanded to deliver to creation. And impossible regarding them, upon them be prayers and peace, are the opposites of these attributes, which are: lying, betrayal by doing something from which they were forbidden with a prohibition of prohibition or dislike, and concealment of something they were commanded to deliver to creation. And possible regarding them, upon them be prayers and peace, is what pertains to human accidents that do not lead to deficiency in their lofty ranks, such as illness and the like.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a classification passage. The author, Sanusi, discusses the three rational judgments (obligatory, impossible, possible) as they apply to the messengers (ʿalayhim al-ṣalātu wa al-salām). First, he states the attributes that are obligatory (wājib) for them: truthfulness (ṣidq), trustworthiness (amānah), and delivering (tablīgh) everything they were commanded to convey to the people. Then, he states what is impossible (mustaḥīl) for them: the opposites of these attributes—namely, lying (kidhb), treachery (khiyānah) by committing something prohibited either as a forbiddance or dislike, and concealing (kitmān) any part of what they were ordered to deliver. Finally, he states what is possible (jāʾiz) for them: any human accidents (aʿrāḍ bashariyya) that do not detract from their high rank, such as illness and similar things.

Misconception: Some think that any human attribute is possible for prophets, including major sins or deficiencies.
Correction: Sanusi specifies only human accidents that do not diminish their lofty rank, such as illness. Prophets are protected from anything that would compromise their message or status.

Checkpoint

A) Truthfulness

Key Takeaway
Messengers must be truthful, trustworthy, and deliver fully; they cannot lie, betray, or conceal; and they experience permissible human conditions like illness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
Prophets experienced illness and human conditions without diminishing their rank. When you face illness or hardship, do you see it as a sign of Allah's disfavor or as a test that can elevate you? How will you change your perspective today?

Third Argument: Special Predicate Over Negated General
Sanusi responds to an objection about special predicate over general.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how negation of a general term changes the logical rule that a specific cannot serve as a predicate of the general.

Core Idea
When the general is negated, the specific can be a predicate.

This lesson examines a key objection in classical logic: that a specific term cannot be a predicate of a general term. Sanusi grants the rule but shows that in the Shahada, the general is negated, so the rule does not apply. The speech negates all gods and then specifies the predicate to one individual.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the original Arabic and the literal English translation. Notice the structure: an objection is stated, granted, and then countered.
Pay attention to the words 'مسلم' (granted) and 'لكن' (but) which signal the logical move.
2
Identify the objection and its concession
The objection is that the specific cannot be predicate of the general. Sanusi says 'is granted'. This shows he accepts the general rule.
The rule is not being rejected; its applicability is limited.
3
Understand the counter: negation changes the logic
In 'لا إله إلا الله', the general term 'إله' is negated by 'لا'. Then the specific 'الله' is affirmed after negation. Because the general is negated, the rule does not apply.
The speech is for negating generality and then specifying the predicate to one individual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثالث: فهو أنْ يُقالَ: قولُكَ: إن الخاص لا يكون خبراً عن العام مسلَّمْ، لكن في (لا إله إلا الله) لم يُخبَرْ بخاصٌ عن عام؛ لأنَّ العموم منفي، والكلامُ إنَّما سيق لنفي العموم وتخصيص الخبرِ المذكور بواحد من أفرادِ ما دلَّ عليه اللفظ العام.
And as for the third: it is that it is said: your saying: 'The specific (al-khāṣṣ) cannot be a predicate (khabar) of the general (al-ʿāmm)' is granted, but in 'lā ilāha illā allāh' the specific is not predicated of the general, because the general is negated (manfī), and the speech is only intended for negating the general and specifying the mentioned predicate to one of the individuals of what the general word (al-lafẓ al-ʿāmm) indicates.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection that the specific (الخاص) cannot serve as a predicate of the general (العام). The objection is conceded but then countered by noting that in 'لا إله إلا الله', the general is negated. The speech is structured to negate generality and then affirm the predicate for a specific individual from among the individuals covered by the general word. Therefore, the specific is not predicated of a positive general; rather, it is predicated after negation, which changes the logical structure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the rule 'special cannot be predicate of general' applies universally in all contexts.
Correction: This passage shows that the rule does not apply when the general term is negated, as in the Shahada.

Checkpoint

A) Because the specific is not actually a predicate here.
B) Because the general is negated, and the speech is for negating generality and specifying the predicate.
C) Because the rule is false in this particular case.
D) Because the general is an individual.

Key Takeaway
The rule that the specific cannot be predicate of the general only applies when the general is affirmed; negation cancels the rule.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Sanusi teaches that the rule about specific-predicate only applies when the general is affirmed. Are there areas in your life where you have affirmed 'general' priorities (career, comfort, approval) without fully negating them, so that Allah remains just a vague predicate? What one practical step can you take today to 'negate the general' so that Allah becomes your specific, central focus?
?
This lesson shows that the negation prepares for a precise specification. Reflect: have you ever truly negated all that is not Allah in your intention during worship? If not, your affirmation of 'illā allāh' may be general, not specific. What can you do right now to make your next prayer or act of worship a direct, specific address to Allah alone?

Logical Analysis of the Exception in the Testimony of Faith
Sanusi enumerates four logical possibilities for the exception (istithna') in 'la ilaha illa Allah' and identifies the correct one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four logical possibilities for the exception in 'la ilaha illa Allah' and why only one is valid when 'ilah' means the object of worship by right.

Core Idea
The testimony's exception is valid only if 'ilah' means the true deity, not any object of worship.

Sanusi uses logical analysis to clarify the testimony of faith. He enumerates all possible combinations of particular and universal terms for the exception and shows that only one yields a true statement. But even that one depends on the correct meaning of 'ilah'. Let's see how he arrives at the sound interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the translation. Notice the enumeration and the repeated 'sound' at the end.
2
Identify the four logical combinations
Find the four possibilities: both particular, both universal, first particular second universal, and first universal second particular. The first three are declared invalid.
These are based on whether المستثنى منه and المستثنى are particular or universal.
3
Understand why three are invalid
Reflect on the first three: they lead to falsehood or unintended meanings. For example, both particular would not make sense for the universal testimony.
The author does not explain why each is invalid in detail, but focuses on the fourth.
4
Examine the fourth possibility
The fourth combination (first universal, second particular) is the candidate. But it splits into two based on what 'ilah' means: absolute object of worship vs. object of worship by right.
This is the critical point.
5
Confirm the correct meaning
Only when 'ilah' means 'object of worship by right' does the testimony become sound, because it negates all false gods and affirms true divinity for Allah alone.
The repetition of 'صح' emphasizes the correctness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصلُ : أنَّ المعاني المقدَّرةَ عقلاً في باعتبار المستثنى منه والمستثنى أربعة : ثلاثة منها باطلة ، والرابع ينقسم قسمين ، أحد قسميه باطل ، والآخرُ هو الذي يصحُ مِنَ الأقسام كلّها فالثلاثةُ الباطلة : أن يكونا جزئيين ، أو كليين ، أوِ الأَوَّلُ جزئيّاً والثاني كلياً ، والرابع عكس الثالث ؛ وهو أن يكونَ الأوَّلُ كلياً والثاني جزئيّاً . فإنْ كانَ المراد بالكليّ الذي هو الإله مطلق المعبود . . لم يصح ؛ لما يلزمُ عليهِ مِنَ الكذب ؛ لكثرة المعبوداتِ الباطلة وإنْ كانَ المراد بالإله المعبود بحقِّ . . صح صح
And the conclusion is that the rationally conceivable meanings with respect to the thing excluded from (المستثنى منه) and the excluded (المستثنى) are four: three of them are invalid, and the fourth divides into two parts, one of which is invalid, and the other is the one that is sound among all the divisions. So the three invalid ones: that both are particular (جزئيين), or both are universal (كليين), or the first is particular and the second is universal. And the fourth is the opposite of the third: that the first is universal and the second is particular. So if what is meant by the universal which is ilah is an absolute object of worship, it is not sound because of the falsehood that follows from it due to the multitude of false objects of worship. And if what is meant by ilah is the object of worship by right, then it is sound, sound.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage presents a logical analysis of the exception (istithna') in the testimony of faith. The author states that the rationally possible meanings of the mustathna minhu (the thing from which exception is made, i.e., the scope of 'la ilaha') and the mustathna (the thing excepted, i.e., 'Allah') are four combinations based on whether each is particular (juz'i) or universal (kulli). Three are invalid: (1) both particular, (2) both universal, (3) first particular and second universal. The fourth is the opposite of the third: first universal and second particular. This is the candidate for validity. However, even this valid structure has two possibilities depending on what is meant by 'ilah' as the universal: if it means 'object of worship in an absolute sense' (including false gods), then the statement would be false because such objects of worship are many, contradicting the negation of all individuals. If 'ilah' means 'the object of worship by right' (i.e., the true deity), then the statement is sound, because the negation denies all false gods, and the affirmation restricts true divinity to Allah alone. The author emphasizes correctness by repeating 'صح' (sound).

Key Terms
المستثنى منه
المستثنى
جزئي
كلي
مطلق المعبود
المعبود بحق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'ilah' in the testimony must mean 'absolute object of worship' leading to falsehood.
Correction: The correct interpretation is 'object of worship by right', which makes the testimony sound.

Checkpoint
Which of the four logical possibilities for the exception is valid according to this passage?
A) Both particular.
B) Both universal.
C) First particular, second universal.
D) First universal, second particular, with 'ilah' meaning the object of worship by right.

Key Takeaway
The testimony 'la ilaha illa Allah' is logically sound only when 'ilah' is understood as the true deity by right.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'la ilaha illa Allah', do you consciously affirm that all false objects of worship are negated and that only Allah, the true deity by right, is worthy of worship—or do you merely repeat the words without this logical precision? How can you make this logical understanding penetrate your heart daily?
?
Sanusi shows that if 'ilah' meant any object of worship, the testimony would be false. Have you ever considered that your own actions might 'worship' things other than Allah (e.g., status, wealth, desires) in a way that contradicts the very meaning of the shahada? What specific step will you take today to ensure your worship is directed solely to the true deity by right?
?
The soundness of your faith depends on a precise definition of a single term ('ilah'). Are you willing to examine your understanding of Allah's attributes and rights with the same rigorous logic that Sanusi applies here, even if it challenges comfortable assumptions? What is one area of your belief where you need to clarify 'by right' versus 'by habit'?

The Four Logical Combinations of Exception
Sanusi enumerates the four possible logical cases for exception and identifies the only valid one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four logical combinations of exception in the testimony of faith and why only one specific combination is valid.

Core Idea
The logic of 'La ilaha illa Allah' hinges on a universal-particular exception.

Sanusi now applies logical analysis to the central Islamic testimony. He asks: what are the possible relationships between the term from which exception is made (المستثنى منه) and the excepted term (المستثنى)? By enumerating and eliminating invalid cases, he isolates the only rationally sound interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the classification. Notice that Sanusi states the conclusion first, then lists the cases.
The passage is concise; take your time to understand the four cases.
2
Identify the four cases
List the four possible combinations of universal (كلي) and particular (جزئي) for the two terms (المستثنى منه and المستثنى). Match them with Sanusi's enumeration.
The first three are completely invalid; the fourth is subdivided.
3
Understand why three are invalid
Reflect on why both particular, both universal, or first particular then universal are invalid. Consider how they fail to properly exclude divinity from false gods.
The commentary will provide more explanation, but try to reason on your own first.
4
Focus on the fourth case
The fourth case (universal then particular) is the only potentially valid one. Note that it is further split into two parts, with only one part being correct. This invites further analysis in subsequent lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصلُ : أنَّ المعاني المقدَّرةَ عقلاً في باعتبار المستثنى منه والمستثنى أربعة : ثلاثة منها باطلة ، والرابع ينقسم قسمين ، أحد قسميه باطل ، والآخرُ هو الذي يصحُ مِنَ الأقسام كلّها . فالثلاثةُ الباطلة : أن يكونا جزئيين ، أو كليين ، أوِ الأَوَّلُ جزئيّاً والثاني كلياً ، والرابع عكس الثالث ؛ وهو أن يكونَ الأوَّلُ كلياً والثاني جزئيّاً .
The conclusion: The meanings rationally estimated in consideration of the thing excepted from and the excepted thing are four: three of them are invalid, and the fourth is divided into two parts, one of its two parts is invalid, and the other is what is valid from all the divisions. So the three invalid ones: that they both be particular, or both universal, or the first particular and the second universal. And the fourth is the opposite of the third: that the first be universal and the second particular.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a logical classification of the possible relationships between the term from which exception is made (المستثنى منه) and the excepted term (المستثنى) in the testimony of faith "لا إله إلا الله". The author first states that there are four possible cases, then declares three entirely invalid and the fourth further subdivided into an invalid part and a valid part. He then explicitly lists the three invalid cases: (1) both terms are particular (جزئيان), (2) both are universal (كليان), or (3) the first is particular and the second is universal. The fourth case is the reverse of the third: the first term is universal and the second is particular. The reader should note that the valid outcome (to be explained in subsequent lessons) falls within this fourth case, but not all of it is valid—only the specific interpretation where the universal term carries the correct meaning.

Key Terms
المستثنى منه
المستثنى
جزئي
كلي
استثناء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that any universal-particular combination is valid.
Correction: The author specifically limits validity to the fourth case, and within it only one interpretation.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, how many of the four logical combinations for the exception in 'لا إله إلا الله' are completely invalid?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
The only valid logical structure for exception in 'La ilaha illa Allah' is when the excepted-from term is universal and the excepted term is particular, but only under a specific interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'La ilaha illa Allah,' do you truly negate every false god from your heart, or do you still allow some attachment to worldly idols? How does this logical structure reveal the comprehensiveness of tawheed? What must you reject today to make your shahada valid?
?
The invalid cases show that if we limit the negation to particular things, we end up with shirk. Are there any 'particular' things in your life that you have not negated from being objects of worship? What would it look like to apply this universal negation to your daily concerns?
?
If someone says 'there is no god but Allah' but still fears creatures or loves them as only Allah should be loved, have they truly understood the universal negation? Examine your own heart: is your love, fear, and hope directed exclusively to Allah, or do you need to realign your devotion?

Logical Proof for the Five Essential Attributes
The commentator explains why the negation of any of the five attributes implies createdness and need.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the negation of any of the five essential attributes leads to a contradiction with God's absolute self-sufficiency, thereby proving their necessity.

Core Idea
Denying any essential attribute makes God dependent on something else.

The commentator unpacks a dense line from the original creed to show why each of the five attributes is necessary. The proof hinges on the fact that if a single attribute were missing, God would be a created being requiring a creator or a substratum — both of which contradict His absolute self-sufficiency.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentary passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Try to grasp the commentator's argument in one go.
Pay attention to the distinction between 'need of a creator' and 'need of a substratum'.
2
Identify the five attributes
List the five attributes explicitly mentioned: existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, being unlike created things, and the first part of self-subsistence (freedom from a particularizer). Note that the second part of self-subsistence (freedom from a substratum) is also proven.
These are the essential attributes that cannot be negated.
3
Understand the two separate proofs
See how the original text's phrase 'he would be in need of a creator or a substratum' splits into two proofs. The first proof covers the first four attributes plus freedom from particularizer; the second proof covers freedom from substratum.
This shows the proof is not redundant but addresses two distinct kinds of dependence.

His saying: ('for if these attributes were not necessary for Him, Exalted...') to its end: he made clear with this speech the aspect of the entailment of His, Exalted, self-sufficiency for these attributes; and that entails the establishment of need; meaning: to the Creator or to a substratum if any one of those attributes were absent.
As for existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, being unlike created things, and one of the two parts of the meaning of self-subsistence—which is being free of need of a particularizer—it is not hidden from you, after you have reached this point, that the negation of each one of these five attributes entails createdness, and you have known from what precedes that every created thing is in need of a creator other than itself, and exalted above that is He for whom absolute self-sufficiency from everything other than Him is necessary.
So our saying in the original creed: ('he would be in need of a creator') is a proof for the necessity of these five attributes for Him, Exalted.
And our saying: ('or a substratum') is a proof for the necessity of the second part of the meaning of self-subsistence; which is being free of need of a substratum.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage provides the logical proof for why the five essential attributes (existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, being unlike created things, and the first part of self-subsistence—freedom from a particularizer) are necessary for God. The commentator explains the original text's statement: 'for if these attributes were not necessary, He would be in need of a creator or a substratum.' The proof is that negating any of these five attributes entails that God would be a created entity (hadith), and every created entity requires a creator (muhdith) distinct from itself. This contradicts God's absolute self-sufficiency. The phrase 'or a substratum' (aw al-mahall) specifically addresses the second part of self-subsistence (qiyam bi-nafsihi), which is independence from a substratum in which accidents inhere. If God were not self-subsistent, He would need a locus (mahall) for His existence, which is also a form of need incompatible with absolute self-sufficiency. Thus, the proof establishes both parts of self-subsistence: (1) independence from a particularizer (mukhassis) and (2) independence from a substratum (mahall).

الاستغناء عن المحل

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is circular or redundant because it only shows that God would need a creator if He were created, which is obvious.
Correction: The commentator explains that the original statement contains two distinct proofs: one for the first four attributes plus freedom from particularizer, and another for freedom from substratum. Both are necessary because they address different types of dependence.

Key Takeaway
The five essential attributes are proven by showing that their absence would entail God's dependence on a creator or a substratum, which is impossible given His absolute self-sufficiency.

How would your life change if you internalize that only God is truly self-sufficient?
?

?

Do you worship Him out of a sense of need for His approval, or because He alone is worthy regardless? Let this lesson push you to examine the purity of your intention.

The Proof for the Five Essential Attributes and God's Transcendence
Commentator explains the rational proof for the necessity of five attributes and transcendence from divine independence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the principle of God's absolute independence (al-istighnā') serves as the rational proof for the necessity of five essential attributes and for His transcendence from all deficiencies, including the attributes of hearing, sight, and speech.

Core Idea
God's perfect independence proves He must have five essential attributes and be free from all defects.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks a key argument from the matn: the rational proof for the essential attributes is grounded in the principle that God is absolutely independent. He shows how negating any of these attributes would imply need, which contradicts independence. This reasoning also establishes God's transcendence from all deficiencies, including the need for hearing, sight, and speech as positive attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic text, noting its logical structure. Then read the full English translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the five attributes and the proof
Locate the list of five attributes: existence, eternity, everlastingness, dissimilarity, and one part of self-subsistence (independence from a specifier). Understand how negating each leads to originatedness (ḥudūth) and thus a need for an originator.
3
Follow the three parts of the proof
The commentator breaks the proof into three statements: need for an originator (proves the five attributes), need for a locus (proves the second part of self-subsistence), and need for a repeller of deficiencies (proves transcendence and attributes like hearing, sight, speech).
4
Understand the inclusion of hearing, sight, and speech
See how the third part, 'one who repels deficiencies,' implies that God must have hearing, sight, and speech, as their absence would be a deficiency. The footnote clarifies a subtle wording issue.

These three attributes belong to Him, may He be exalted (1); because you have known from what preceded that the rational proof for establishing them is that their opposites are deficiencies, and our Master, glorious and mighty, is transcendent above deficiencies by consensus of the rational people. His statement: (For if these attributes were not necessary for Him, may He be exalted ... etc.) to its end: He explained by this statement the aspect of the implication of His independence, may He be exalted, by these attributes; and that necessitates the establishment of need; meaning: to the originator or the locus if any one of those attributes were negated. As for existence, eternity, everlastingness, dissimilarity to originated things, and one part of the meaning of self-subsistence (al-qiyām bi al-nafs)—which is independence from a specifier (al-istighnā' 'an al-mukhassiṣ) — it is not hidden from you, after you have reached this point, that the negation of each one of these five attributes entails originatedness (al-ḥudūth), and you have known from what preceded that every originated thing is dependent on an originator other than it, and He is exalted above that for whom absolute independence from everything other than Him is necessary. So our statement in the original creed: (He would be in need of an originator) is a proof for the necessity of these five attributes for Him, may He be exalted. And our statement: (or the locus) is a proof for the necessity of the second part of the meaning of self-subsistence, which is independence from a locus (al-istighnā' 'an al-maḥall). (1) He used the word 'al-ʿināya' because this interpretation is not immediately apparent from the author; for what is apparent from his statement: (and included in that) is that the reference is to transcendence, not to its necessity, but the inclusion is in its necessity. (Dasūqī, p. 246). And our statement: (or one who repels deficiencies from Him) is a proof for the necessity of transcendence from deficiencies, which includes the necessity of hearing, sight, and speech for Him, may He be exalted (1). (1) By this he has established eleven creeds; by adding the meanings of the three mentioned attributes, which are that He, may He be exalted, is hearing, seeing, speaking.

Commentary
This is an extended commentary on a proof for the necessity of divine attributes. It begins by identifying three attributes (likely the ones just mentioned in the matn) and states that the rational proof for them is that their opposites are deficiencies, and God is declared free from deficiencies by consensus of rational people. The commentator then explains the author's statement: 'If these attributes were not necessary for Him...' showing that the aspect of implication is God's independence—negation of any attribute would entail need for an originator or a locus. The passage then specifies five attributes: existence, eternity, everlastingness, dissimilarity to originated things, and one part of self-subsistence (independence from a specifier). Negating each entails originatedness (ḥudūth), and every originated thing needs an originator—so proving God's absolute independence proves these attributes are necessary. The commentary then breaks down three statements from the original creed: the first ('He would need an originator') is proof for the five attributes; the second ('or the locus') is proof for the second part of self-subsistence (independence from a locus); the third ('or one who repels deficiencies') is proof for transcendence from deficiencies, which includes the attributes of hearing, sight, and speech. A footnote clarifies that the word 'ʿināya' indicates the interpretation is not immediate; the apparent meaning of 'included in that' refers to transcendence, but the inclusion is in the necessity. The second footnote notes that this entire section establishes eleven creeds, including the three attributes of hearing, sight, and speech.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes what it proves (circular reasoning).
Correction: The proof does not assume the necessity of attributes; it starts from premises that deficiencies are impossible for God and that every originated thing needs an originator, then shows that negating an attribute would entail a deficiency or originatedness, which contradicts God's independence.

Key Takeaway
God's absolute independence rationally proves the necessity of five essential attributes and His transcendence from all defects, including the positive attributes of hearing, sight, and speech.

Proof that All Things Besides God are Temporally Originated
Sanusi proves temporal origination of all things besides God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof (burhān) that everything other than God must have originated at some point in time (ḥudūth).

Core Idea
If anything were eternal, it would be independent of God — but everything else depends entirely on Him.

This passage forms the backbone of the kalām cosmological argument. Sanusi recalls a previously proven premise: what is eternal cannot cease to exist. He then applies this to the world: if any part of it were eternal, it would be self-sufficient and need no creator. But everything besides God is radically dependent on Him. The only conclusion is that all contingent things are temporally originated.

Guided Path
1
Restate the premise
Read the opening line carefully: 'You have already known by proof that what is established as eternal, its non-existence is impossible.' This is the foundation.
The premise is that the eternal (qadīm) cannot cease to be.
2
Apply the conditional
Follow the conditional statement: if something from the world were eternal, it would be necessarily existent (wājib al-wujūd) and not accept non-existence at all.
This directly follows from the definition of eternity.
3
Notice the implication of independence
If it does not accept non-existence, it would not need a specific cause (mukhaṣṣiṣ). Contrast this with the universal need of everything else for God.
The need for a cause is tied to possibility of non-existence.
4
Observe the conclusion
The author concludes that temporal origination (ḥudūth) is necessary for everything besides God.
Since the premise leads to contradiction if something were eternal, it must be originated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد عرفت بالبرهان فيما سبقَ أنَّ ما ثبت قدمه استحال عدمه، فلو كانَ شيءٌ مِنَ العالم قديماً لكانَ ذلك الشيء واجب الوجودِ، لا يقبلُ العدم أصلاً، لا سابقاً ولا لاحقاً، وإذا كانَ لا يقبلُ العدم لم يفتقرُ إلى مخصص، كيف وكلُّ ما سواه تعالى مفتقر إليه غاية الافتقار ابتداءً ودواماً ؟! فوجبَ إذاً الحدوثُ لكلِّ ما سواه جلَّ وعزَّ . وَيُؤْخَذُ مِنْهُ أَيْضاً : أَنْ لا تَأْثِيرَ لِشَيْءٍ مِنَ الْكَائِنَاتِ فِي أَثَرِ ما، وَإِلا لَزِمَ أَنْ يَسْتَغْنِيَ ذَلِكَ الْأَثَرُ عَنْ مَوْلانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ، كَيْفَ وَهُوَ تَعَالَى الَّذِي يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَيْهِ كُلُّ مَا سِوَاهُ عُمُوماً وَعَلَى كُلِّ حَالٍ ؟! هَذَا إِنْ قَدَّرْتَ أَنَّ شَيْئاً مِنَ الْكَائِنَاتِ يُؤَثرُ بِطَبْعِهِ وَأَمَّا إِنْ قَدَّرْتَهُ مُؤَثّراً بِقُوَّةٍ جَعَلَهَا اللَّهُ تَعَالَى فِيهِ كَمَا يَزْعُمُهُ كَثِيرٌ مِنَ الْجَهَلَةِ .. فَذَلِكَ مُحَالُ أَيْضاً؛ لِأَنَّهُ يَصِيرُ حِينَئِذٍ مَوْلانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ مُفْتَقِراً فِي إِيجَادِ بَعْضِ الْأَفْعَالِ إِلَى وَاسِطَةٍ، وَذَلِكَ بَاطِلٌ؛ لِمَا عَرَفْتَ قَبْلُ مِنْ وُجُوبِ اسْتِغْنَائِهِ جَلَّ وَعَزَّ عَنْ كُلِّ مَا سِوَاهُ. لا شكّ أنَّهُ لو خرج عن قدرتِهِ تعالى ممكن ما .. لم يكن ذلكَ الممكنُ مفتقراً إليهِ تعالى، بل إنَّما يفتقر إلى مَنْ أوجدَهُ، كيف وكلُّ ما سواه مفتقر إليهِ تعالى غاية الافتقار ؟!
You have already known by proof previously that what is established as eternal its non-existence is impossible. So if something from the world were eternal, that thing would be necessarily existent (wajib al-wujud), not accepting non-existence at all, neither prior nor subsequent. And if it does not accept non-existence, it would not need a specific cause (mukhaṣṣiṣ). How, when everything besides Him—exalted is He—is in utmost need of Him, initially and perpetually?! So temporal origination (al-ḥudūth) is therefore necessary for everything besides Him—glory and majesty be to Him. And from this it is also derived: that nothing of the contingent beings has any influence (taʼthīr) on any effect, otherwise that effect would necessarily become independent of our Patron—glory and majesty be to Him. How, while He—exalted is He—is the One to Whom everything besides Him is in need universally and in every state?! This is if you suppose that something of the contingent beings influences by its nature. But if you suppose it to be an influencer by a power that God—exalted is He—placed in it, as many of the ignorant claim, then that is also impossible; because then our Patron—glory and majesty be to Him—would become in need of an intermediary in bringing about some actions, and that is invalid, due to what you have previously known of the necessity of His—glory and majesty be to Him—being independent of everything besides Him. There is no doubt that if any possible thing (mumkin) were outside His power—exalted is He—then that possible thing would not be in need of Him—exalted is He—but rather it would be in need of the one who brought it into existence. How, while everything besides Him is in utmost need of Him—exalted is He?!
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is a continuous proof and refutation. It begins by restating a previously proven principle: if something is eternal, its non-existence is impossible; thus it would be necessarily existent (wajib al-wujud). The author then applies this to the world: if any part of the world were eternal, it would be necessary and independent of a creator (mukhaṣṣiṣ). But everything besides God is dependent on Him, so everything else must be originated (ḥādith). From this conclusion, the author derives a further corollary: nothing in creation has any independent causal influence (taʼthir) on any effect, because if it did, that effect would be independent of God, contradicting universal dependence. The author then considers two scenarios for claimed causal influence. The first is that a created thing influences by its own nature (bi-ṭabʻihi)—this is rejected as leading to independence from God. The second is that it influences by a power God placed in it, as many ignorant people claim. This is also impossible because it would make God need an intermediary to produce some actions, which violates the necessary self-sufficiency of God (istighnāʼ). Finally, the author adds that if any possible thing (mumkin) were outside God's power, it would depend on its own cause rather than on God, which again contradicts universal dependence. Thus the entire argument reinforces that God alone is the true agent and cause of all things.

Key Terms
حدوث (ḥudūth)
قدم (qidam)
واجب الوجود (wājib al-wujūd)
مخصص (mukhaṣṣiṣ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people mistakenly think that 'eternal' (qadīm) simply means 'very old' or 'long-lasting', without implying necessary existence or independence from a creator.
Correction: In kalām, qadīm means that which has no beginning and cannot cease to exist; it is necessarily existent and independent of any cause, as the passage demonstrates.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if something from the world were eternal, what would it not need?
A) A specific cause (mukhaṣṣiṣ)
B) Existence at all
C) Perfection
D) Power

Key Takeaway
Everything besides God must be temporally originated because only God can be eternal and self-sufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If everything besides God is in utmost need of Him at every moment, what does that mean for your own sense of self-sufficiency? Where in your life do you act as if you are independent of God?
?
The proof shows that anything eternal would not need a cause. Do you live as if you are eternal and self-sufficient? What would change if you truly internalized that you are temporally originated and dependent on God?
?
Sanusi states 'you have already known by proof'—but do you actually know it in your heart? What would it take for this truth to move from intellectual acceptance to a living conviction that shapes your actions?

Denial of Independent Causal Efficacy in Creation
Sanusi denies independent causal efficacy in creation and refutes two conceptions of created causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why no created thing can have independent causal power over its effects, and why even divinely bestowed causal power is impossible.

Core Idea
If anything besides God had real causal power, it would break the chain of absolute neediness to Him.

Having established that all things besides God are originated, Sanusi draws a further implication: nothing in creation can truly cause anything independently. He considers and refutes two possible conceptions of created causality — one that gives things natural power, and another that claims they act by a power God placed in them. Both lead to the same absurdity: making God dependent on intermediaries, which contradicts His necessary self-sufficiency.

Guided Path
1
Derive the principle
Read the first line: 'And from this it is also derived: that nothing of the contingent beings has any influence (taʼthīr) on any effect.' The principle comes from the ḥudūth proof.
If everything is dependent on God, then nothing can independently cause an effect.
2
First scenario: natural influence
Consider the first hypothetical: if a created thing influences by its own nature (bi-ṭabʻihi), the effect would become independent of God. Note the rhetorical 'How?' that underscores absurdity.
Natural causality would imply the effect doesn't need God, contradicting universal dependence.
3
Second scenario: divinely bestowed power
Now the second hypothetical: if a thing influences by a power God placed in it, as many ignorantly claim, God would then need an intermediary for some actions, which violates His necessary self-sufficiency (istighnāʼ).
Even a delegated power would make God dependent on an intermediary, which is impossible.
4
Final argument: possibility outside God's power
Read the concluding sentences: if any possible thing were outside God's power, it would not need God but would need its own creator. This reinforces that all possible things are under God's sole power.
This closes any loophole: nothing can be independent of God's power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد عرفت بالبرهان فيما سبقَ أنَّ ما ثبت قدمه استحال عدمه، فلو كانَ شيءٌ مِنَ العالم قديماً لكانَ ذلك الشيء واجب الوجودِ، لا يقبلُ العدم أصلاً، لا سابقاً ولا لاحقاً، وإذا كانَ لا يقبلُ العدم لم يفتقرُ إلى مخصص، كيف وكلُّ ما سواه تعالى مفتقر إليه غاية الافتقار ابتداءً ودواماً ؟! فوجبَ إذاً الحدوثُ لكلِّ ما سواه جلَّ وعزَّ . وَيُؤْخَذُ مِنْهُ أَيْضاً : أَنْ لا تَأْثِيرَ لِشَيْءٍ مِنَ الْكَائِنَاتِ فِي أَثَرِ ما، وَإِلا لَزِمَ أَنْ يَسْتَغْنِيَ ذَلِكَ الْأَثَرُ عَنْ مَوْلانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ، كَيْفَ وَهُوَ تَعَالَى الَّذِي يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَيْهِ كُلُّ مَا سِوَاهُ عُمُوماً وَعَلَى كُلِّ حَالٍ ؟! هَذَا إِنْ قَدَّرْتَ أَنَّ شَيْئاً مِنَ الْكَائِنَاتِ يُؤَثرُ بِطَبْعِهِ وَأَمَّا إِنْ قَدَّرْتَهُ مُؤَثّراً بِقُوَّةٍ جَعَلَهَا اللَّهُ تَعَالَى فِيهِ كَمَا يَزْعُمُهُ كَثِيرٌ مِنَ الْجَهَلَةِ .. فَذَلِكَ مُحَالُ أَيْضاً؛ لِأَنَّهُ يَصِيرُ حِينَئِذٍ مَوْلانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ مُفْتَقِراً فِي إِيجَادِ بَعْضِ الْأَفْعَالِ إِلَى وَاسِطَةٍ، وَذَلِكَ بَاطِلٌ؛ لِمَا عَرَفْتَ قَبْلُ مِنْ وُجُوبِ اسْتِغْنَائِهِ جَلَّ وَعَزَّ عَنْ كُلِّ مَا سِوَاهُ. لا شكّ أنَّهُ لو خرج عن قدرتِهِ تعالى ممكن ما .. لم يكن ذلكَ الممكنُ مفتقراً إليهِ تعالى، بل إنَّما يفتقر إلى مَنْ أوجدَهُ، كيف وكلُّ ما سواه مفتقر إليهِ تعالى غاية الافتقار ؟!
You have already known by proof previously that what is established as eternal its non-existence is impossible. So if something from the world were eternal, that thing would be necessarily existent (wajib al-wujud), not accepting non-existence at all, neither prior nor subsequent. And if it does not accept non-existence, it would not need a specific cause (mukhaṣṣiṣ). How, when everything besides Him—exalted is He—is in utmost need of Him, initially and perpetually?! So temporal origination (al-ḥudūth) is therefore necessary for everything besides Him—glory and majesty be to Him. And from this it is also derived: that nothing of the contingent beings has any influence (taʼthīr) on any effect, otherwise that effect would necessarily become independent of our Patron—glory and majesty be to Him. How, while He—exalted is He—is the One to Whom everything besides Him is in need universally and in every state?! This is if you suppose that something of the contingent beings influences by its nature. But if you suppose it to be an influencer by a power that God—exalted is He—placed in it, as many of the ignorant claim, then that is also impossible; because then our Patron—glory and majesty be to Him—would become in need of an intermediary in bringing about some actions, and that is invalid, due to what you have previously known of the necessity of His—glory and majesty be to Him—being independent of everything besides Him. There is no doubt that if any possible thing (mumkin) were outside His power—exalted is He—then that possible thing would not be in need of Him—exalted is He—but rather it would be in need of the one who brought it into existence. How, while everything besides Him is in utmost need of Him—exalted is He?!
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is a continuous proof and refutation. It begins by restating a previously proven principle: if something is eternal, its non-existence is impossible; thus it would be necessarily existent (wajib al-wujud). The author then applies this to the world: if any part of the world were eternal, it would be necessary and independent of a creator (mukhaṣṣiṣ). But everything besides God is dependent on Him, so everything else must be originated (ḥādith). From this conclusion, the author derives a further corollary: nothing in creation has any independent causal influence (taʼthir) on any effect, because if it did, that effect would be independent of God, contradicting universal dependence. The author then considers two scenarios for claimed causal influence. The first is that a created thing influences by its own nature (bi-ṭabʻihi)—this is rejected as leading to independence from God. The second is that it influences by a power God placed in it, as many ignorant people claim. This is also impossible because it would make God need an intermediary to produce some actions, which violates the necessary self-sufficiency of God (istighnāʼ). Finally, the author adds that if any possible thing (mumkin) were outside God's power, it would depend on its own cause rather than on God, which again contradicts universal dependence. Thus the entire argument reinforces that God alone is the true agent and cause of all things.

Key Terms
تأثير (taʼthīr)
طبع (ṭabʻ)
قوة (quwwa)
استغناء (istighnāʼ)
واسطة (wāsiṭa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that denying causal efficacy means that created things have no properties or actions at all, but the argument only denies independent, necessary causal power.
Correction: Created things can still be instrumental channels if God creates the effect directly alongside the action. The denial is of any real, independent causal influence that would make the effect independent of God.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would happen if a created thing influenced an effect by a power God placed in it?
A) It would be a valid form of causality.
B) God would become in need of an intermediary.
C) The effect would be independent of God.
D) It would prove that the thing is eternal.

Key Takeaway
No created being can have any real causal influence, because that would undermine the absolute and direct dependence of all things on God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve a success — a promotion, a healed relationship, a solved problem — does your heart immediately acknowledge that God alone is the Creator of that outcome, or do you find yourself attributing it to your own efforts, talents, or the help of others? Where does the teaching of this lesson expose a hidden shirk in your gratitude?
?
Think of a time you were anxious about a future result — an exam, a medical test, a financial need. Where was your focus: on the means (studying, doctors, savings) or on the Creator of those means? What does your anxiety reveal about your real belief in God's absolute sovereignty and the denial of independent causes?
?
If you truly internalized that no created being — not a doctor, a friend, a job, or even your own self — has any real power to bring about good or harm, what would change today in how you pray, how you plan, and how you face difficulty? Commit to one concrete action that reflects this tawḥīd in your daily life.
